People v. Liberta


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


100 A.D.2d 741; 473 N.Y.S.2d 636; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 17747



March 6, 1984 

 

802 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sutton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


99 A.D.2d 361; 473 N.Y.S.2d 203; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16965



March 5, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County, rendered September 20, 1982, modified, on the law, by vacating the convictions for criminal facilitation in the fourth degree and the sentences imposed thereon and the counts of grand larceny in the third degree are dismissed. As so modified, judgment affirmed and matter remitted to the Supreme Court, Suffolk County, for further proceedings pursuant to CPL 460.50 (subd 5).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court in Suffolk County (New York) convicted defendant of a scheme to defraud in the first degree, 10 counts of criminal facilitation in the fourth degree, and 11 counts of altering a mileage registering device. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: At the conclusion of the trial testimony, the trial court found that the People failed to prove the 15 counts of grand larceny alleged in the indictment and decided that these counts could not be presented to the jury. The court decided to submit 15 counts of criminal facilitation in the fourth degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 115.00 as lesser included offenses. The State and defense counsel consented to the trial court's decision. The People alleged that the grand larceny charges were premised on allegations of acting in concert or accessorial liability, that the counts of criminal facilitation were properly submitted to the jury as lesser included offenses, and that defendant waived any objections to submitting the criminal facilitation counts to the jury. The court held that the trial court committed reversible error in submitting the counts of criminal facilitation to the jury. Criminal facilitation was not a lesser included offense to grand larceny. The trial court lacked jurisdiction to submit the criminal facilitation counts to the jury. Defendant was powerless to waive the jurisdictional requirements.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment of the trial court by vacating defendant's convictions and sentences imposed for criminal facilitation in the fourth degree and by dismissing the counts for grand larceny in the third degree. As modified, the court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentences. The court remitted to the trial court for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Presentment to Jury of Crime Not Formally Charged -- Waiver

1. The submission to a jury of an alleged crime which is neither expressed in the accusatory instrument nor a lesser included offense to any charge in the accusatory instrument goes to the subject matter jurisdiction of the court, and objection thereto may not be waived by the defendant; moreover, the manner or form of a defendant's purported waiver is irrelevant and the defendant is [***2] powerless to confer on a court the authority to prosecute him for a crime without an appropriate accusatory instrument. Accordingly, where defendant was convicted in a superior court of 10 counts of criminal facilitation in the fourth degree, a misdemeanor which is not a lesser included offense to any charges he was formally accused of, without ever having been indicted for criminal facilitation or formally charged with those crimes by superior court information or otherwise, the court was without jurisdiction to consider criminal facilitation despite the fact that defendant consented to its presentment to the jury.

Crimes -- Lesser Included Offense -- Criminal Facilitation

2. A crime is not a lesser included offense unless in all circumstances, not only in those presented in the particular case, it is impossible to commit the greater crime without concomitantly, by the same conduct, committing the lesser offense. Accordingly, counts of criminal facilitation in the fourth degree were improperly submitted to the jury as lesser included offenses of charges against defendant of grand larceny in the third degree despite the fact that the latter charges may have been premised [***3] on allegations of acting in concert, or accessorial liability; the distinction between principal and accessory has no bearing on the definition of the crimes of grand larceny charged in the indictments; moreover, it is possible to be an accessory to a crime without being a facilitator.

 

803 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Kendall v. Evans


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


100 A.D.2d 508; 472 N.Y.S.2d 716; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 17478



March 5, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city court judge (judge) appealed an order of the Supreme Court for Westchester County (New York), which granted summary judgment to defendants, Administrative Judge and Chief Administrator of the Courts of the State of New York and Administrative Board of the Judicial Conference of the State of New York, in the judge's action for a declaration that he was entitled to the same pay as other city court judges.


OVERVIEW: The judge was paid a salary lower than that of city court judges in certain other cities. The judge brought an action against defendants for a declaration that N.Y. Jud. Law § 39 was unconstitutional insofar as it promulgated unfavorable salary differentials among city court judges. The judge also sought a retroactive money award. Defendants brought a summary judgment motion, alleging that because the judge retired after bringing the action, the issue of prospective relief was moot and the issue of retroactive relief should be before the claims court. The trial court granted the motion, and the judge appealed. The court reversed, holding that the judge's claim for retroactive pay was ancillary to, and dependent on, a favorable judgment on the issue of § 39's constitutionality. While claims against the state seeking money damages were normally brought in the claims court, a declaratory judgment action in the trial court was an appropriate vehicle for challenging the constitutionality of § 39.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order granting summary judgment to defendants in the judge's action against defendants seeking a declaration that he was entitled to the same pay as other city court judges.

 

804 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Garcia


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


99 A.D.2d 738; 472 N.Y.S.2d 626; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 17102



February 28, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed their convictions by the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which convicted defendants, after a bench trial, of criminal sale of a controlled substance, criminal possession of a controlled substance, and criminal use of drug and sentenced them to concurrent terms.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the sentences imposed upon defendants of concurrent indeterminate terms of five years to life imprisonment, upon their convictions of two class A-I felonies, were violative of N.Y. Penal Law § 70.00(3)(a)(cl)(i). The trial court's finding that the statutorily mandated indeterminate minimum sentences of 15 years were so grossly disproportionate to the crimes of which defendants were convicted so as to constitute cruel and unusual punishment is unsupported by the record and was error. Defendants were convicted of possessing and selling one pound of cocaine and apparently were "dealers". Because the quantity of the controlled substance found on defendants was substantially greater than the minimum required for a class A felony, there was no support for a finding that defendant's case was one of the rare cases on its particular facts that might justify finding that the applicable sentencing provision was unconstitutional as applied to defendants.


OUTCOME: The courtmodified the trial court judgment reversing the sentence of five years to life imposed in respect to the convictions of the sale of a controlled substance, and remanded for resentencing.

 

805 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Allstate Life Ins. Co. v. Superintendent of Ins.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


99 A.D.2d 278; 472 N.Y.S.2d 346; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16551



February 23, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Order, Supreme Court, New York County, entered on February 9, 1983, reversed, on the law, without costs and without disbursements, and a judgment entered declaring that the regulation challenged in this action (11 NYCRR 30.2 [a] [1], [3], [4]), is reasonably related to and supportive of a legitimate State interest, as expressed in section 213 of the Insurance Law, and is not violative of the plaintiff's constitutional rights.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state superintendent of insurance sought review of an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, New York County (New York) that converted a proceeding brought pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to a declaratory judgment action, denied its motion for summary judgment, and directed that a trial be held, in an action regarding N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 11, §§ 30.2(a)(1), (3), (4).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff insurer, as a foreign life insurance company doing business in the state, was subject to regulation by the superintendent pursuant to N.Y. Ins. Law art. 9-A. The insurer sought a declaratory ruling for permission to conduct a seven-day conference for its top production agents to be held in Mexico. The superintendent denied permission on the ground that the proposed conference would violate N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 11, §§ 30.2(a)(1), (3), (4). The trial court construed the action as seeking a declaration that the regulation was unconstitutional or inconsistent with N.Y. Ins. Law § 213, and it sua sponte converted the action to a declaratory judgment pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 103(c). On appeal, the court reversed. The court found that the insurer's plan did not qualify as a fundamental constitutional right and that the regulation did not proceed along suspect lines. The regulation was not subject to a finding of invidious discrimination but was reasonably related to a legitimate state interest. The court entered a judgment that the regulation was not violative of the insurer's constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court, which converted the insurer's action for a declaratory ruling to an action for declaratory judgment and ordered that a trial be held regarding a regulation for insurance conferences.


HEADNOTES

Appeal -- Appeal as of Right -- Order Denying Declaration in Favor of Regulation's Validity

1. In an action to vacate a determination of the Superintendent of Insurance that a conference proposed by petitioner would, if held, violate a rule of the Insurance Department, brought pursuant to CPLR article 78 and converted by court order to a declaratory judgment action, the petitioner's contention that said order, insofar as it [***2] directs a trial to resolve factual issues, is not appealable as of right because it is an order in an article 78 proceeding is without merit. To the extent it denied a declaration in favor of the regulation's validity, the order is appealable as of right. ( CPLR 5701, subd [a], par 2.)

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Regulation -- Regulation of Educational Conferences Conducted by Life Insurance Companies

2. 11 NYCRR 30.2, which prescribes time, cost and geographical limitations on educational conferences conducted by life insurance companies, does not deny petitioner life insurance company due process or equal protection under the Federal or State Constitutions on the ground that its cost limitation has a harsher impact on petitioner than on other life insurance companies by virtue of petitioner's allegedly unique and cost-efficient operation. Petitioner's plan to educate its agents for seven days in Acapulco, Mexico, at a cost of 31.6% of the first year commissions needed to qualify an agent for attendance does not qualify as a fundamental constitutional right; nor is the regulation's application of a firm 10% cost limitation on agency conference expenses to all [***3] the life insurance companies doing business in New York subject to a finding of invidious discrimination. The regulation, as a matter of law, is reasonably related to and supportive of a legitimate State interest in fostering economy in the operation of life insurance companies. Moreover, the limitations prescribed by the regulation are not to be construed as starting points for carving out "reasonable" exceptions and extensions.

 

806 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Best Way Beer & Soda Distributors, Inc. v. New York State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


99 A.D.2d 727; 472 N.Y.S.2d 350; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 17082



February 23, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner beer distributor sought review of a determination of the respondent New York State Liquor Authority that imposed penalties against the distributor's wholesale beer license based on a determination that the distributor increased the price of beer sold to retail licensees within 180 days after the last price decrease on such beer in violation of N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 55-b(2). The Authority imposed a suspension and bond claim.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, petitioner asserted that the statute violated the Sherman Antitrust Act in that it fostered and promoted restraint of trade and price fixing, thereby eliminating competition. The court found that the Supreme Court set forth two criteria for antitrust immunity. First, the challenged restraint must be one clearly articulated and affirmatively expressed as state policy; second, the policy must be actively supervised by the state itself. Here, the first criterion was concededly met and with regard to the second criteria, the court found that because the statute was administered by the State Liquor Authority, an agency of the State of New York, it was "compelled by direction of the state acting as a sovereign" and immune from the Sherman Act. However, the court ruled that the penalty imposed upon the distributor was so grave in its impact that it was disproportionate to the misconduct. The distributor co-operated fully in the investigation and the violation was unintentional. The improper pricing continued for only one week and there was no evidence that the violation would be continued or repeated in the future. Consequently, a fine was a more appropriate penalty.


OUTCOME: The court remanded for reconsideration by the for reconsideration of the penalty imposed, and otherwise confirmed the Authority's decision.

 

807 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Campion Funeral Home, Inc. v. Axelrod


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


99 A.D.2d 901; 472 N.Y.S.2d 501; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 17309



February 16, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner funeral home and others filed a petition pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Prac. L. & R. art. 78 to review a determination of respondent Commissioner of the Department of Health, which had sustained petitioners' guilt of certain violations of the Public Health Law.


OVERVIEW: After petitioners were found guilty of certain misconduct in the operation of a funeral home, they filed a petition challenging the findings of the Commissioner. The court found that the petitioners were not guilty of fraud and deceit, but had failed to provide an itemized list of the services they provided in violation of law. The court noted that N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 3450(1)(e) proscribed the practices of fraud, deceit, or misrepresentation in the business of a funeral establishment, but found that petitioners had merely substituted similar vaults by different manufacturers. Thus, they had not violated this provision. But N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 3440-a required funeral directors to provide, at the time funeral arrangements were made, a written statement showing the price of the funeral, including an itemized list of the services and merchandise furnished for such a price and a statement of the cash advances and expenditures advanced. Petitioners could only be penalized for this violation.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the Commissioner's determination required modification and remitted the matter for reconsideration of the appropriate penalty for petitioners.

 

808 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Luca v. D'Elia


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


99 A.D.2d 778; 471 N.Y.S.2d 875; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 17164



February 14, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother sought review of the order from respondent State Commissioner of Social Services (New York), which affirmed a decision of a county Department of Social Services to deny a grant of emergency assistance for needy families with children to the mother to buy a heating unit for the family home to replace one that had failed.


OVERVIEW: When the heating unit in a family's home broke, the mother applied to the county Department of Social Services for emergency assistance to needy families with children for a grant to obtain a new unit. The Department denied the request and the Commissioner affirmed the denial under N.Y. Comp. Code R. & Regs. tit. 18, § 372.4(b). The mother brought an action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 to review the Commissioner's determination. The court granted the mother's petition and annulled the Commissioner's affirmation. The court held that N.Y. Comp. Code R. & Regs. tit. 18, § 372.4(b) was invalid and void because it conflicted with N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 131-a. The breakdown of a home heating unit constituted destitution and was an emergency situation under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 350-j. It did not matter that the mother borrowed the money to buy a heating unit. But the court also held that a $ 500 limitation on such assistance did not violate equal protection because there was a rational basis for the limitation.


OUTCOME: The court granted the mother's petition and annulled the Commissioner's order affirming the county Department Of Social Services' denial of grant of emergency assistance to needy families with children to the mother to replace her broken heating unit. The court remanded the case to the county Department of Social Services, noting that a regulatory limitation of $ 500 on the grant was not unconstitutional.

 

809 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Celestial Food Corp. v. New York State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


99 A.D.2d 25; 471 N.Y.S.2d 654; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16518



February 6, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County, entered August 6, 1982, reversed, on the law, with costs, determination confirmed insofar as reviewed, and proceeding dismissed on the merits.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state liquor authority challenged the judgment from the Supreme Court at Special Term, Suffolk County (New York), which overturned its condition of requiring appellee liquor licensee to permanently seal access to an unlicensed game room as a condition to the issuance of an eating place beer license. The authority brought an action, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, and sought reinstatement of its determination.


OVERVIEW: The licensee argued that the authority's determination requiring it to permanently seal access to an unlicensed game room as a condition to the issuance of an eating place beer license was arbitrary and capricious. The trial court found the condition to be based on speculation or supposition of future problems and an unconstitutional denial of equal protection of the law. The trial court relied upon affidavits submitted by the licensee's attorney stating that other of the licensee's establishments with attached game rooms were issued licenses without the imposition of a sealing requirement. The court upheld the authority's determination because its imposition of the condition was not without sound basis in reason and without regard to the facts. The court disregarded the licensee's purported "proof" because it conflicted with the record and the trial court erred in considering it. Lastly, the court held that the licensee had no inherent right to engage in the business of selling intoxicating liquors.Accordingly, the court reversed the order and dismissed the proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, on the law, with costs, the order rendered by the trial court, which overturned the authority's imposition of a condition for the issuance of the licensee's eating place beer license, and dismissed the proceeding.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses

1. A determination of the State Liquor Authority requiring petitioner restaurant operator to permanently seal access to an unlicensed game room as a condition to the issuance of an eating place beer license is not arbitrary and capricious and should be upheld; the condition is entirely reasonable since the attractions featured in the game room will be patronized by many youths below the legal drinking age and the authority is merely undertaking to retain some measure of control.

Proceeding against Body or Officer -- Evidence

2. In a CPLR article 78 proceeding challenging a determination of an administrative agency,  [***2] Special Term improperly relied upon attorney's affidavits in reaching its conclusion that the petitioner was unconstitutionally denied equal protection of the law; judicial review of administrative action is limited to the facts and record adduced before the agency when the determination was made, and constitutional objections must be raised at the administrative level or they are not available to attack the determination in subsequent judicial proceedings.

 

810 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Daniel C.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


99 A.D.2d 35; 472 N.Y.S.2d 666; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16520



January 30, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Surrogate's Court, Westchester County, dated August 12, 1982, affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant natural mother filed an action in the Westchester County Surrogate's Court (New York) to revoke her consent, given pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 115-b, to the adoption of her son by respondent adoptive parents. The surrogate's court determined that the interests of the child were best served by adoption and denied the natural mother's request to revoke her consent. The mother appealed, alleging that § 115-b was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The mother signed a form drafted pursuant to § 115-b after obtaining advice of counsel consenting to the adoption of her son. The form indicated that the consent was irrevocable after 30-days from commencement of the adoption proceedings. Six days after the adoption proceedings began, the mother attempted to revoke her consent. The surrogate's court held a hearing pursuant to § 115-b to determine whether the best interests of the child would best be served by adoption or return of the child to the mother. During the revocation hearing the mother's counsel admitted that the mother was not misled by the consent form that revocation was subject to a hearing on the best interests of the child. On appeal, the court found that the mother's counsel's admission was conclusive on the mother. She had suffered no injury in fact or prejudice from the allegedly misleading language in the consent form and therefore had no standing to raise the issue of the constitutionality of the statute. The plain language of the statute did not require that the consent form contain the language that revocation was subject to a hearing to determine the best interests of the child.


OUTCOME: The judgment that the interests of the child were best served by adoption was affirmed. The mother lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of the statute at issue.


HEADNOTES

Adoption -- Consent -- Revocation

 Section 115-b of the Domestic Relations Law does not require that the extrajudicial consent form utilized in private placement adoptions inform the natural parent of the actual legal consequences of a timely application to revoke such consent where it is opposed by the adoptive parents, i.e., a hearing is held to determine the best interests of the child, at which hearing the natural parent derives no advantage from parenthood (Domestic Relations Law, § 115-b, subd 1, par [d]). Further, a natural mother who timely [***2] filed a revocation of such consent to the adoption of her child by petitioners lacks standing to challenge the constitutionality of the provisions of the statute relative to extrajudicial consents to private placement adoptions, where she admitted that the statute had been explained to her by her attorney when she signed the consent form and her attorney stated in her presence in open court that his client made no claim that she had been misled by the language of the form indicating that her consent would become irrevocable 30 days after the commencement of adoption proceedings unless written notice of revocation was given. Accordingly, following a hearing at which it was determined that the best interests of the infant would be served by his adoption by petitioners, the Surrogate properly denied the natural mother's application to withdraw her extrajudicial consent to the adoption.

 

811 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Birkeland v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


98 A.D.2d 395; 470 N.Y.S.2d 661; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16488



January 16, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, Queens County, dated October 18, 1982, affirmed insofar as appealed from, with one bill of costs payable to the respondents appearing separately and filing separate briefs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the president of an association of court officers and others, appealed from an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Queens County, (New York), which granted summary judgment to defendants, a state and others, and declared 1980 N.Y. Laws ch. 846, to be constitutional to the extent that it was not in violation of N.Y. Const. art. V, § 6.


OVERVIEW: The legislature determined that all nonjudicial officers and employees of the courts employed by a political subdivision of the state would become employees of the state under a newly unified court system pursuant to N.Y. Jud. Law § 39. Because a new classification plan was not adopted until three years later, virtually no testing for competitive positions occurred during that time, and many competitive positions were filled by provisionally appointed employees. 1980 N.Y. Laws ch. 846 excused such employees from testing if they were employed for one year. Plaintiffs argued that this enactment violated N.Y. Const. art. V, § 6, which required competitive examinations for civil service if practicable. The court affirmed the entry of summary judgment for defendants. Legislation carried a strong presumption of constitutionality. It was within the province of the legislature to determine that a serious disruption in court services would result from the immediate use of competitive examinations to fill the positions occupied by provisional employees and that the best solution would be to enact the "cover in" law at issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision granting summary judgment to defendants and declaring the statute to be constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Nonjudicial Personnel -- Provisional Employees

As a result of the delay in adopting a classification structure for all nonjudicial officers and employees who became new State employees under the new Unified Court System in 1977, virtually no testing for competitive positions occurred between 1976 and 1979, and chapter 846 of the Laws of 1980, a direct response to the problem created by the filling of many competitive positions with provisional employees, was enacted to provide that certain of those employees in the Tenth Judicial District would continue to serve as [***2] permanent employees; the enactment is not violative of section 6 of article V of the New York Constitution, which provides that civil service appointments shall be made, according to merit and fitness to be ascertained, as far as practicable, by examination which, as far as practicable, shall be competitive. It was within the province of the Legislature to determine that a serious disruption in court services would result from the immediate use of competitive examinations to fill the positions occupied by provisional employees, and that the best solution would be to enact the subject "cover in" law.

 

812 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Clearwater


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


98 A.D.2d 912; 470 N.Y.S.2d 934; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 21235



December 29, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the County Court of Chemung County (New York), which entered judgment convicting defendant of robbery in the third degree and sentenced him as a second felony offender, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 70.06, based upon a prior felony conviction for operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of alcohol, a violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192(5).


OVERVIEW: After defendant pled guilty to robbery in the third degree, the trial court entered judgment convicting him and sentenced him as a second felony offender. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that defendant's violation of § 1192(5) served as a predicate felony act, which N.Y. Penal Law § 70.06(1)(b) defined as a conviction in New York for a felony offense, and that he was a second felony offender as defined by § 70.06(1)(a) because he was convicted of a felony defined in N.Y. Penal Law. ch. 70 after he was subject to the predicate felony conviction. The court rejected defendant's argument that the imposition of a harsher sentence on a defendant who was first convicted of a felony outside of the New York Penal Law and later convicted of a felony as defined by the Law, but not where the convictions occurred in the reverse order, violated equal protection. The court explained that the classification was rational because the legislature could reasonably have concluded that the nature of the felonies defined in the Law warranted harsher sentences for those convicted of felonies defined in the Law who had also previously been convicted of any felony.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for robbery in the third degree and his sentence as a second felony offender.

 

813 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Canigiani v. Board of Assessors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


98 A.D.2d 233; 471 N.Y.S.2d 95; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20873



December 27, 1983 



DISPOSITION: In Proceeding No. 1, judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County, entered October 20, 1981, modified, on the law, by deleting the second decretal paragraph thereof, which declared chapter 126 of the Laws of 1979, insofar as it prohibits the use of State equalization rates promulgated by the State Board of Equalization and Assessment as a method of proving inequality, unconstitutional. As so modified, judgment affirmed insofar as appealed from, without costs or disbursements.

In Proceeding No. 2, judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County, entered October 20, 1981, modified, on the law, by deleting the second decretal paragraph thereof, which declared chapter 126 of the Laws of 1979, insofar as it prohibits the use of State equalization rates promulgated by the State Board of Equalization and Assessment as a method of proving inequality, unconstitutional. As so modified, judgment affirmed insofar as appealed from, without costs or disbursements.

In Proceeding No. 3, order of the Supreme Court, Nassau County, entered September 28, 1982, reversed, without costs or disbursements, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings consistent [***3] with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Board of Assessors sought review of three judgments of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Nassau County (New York), which entered judgment in favor of first petitioner and second petitioner, both of whom were real property owners. The special term allowed them to utilize the state equalization rates to reduce assessments. Third petitioner's motion for partial summary judgment was granted on the basis of collateral estoppel.


OVERVIEW: In September 1981, the special term reduced the tax assessments for first and second petitioners. In 1982, it estopped the Board from challenging the validity of the state rates for specific years, holding that third petitioner was entitled to use the state rates for those years. In October 1981, however, the 1979 amendment to N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 720(3) expired. The law as re-enacted in December 1981 contained a new N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 720(3), which again barred the introduction of the state rates in Nassau County and New York City as evidence of inequality in tax assessment review proceedings The primary issue to be resolved was whether it was proper to allow petitioners, who were challenging assessments on the ground of inequality, to establish a ratio of assessed valuation to fair market value by means of the state equalization rates. The court held that it was proper, but only in the first two judgments, which were affirmed. The court reversed and remitted the third proceeding, finding that the new statute was not to be applied retroactively, and that the third proceeding had not progressed to the appropriate stage before the new provision was enacted.


OUTCOME: The court modified the first two judgments by deleting paragraphs that had declared the statute unconstitutional insofar as it had prohibited the use of state equalization rates as a method of proving equality. The court affirmed as modified. In the third, the court reversed the grant of partial summary judgment that had allowed third petitioner the use of the ratio and the state rates. The court remitted the matter in the third proceeding.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Assessment -- Use of Equalization Rates -- Collateral Estoppel

1. Judgments in proceedings brought challenging real property tax assessments on the ground of inequality allowing petitioners to utilize State equalization rates as a method of proving ratio between assessed value and fair market value and reducing the assessment on petitioners' properties for the tax years 1971/1972 through 1980/1981 are modified by deleting declarations as to the unconstitutionality of former subdivision 3 of section 720 of the Real Property Tax Law, which precluded use of the State rates in such proceedings, since the statute expired during the pendency of the appeals and was not applicable to the case as it stood before the Appellate Division on appeal; new subdivision 3 of section 720 of the Real Property Tax Law, which became effective on December 3, 1981 and again prohibited the use of State rates in inequality review proceedings in Nassau County is also not applicable, since judgments utilizing the State rates were entered before the present statute was enacted, and the present subdivision 3 is, by its own terms, prospective in application and not [***4] applicable to cases in which a judgment had been entered prior to its enactment. The validity of State rates for tax years 1971/1972 through 1977/1978 may not be challenged since their validity has been previously adjudicated and reduced to interlocutory judgment, and the use of State rates for the tax years 1978/1979 through 1980/1981 was justified by the evidence.

Taxation -- Assessment -- Use of Equalization Rates -- Collateral Estoppel

2. A judgment in a proceeding brought challenging real property tax assessments on the ground of inequality granting petitioner partial summary judgment on the issue of ratio for the tax years 1965/1966 through 1980/1981 on the basis of collateral estoppel, and allowing him to utilize the State equalization rates for those years as proof of ratio is reversed; subdivision 3 of section 720 of the Real Property Tax Law, which prohibited the use of State rates in inequality review proceedings in Nassau County, has been held to be constitutional on its face and applicable to proceedings pending on December 3, 1981, the effective date of the legislation, where no evidentiary hearings had been held or interlocutory judgments made prior to that [***5] date, and since the proceeding had not progressed to either of these stages at the time of the effective date of the new subdivision 3, new hearings must be held pursuant to the provisions of the statute. The doctrine of collateral estoppel is applicable only where the use of the State rates is not otherwise barred by a valid and applicable statute, and cannot be used to negate an act of the Legislature which validly prohibits the use of the State rates in a particular proceeding.

 

814 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Warren v. Delaney


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


98 A.D.2d 799; 469 N.Y.S.2d 975; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 21126



December 27, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff judgment debtors sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), which granted defendant creditors' motion for summary judgment in an action by the judgment debtors, which claimed that their checking accounts were statutorily exempt from restraint.


OVERVIEW: The judgment debtors filed an action against the creditors, claiming that the funds in their checking accounts were statutorily exempt from restraint, execution, and levy. The complaint specifically alleged that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5222 and 5232 violated the New York Constitution by authorizing the restraint and levy of personal property without providing the judgment debtor with notice of or an adequate opportunity to raise the claim of exemptions to the restraint or levy. The trial court dismissed the complaint against all the creditors. On appeal, the court noted that after the trial court's judgment, N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5222 and 5232 were amended. However, the court held that the appeal should not be dismissed on mootness considerations. The judgment debtors' claim for compensatory and punitive damages saved the action from the bar of mootness. The court further found that special damages could be awarded to the judgment debtors for the wrongful deprivation of their property. Finally, the court held that while some of the claims were properly dismissed, the judgment debtors were entitled to a new trial on the issue of damages with regard to the remaining claims.


OUTCOME: The order awarding summary judgment to the creditors was affirmed in part, reversed in part, modified in part, and remanded for a new trial on the issue of damages with regard to some claims.

 

815 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Brogan v. Ford Motor Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


98 A.D.2d 21; 470 N.Y.S.2d 884; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20373



December 22, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Decisions affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer sought review of an order of respondent Workers' Compensation Board (New York) that determined that respondent claimant had sustained a 23 percent binaural schedule loss of hearing due to long-term exposure to noise injurious levels, and a subsequent order reaffirming the initial determination. On appeal, the employer challenged the board's adoption of regulations pertaining to occupational loss of hearing.


OVERVIEW: The employer argued that the board exceeded its authority when it adopted the regulations on hearing loss applied to the instant claim because it failed to hold a public hearing, as required by N.Y. Lab. Law § 29. While generally such a hearing was required under § 29 for labor law regulations, the court noted that N.Y. Work. Comp. Law § 49-gg governed the adoption of regulations in the area of workers' compensation. As such, the procedural requirements of § 49-gg were applicable to the board's hearing loss regulations. Moreover, § 49-gg did not require a public hearing. The court also rejected the employer's argument that the board abused its discretion by not making an allowance for prescbycusis, hearing loss due to aging, in the regulations. Because hearing loss occurred at hertz levels that were much higher than the loss set forth in the regulations, there was no need to take a deduction for prescbycusis. Finally, the court rejected the employer's constitutional challenge to the regulations because the board stayed within its authority in adopting the regulations, and that there was a reasonable relationship between the regulations and the purpose of the legislation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the board's order finding that the claimant had suffered a compensable hearing loss, as well as the subsequent order reaffirming the initial decision.


HEADNOTES

Workers' Compensation -- Occupational Loss of Hearing -- Adoption of Regulations Pertaining to Such Loss

1. Section 49-gg of the Workers' Compensation Law, which outlines the procedural steps the Workers' Compensation Board must take in adopting regulations, supersedes section 29 of the Labor Law which requires the holding of a public hearing before any rule is adopted, amended or repealed. Accordingly, since section 49-gg does not provide for a hearing, it is reasonable to assume that the Legislature did not intend one and, therefore, the board did not err in failing to hold hearings prior to adopting regulations pertaining to standards for determining when a worker has suffered an occupational [***2] loss of hearing.

Workers' Compensation -- Occupational Loss of Hearing -- Presbycusis Deduction

2. The Workers' Compensation Board did not abuse its discretion in failing to provide a deduction for presbycusis, hearing loss due to aging, in regulations it adopted pertaining to standards for determining when a worker has suffered an occupational loss of hearing; there is a rational basis for the board's determination, since hearing loss from repeated exposure to excessively loud noise usually occurs in the 4,000 hertz area thus obviating the need for such deduction if frequencies below the 4,000 hertz level were adopted. Moreover, section 49-gg of the Workers' Compensation Law does not mandate that a deduction for presbycusis be factored into the board's formula.

Workers' Compensation -- Occupational Loss of Hearing -- Adoption of Regulations by Special Committee -- Limitation of Size of Committee

3. The special committee which drafted regulations regarding occupational loss of hearing was not enlarged beyond the limit imposed by section 49-gg of the Workers' Compensation Law through consultation with additional experts; such consultation was consistent with [***3] the explicit statutory direction to "meet and confer with representatives of the workers' compensation board and with representatives of employers and employees"; moreover, the filed regulatory impact statement named only six members of the committee, a number well within the statutory limit. In addition, the regulations were supported by a majority of the committee.

Workers' Compensation -- Occupational Loss of Hearing -- Constitutionality of Regulations

4. The Workers' Compensation Board did not violate the New York State Constitution in adopting regulations pertaining to occupational loss of hearing on the grounds that it exceeded its delegated authority conferred by section 49-gg of the Workers' Compensation Law and that it adopted regulations contrary to the prevailing medical authority; the board stayed within its delegated authority in adopting the regulations and there is a reasonable relationship between the regulations and the intended purpose of the legislation.

Bamond v. Regan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


98 A.D.2d 896; 471 N.Y.S.2d 27; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 21214



December 22, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizen sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which dismissed his application against respondent state comptroller in a proceeding in which the citizen alleged that federal income tax was illegal and unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The citizen filed a petition that asserted that federal income tax was illegal and unconstitutional. The petition failed to recite or describe the relief sought other than to state, "I do hereby exercise my legal right to rescind and cancel and to render null and void for any and all purposes each and every one of all so-called income tax returns or Form 1040 which at any time past I might have ever caused to be signed with my name or sent to the Internal Revenue Service." The trial court dismissed the citizen's application. The citizen appealed. The court affirmed and held that the trial court was correct in dismissing the petition, which failed to state a cause of action or to set forth a request for relief. The court also held that those issues pertaining to denial of welfare and social security to the citizen's children and alleged defective road conditions, having been raised for the first time in the citizen's brief and not presented to the trial court, were not for consideration with the court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the citizen's petition against the state comptroller for failure to state a cause of action or to set forth a request for relief.
