Peconic Ave. Businessmen's Ass'n v. Brookhaven


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


98 A.D.2d 772; 469 N.Y.S.2d 483; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 21097



December 19, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff business association appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Suffolk County (New York), which declared Unif. Code of Traf. Ords. of the Town of Brookhaven, N.Y., art. X, § 60(T)(3) to be constitutional. The supreme court also denied plaintiff's application for a permanent injunction against the enforcement of the ordinance, which prohibited trucks over 5,000 pounds from using a specified portion of a road.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance prohibited trucks from using the shortest route between an industrial area and an expressway. Plaintiff, whose members owned businesses that relied upon the use of the prohibited portion of the road, alleged that the ordinance was not reasonably related to defendant town's police powers. Plaintiff also alleged that the extra expense involved in using the alternate route together with the resulting decrease in the value of industrial property constituted a taking without compensation. At trial, plaintiff's expert attributed importance to the road as a "connector" highway; he also testified that the new, alternate route to be taken as a result of the ordinance passed through residential areas, as well as a dangerous intersection. The town offered no evidence. The special term declared the ordinance to be constitutional, but the court reversed on appeal. The court found that the ordinance bore no real or substantial relationship to the public welfare. Accordingly, the court concluded that the ordinance encroached on the exercise of private property rights without substantial relation to a legitimate governmental purpose, and was therefore unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, on the law and the facts, the judgment. The court declared that the ordinance was unconstitutional, and granted plaintiff's application for a permanent injunction against the enforcement of the ordinance.

 

818 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Rochester v. Chiarella


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


98 A.D.2d 8; 470 N.Y.S.2d 181; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20371



December 16, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant protesters, a class of individuals who protested the constitutionality of a tax, challenged an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Monroe County (New York), which declared that Rochester, N.Y. Ordinance No. 83-162, authorizing refunds to nonprotesting taxpayers, was a constitutional exercise of the city's authority under N.Y. Gen. City Law § 20(5).


OVERVIEW: In their class action, the protesters obtained a ruling that nonprotesters had no legal right to recover their payments of an unconstitutionally levied real property tax. The city responded by enacting Ordinance No. 83-162, authorizing equitable repayment of the taxes to the nonprotesters. On remittitur, the supreme court ruled that the ordinance was constitutional, and the protesters appealed. The court affirmed. The constitutional prohibition against gifts and loans, N.Y. Const. art. VIII, § 1, was no impediment to the ordinance because N.Y. Gen. City Law § 20(5) permitted municipalities to pay equitable claims. When the only impediment to legal liability lay in a technical defect which barred an otherwise valid claim, a recognizable moral and equitable obligation arose on which the city could act. The ordinance did not violate § 20(5)'s prohibition against waiver of the defense of the statute of limitations because the nonprotesting taxpayers never had legal claims which could have been subject to a limitations period and any action on their part could have been treated as a plenary action for money had and received, in which the applicable limitations period was six years.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order declaring that a city ordinance authorizing refunds to taxpayers who did not protest the constitutionality of a tax was a constitutional exercise of the city's authority.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Refund of Real Property Tax to Nonprotesting Taxpayers -- Equitable Claims

1. An ordinance of the City of Rochester which authorizes refunds to all taxpayers who, without protest, paid unconstitutionally levied real property taxes is not invalid on the ground that the payment of refunds to nonprotesters would violate the constitutional prohibition against gifts by municipalities (NY Const, art VIII, § 1). When the only impediment to legal liability lies in a technical defect which bars an otherwise valid claim, a recognizable moral and equitable obligation arises on which the city may act; accordingly, since the subject ordinance does nothing more than recognize the claims of the nonprotesters as equitable claims and provide that any compromise with nonprotesters be limited [***2] to the amount available in the city's special fund for tax refunds, it is a proper exercise of the legislative function by the city council; nothing in the ordinance affects the legal claims of the protesters against the city which have been established earlier.

Taxation -- Refund of Real Property Tax to Nonprotesting Taxpayers -- Waiver of Statute of Limitations

2. An ordinance of the City of Rochester which authorizes the payment of refunds to taxpayers who, without protest, paid unconstitutionally levied real property taxes does not violate the prohibition of subdivision 5 of section 20 of the General City Law against waiver of the defense of the Statute of Limitations; the nonprotesting taxpayers never had legally enforceable claims which could be subjected to a Statute of Limitations defense. Moreover, a challenge to a tax statute alleged to be unconstitutional may be treated as a plenary action for money had and received for which the applicable limitations period is six years; inasmuch as the first illegal tax assessment was made on July 1, 1974, and the counterclaim of the nonprotesting taxpayers was interposed on June 26, 1980, the action would have been timely [***3] commenced.

 

819 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Friedsam v. State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


98 A.D.2d 26; 470 N.Y.S.2d 848; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20374



December 15, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent New York State Tax Commission sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York), which, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, annulled a determination of the Commission denying petitioner taxpayer's claim for a personal income tax deduction.


OVERVIEW: A Connecticut resident employed in New York claimed alimony payments made to his former wife as an adjustment to income on his nonresident tax return. The Commission disallowed the adjustment pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 632(b)(1)(B) and rejected the argument that its application violated the privileges and immunities clause of U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2, cl. 1. The trial court granted the taxpayer's application by annulling the determination of the Commission. On appeal, the Commission asserted that alimony payments were purely personal in nature, were not related to income producing activities in New York, and were nondeductible. The court found that there was no substantial reason for the disparate treatment between residents and nonresidents. The court noted that no governmental policy explaining the disparity in treatment accorded nonresidents existed, and there was no showing that the factor of residence had a legitimate connection with allowing the deduction. The court noted that under the Commission's interpretation, the only criterion was whether the payor was a resident or nonresident. The court concluded that without more, there resulted a constitutional violation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court annulling a determination of the Commission to deny the taxpayer's claim for a personal income tax deduction.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Personal Income Tax -- Alimony Deduction for Nonresident Taxpayer

The disallowance of a deduction for alimony payments to nonresident taxpayers while permitting such a deduction to resident taxpayers constitutes a violation of the privileges and immunities clause (US Const, art IV, § 2, cl 1); there is neither a State governmental policy expressing a reason for the disparity in treatment accorded nonresidents, nor any showing that the factor of residence has a legitimate concern with the allowance of the deduction. There is no substantial reason for disparate treatment between residents and nonresidents in allowing the alimony deduction; accordingly, a determination of the State Tax Commission which disallowed petitioner a deduction for the payment of alimony on his nonresident tax return solely because petitioner was a nonresident [***2] was properly annulled.

 

820 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Adoption of Joseph LL.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


97 A.D.2d 263; 470 N.Y.S.2d 784; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20354



December 8, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father challenged the order of the Family Court, Chenango County (New York), which approved the adoption of the father's biological child without the consent of the father and dismissed his petition for visitation.


OVERVIEW: After the father and the mother were divorced, the mother retained custody of the child. The father was convicted for arson in the third degree and sentenced to a term of 7 1/2 to 15 years in a state prison. The mother's new husband petitioned to adopt the child. The trial court approved the adoption without the father's consent. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that there was no constitutional infirmity in the provisions of N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111(2)(d) and N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 79(1), which combined to eliminate the need for the consent of a natural parent to the adoption of his or her child where the natural parent had received a sentence of imprisonment for any term less than for life. The statutory scheme constituted a valid exercise of the state's power to act in the child's best interest. There was clear and convincing evidence to support the finding that the best interest of the child would be served by permitting adoption by the mother's husband without the consent of the father. Additionally, the father received timely notice of the adoption proceeding and actively participated, with the assistance of counsel, in the hearing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order approving the adoption of the minor child and the dismissal of his petition for visitation.


HEADNOTES

Adoption -- Consent -- Consent of Parent Sentenced to Imprisonment Not Required

There is no constitutional infirmity in the provisions of section 111 (subd 2, par [d]) of the Domestic Relations Law and subdivision 1 of section 79 of the Civil Rights Law, which combine to eliminate the need for the consent of a natural parent to the adoption of his or her child where the natural parent has received a sentence of imprisonment in a State correctional facility for any term less than for life; there is no violation of the equal protection clause since the State's concerns in facilitating the adoption of young children and having the adoption proceeding completed expeditiously are legitimate State interests and the natural parent, through his own willful and wanton [***2] conduct in violating the Penal Law, has effectively removed himself from any active role in the daily supervision, education, protection and care of his child for a substantial period of time during the child's formative years, and eliminating the need for the consent to adoption under such circumstances serves the State's legitimate interest in providing the child with the stability of a normal two-parent home. Although the subject child was born in wedlock, the family relationship was fleeting at best and there is no substantive due process violation; the statutory scheme does not create an unconstitutional irrebuttable presumption of unfitness since the court is merely given the power to approve an adoption without the natural parent's consent and whether the court will exercise this power depends upon the best interests of the child.

 

821 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Shattenkirk v. Finnerty


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


97 A.D.2d 51; 471 N.Y.S.2d 149; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20329



December 1, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified, on the law, without costs, by vacating decretal paragraphs three through six and substituting therefor a declaration that Budget Bulletin D-1052 is not violative of any of the constitutional or statutory provisions raised by petitioners, and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, Budget Director of the State of New York, appealed a judgment in favor of petitioner state employees by the Supreme Court of Rensselaer County (New York), which declared Budget Bulletin D-1052 null and void and the "98 percent rule" set forth therein unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The state employees argued that Budget Bulletin D-1052 violated the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11 because it authorized the budget director to withhold an eight percent salary increase where their salaries were either 98 percent of their supervisors' salaries, or they were "exempt" employees. The trial court found the regulation to be arbitrary and capricious. The budget director appealed. The court modified the trial court's judgment to declare that Budget Bulletin D-1052 was not unconstitutional. The court concluded that sufficient standards were set forth such that the delegation of legislative authority to the budget director was in all respects constitutional. The court determined that salary compression was a valid state objective, which did not violate the policy of equal pay for equal work set forth in N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 115. The withholding of the salary increase for only the highest paid management or exempt employees was rational and not arbitrary and capricious. Thus, the budget director's withholding of salary increases did not violate the state employees rights to equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's judgment to declare that the budget regulation issued by the budget director was not violative of any of the constitutional or statutory provisions raised by the state employees.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Compensation and Benefits -- Withholding of Salary Increase from Certain Employees

1. Chapter 460 (§ 7, subd 10) of the Laws of 1982, which provides that, notwithstanding an 8% salary increase for positions designated management/confidential by the Civil Service Law, any increase may be withheld in whole or in part from any officer or employee when in the opinion of the Director of the Budget such increase is not warranted or is not appropriate, is not an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority; there is no constitutional [***2] prohibition against the delegation of power, with reasonable safeguards and standards, to an agency or commission to administer the law as enacted by the Legislature, and the words "warranted" and "appropriate" set forth a constitutionally permissible standard.

Civil Service -- Compensation and Benefits -- Withholding of Salary Increase from Certain Employees

2. The 98% rule set forth in a budget bulletin directing that certain upper-level management/confidential State employees receive a scheduled salary increase only to the extent that their salaries would not be greater than 98% of their supervisor's salary was not arbitrary and capricious, nor was it violative of the equal protection clauses of the Federal and State Constitutions; such salary compression, which has been implemented in previous years, is a legitimate State objective, and there was thus a legitimate State purpose behind the enactment of chapter 460 (§ 7, subd 10) of the Laws of 1982, pursuant to which the subject bulletin was implemented, providing that notwithstanding an 8% salary increase for management/confidential positions, any increase could be withheld when in the opinion of the Budget Director [***3] such increase is not warranted or is not appropriate. Although the 98% rule was applicable to only a small group of employees and others received the increase, the two groups were not similarly situated, and the statute authorizes the withholding of salary increases under the 98% rule from "any" officer or employee, which means "all" or "every" and imports no limitation; in matters involving the State budget, equal protection does not require that all classifications be made with mathematical precision, nor does the principle of equal pay for equal work mandate that such principle must be applied in all cases under any and all conditions.

Civil Service -- Compensation and Benefits -- Withholding of Salary Increase from Certain Employees

3. A budget bulletin directing that all "exempt" employees in positions designated as management/confidential and earning more than $ 23,065 annually would not receive any of a scheduled salary increase, was in accordance with chapter 460 (§ 7, subd 10) of the Laws of 1982, which provides that notwithstanding an 8% salary increase for management/confidential positions, any increase may be withheld in whole or in part from any officer or [***4] employee when in the opinion of the Director of the Budget, such increase is not warranted or is not appropriate; in promulgating the budget bulletin, the Budget Director did not abdicate any responsibility that the Legislature had delegated to him, and the withholding of salary increases from exempt employees did not violate their rights to equal protection. The Budget Director's decision to withhold the salary increases from upper-level exempt employees, as part of a series of stringent measures relative to the State's work force, had a rational basis directly related to a State objective, and such decision cannot be condemned merely because it did not produce the budgetary savings originally expected.

 

822 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Butler


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


96 A.D.2d 140; 468 N.Y.S.2d 274; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20310



November 4, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Monroe County Court (New York), which convicted him of driving while intoxicated as a felony under N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192(5).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for driving while intoxicated (DWI) and mistakenly charged with a misdemeanor. His counsel sent a note to the traffic court that said defendant would plead guilty to the offense. It was discovered that defendant should have been charged with a felony because of a prior DWI conviction in the last 10 years. Defendant pled guilty to the felony and was sentenced to probation. Defendant appealed the conviction, and the court affirmed. The court held that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192(5) did not create an unlawful bill of attainder because it enhanced a misdemeanor DWI to a felony based on a conviction for DWI within the previous 10 years. The potential pool of DWI misdemeanants was unlimited, and the statute did not impermissibly focus on an identifiable group but set forth a rule of general applicability. Both the initial misdemeanor and the subsequent felony conviction were predicated upon a judicial adjudication of guilt based on an objective legal standard.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for felony DWI.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Bill of Attainder -- Felony Status for Subsequent Convictions of Driving while Intoxicated

1. Subdivision 5 of section 1192 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law is not an unconstitutional bill of attainder on the ground that it enhances a misdemeanor driving while intoxicated (D.W.I.) penalty to a felony upon a second conviction within 10 years without judicial process, other than proof of the previous conviction, only to those individuals who are prior D.W.I. misdemeanants; the potential pool of D.W.I. misdemeanants and, therefore, potential D.W.I. recidivists, is unlimited; accordingly, the statute does not impermissibly focus on an identifiable group but, rather, sets forth a rule of general applicability; the judicial process involved is not insufficient to support a felony conviction since both the initial misdemeanor and the subsequent felony convictions are predicated upon a judicial adjudication of guilt based on an objective [***2] legal standard.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Felony Status for Subsequent Conviction of Driving while Intoxicated -- Sufficiency of Prior Misdemeanor Conviction

2. Defendant's felony conviction, upon a guilty plea, of driving while intoxicated resulting from a prior misdemeanor conviction for the same offense, is not unconstitutional on the ground that the procedural due process attending misdemeanor trials that result in conviction is insufficient to render a recidivist a felon; since defendant does not contend that he was unrepresented by counsel during his prior misdemeanor conviction, said conviction may constitutionally serve as a predicate for the subsequent felony conviction.

Crimes -- Former Jeopardy -- Guilty Plea to Misdemeanor by Mail

3. A letter from defense counsel, enclosing a signed traffic information and stating that defendant pleaded guilty to a misdemeanor charge of driving while intoxicated, sent to a Town Justice, is not equivalent to an accepted plea of guilty; a guilty plea to a misdemeanor can only be made in person or, with permission of the court, by counsel after filing of the defendant's written subscribed authorization;  [***3] accordingly, defendant's attempted plea by mail was a nullity and, therefore, jeopardy did not attach prior to the subsequent Grand Jury indictment of defendant for driving while intoxicated and defendant's guilty plea thereto, so as to render said indictment and plea violations of the double jeopardy clause of the Fifth Amendment of the United States Constitution.

 

823 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Elmwood-Utica Houses, Inc. v. Buffalo Sewer Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


96 A.D.2d 174; 468 N.Y.S.2d 227; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20314



November 4, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously modified, and as modified affirmed, without costs, and matter remitted to Supreme Court, Erie County, for further proceedings, in accordance with the opinion herein, and judgment entered declaring chapter 862 of the Laws of 1981 constitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff apartment building and defendant sewer authority each sought review of an order from the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which, in the building's action challenging the ad valorem portion of the authority's sewer rent charge, denied the building's request for a stay of any foreclosure action against the lien of the building's unpaid sewer rents, and denied the authority's motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The building received a notice from the authority that the building was in default for failure to pay the ad valorem portion of the sewer rent charge assessed by the authority, and that a lien had been placed on the property. The building filed an action challenging the authority's ability to charge the ad valorem assessment. The authority filed a motion for summary judgment, and the building filed a motion to stay any foreclosure proceedings instituted by the authority in collection of the lien. The trial court denied both motions and both parties sought review. The court found that the laws granting the authority the ability to charge the ad valorem assessment were not unconstitutional, that the building's claims were insufficient to support its claims of civil rights violations, that the authority's imposition of sewer rent charges on any basis other than those provided for in the law was unlawful, and that the authority's method for determining sewer rents was also unlawful.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's order by granting the authority's summary judgment motion as it applied to the building's claims of civil rights violations and the unconstitutionality of the laws allowing the authority to assess sewer rents.


HEADNOTES

Public Authorities -- Buffalo Sewer Authority -- Sewer Rents

Computation of sewer rents by defendant sewer authority using assessed valuation of the serviced property as a component of the charge for sewer service does not constitute the imposition of an unauthorized tax. Further, section 1180 of the Public Authorities Law,  [***2] as amended by section 1 of chapter 862 of the Laws of 1981, which provides that no ad valorem sewer rent be assessed against property exempt from real property taxes under specified sections of the Real Property Tax Law, and section 2 of chapter 862, which "legalized, validated, ratified and confirmed" the pre-existing practice of defendant "in granting exemption from ad valorem sewer rents granted properties exempt from real property taxes", are constitutional. However, defendant's practice of exempting certain tax-exempt properties from the assessed valuation component of its sewer rent, charging them only the component based on water consumption is unlawful, since the nonexempt users have paid a disproportionate share of the cost of rendering the service in violation of the mandate of section 1180 of the Public Authorities Law that sewer rents "be collected from all real property" on an "equitable basis"; should defendant persist in using the ad valorem component as a basis of its charges against nonexempt users, some other factor must be introduced into its methodology in relation to real property exempted under section 1180 in order to provide the equality required by said statute.

 [***3] Civil Rights -- Federal Civil Rights Claim

2. Allegations by plaintiff real property owner that defendant sewer authority granted ad valorem sewer rental exemptions to certain taxpayers and that defendant notified plaintiff's mortgagee that plaintiff's sewer rents were in default do not state causes of action for deprivation of civil rights under section 1983 of title 42 of the United States Code; plaintiff, which has never paid the challenged portion of the sewer rent, has never been deprived of any rights, privileges or immunities, and its claim that other entities have been granted exemptions to which they are not entitled is insufficient to establish a cause of action under section 1983, which was designed to provide a Federal remedy in cases where the State remedy is inadequate. Moreover, even if defendant's notice to plaintiff's mortgagee that sewer rents were unpaid were found to be a violation of plaintiff's rights, punitive damages could not be awarded, as defendant is a public authority and the reasons which foreclose imposition of punitive damages upon municipalities apply equally to it.

 

824 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Stemmer v. Board of Assessors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


97 A.D.2d 979; 468 N.Y.S.2d 785; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20828



November 4, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners sought review of a judgment by the Supreme Court, Onondaga County (New York), which ruled in favor of defendant board of assessors in the owners' action seeking a declaratory judgment that the method of fractional assessment required by N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 305 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The owners filed an action seeking a declaratory judgment that the method of fractional assessment of real property required by § 305 was unconstitutional. The trial court ruled in favor of the board. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's decision. The court held that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague. The court found that the language of the statue requiring all real property to be assessed at a uniform percentage of value referred to the market value of the property. The court further stated that § 305 did not violate the equal protection clause because taxing jurisdictions were not bound to assess property at 100 percent of its full value. So long as all real property was assessed at a uniform percentage value, as was required by the statute, there was no infringement of equal protection. The court found that fractional assessments declared invalid in a previous case were not so declared invalid on constitutional grounds but upon another section of the real property law that had since been repealed. The court concluded that the adoption of § 305 explicitly authorized the fractional assessment method already approved in other courts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

825 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Harry M.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


96 A.D.2d 201; 468 N.Y.S.2d 359; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20318



October 31, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs or disbursements, and new trial granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court, Dutchess County (New York), authorized respondent mental institution to continue to confine petitioner patient involuntarily under N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 15.35. The patient contended that he was denied due process where the lower court's jury instructions could have led the jury to conclude he needed further confinement merely because treatment would be beneficial to him.


OVERVIEW: After being found incompetent to stand trial for the misdemeanor charge of menacing, the patient, who had a history of chronic paranoid schizophrenia and mental retardation, was confined to a mental institution for 90 days. Upon the expiration of that period the state was obligated to release him or commence civil commitment proceedings. The state proceeded under N.Y. Mental Hyg. § 15.01 to seek involuntary admission to the mental institution. In charging the jury, the lower court made statements that could have been construed by the jury that the patient required further retention because treatment would be beneficial to him even though he posed no threat of harm to himself or others. The jury found that the patient should be involuntarily confined. On appeal, the court held that because it believed that the jury charge might have conveyed the impermissible impression that the patient legitimately could be confined for the purposes of treatment, the lower court's order confining the patient should be reversed.


OUTCOME: The lower court's order confining the patient involuntarily was reversed and a new trial was granted.


HEADNOTES

Insane and Other Incompetent Persons -- Involuntary Commitment -- Instructions to Jury

1. In a proceeding pursuant to section 15.35 of the Mental Hygiene Law wherein the Supreme Court certified a jury verdict finding that petitioner was mentally retarded and in need of further retention at a State developmental center, the court's instructions denied petitioner due process of law because they created a possibility that the jury would find him to be in need of further retention merely because treatment would be beneficial to him even if he posed no threat of harm to himself or others, and accordingly, a new trial is granted; if an individual can live safely in freedom and is not dangerous to himself or others, due process will not tolerate his involuntary [***2] commitment irrespective of whether treatment some may deem beneficial will be provided. A jury charge must include a directive that a patient may not be retained involuntarily unless the State establishes, by clear and convincing evidence, that he poses a substantial threat of physical harm to himself or others, and that such a threat can result from a failure to meet his essential need for food, clothing or shelter.

Evidence -- Business Records

2. In a proceeding brought pursuant to section 15.35 of the Mental Hygiene Law wherein it was found that petitioner was mentally retarded and in need of further retention at a State developmental center, it was error to permit petitioner's entire institutional file to be received into evidence as a business record ( CPLR 4518); while the recorded conclusions of physicians and others attending to petitioner's needs would be admissible where they were germane to treatment and could be utilized by in-court experts as the basis of their opinions, many of the entries included statements and remarks made by third parties who were not under a business duty to report to the entrant or were not otherwise admissible under an independent exception [***3] to the hearsay rule, and thus such portions should have been excluded.

 

826 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bartholomew v. State Ins. Dep't


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


97 A.D.2d 673; 469 N.Y.S.2d 219; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20301



October 27, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applicant sought review of the determination of respondent State Insurance Department (Department) (New York) that denied his application for a license as an insurance broker.


OVERVIEW: The applicant sought an insurance broker's license in New York. After a hearing, the Department denied the application based upon his denial of such a license in California. In proceedings there, it was determined, inter alia, that the applicant had improperly diverted funds from an insurance company that he owned to another company in order to avoid the termination of that business when it was statutorily insolvent. In confirming the Department's denial, the court held that where the applicant was given ample opportunity at a hearing in California to dispute the California finding of his untrustworthiness and offered no good reason not to credit the finding, such finding supplied the substantial evidence of character deficiency to support the denial of the license in New York.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the determination of the Department denying the applicant's application for a license as an insurance broker.

 

827 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Ten Hoeve v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


97 A.D.2d 678; 469 N.Y.S.2d 174; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20305



October 27, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Board of Education of Dundee Central School District, Board of Regents of the State of New York, and Commissioner of Education of the State of New York, sought to overturn the summary judgment entered by the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which held that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 8, § 156.3(b) was invalid and that respondent bus driver should not have been terminated.


OVERVIEW: Section 156.3(b) provided that bus drivers were not permitted to continue driving buses once they reached 65 years of age, and when the bus driver reached age 65 he was terminated. The lower court held that § Section 156.3(b) violated the State and Federal Constitutions and that it violated N.Y. Retire. & Soc. Sec. Law § 501(15). The court affirmed that portion of the lower court's order that held that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 8, § 156.3(b) was inconsistent with N.Y. Retire. & Soc. Sec. Law § 501(15) and thus invalid. Section 501(15) provided that the mandatory retirement age was 70 for all public employees except police or fire members, and N.Y. Retire. & Soc. Sec. Law § 500(a) provided that if there was a conflict between N.Y. Retire. & Soc. Sec. Law § 501(15) and another provision, then § 501(15) was controlling. Thus, the discharge of the bus driver at age 65 was improper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed that portion of the lower court's order which held that mandatory retirement for school bus drivers at the age of 65 violated New York's statutory retirement law. The portion of the lower court order holding that the bus driver's termination was unconstitutional under the State and Federal Constitutions was stricken.

 

828 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


State v. Wolowitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


96 A.D.2d 47; 468 N.Y.S.2d 131; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19876



October 24, 1983 



DISPOSITION: (1) Order and judgment (one paper) of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County, dated June 1, 1981, reversed insofar as appealed from, on the law, the first, second, third, seventh, eighth and ninth decretal paragraphs are deleted, the appellant's petition is amended in accordance with the opinion herein, and it is determined that so much of paragraph 7 of the form lease which provides that the interest on a tenant's security, less a 1% service charge "shall be paid to the Resident after expiration of the lease", is void as a matter of law, and [***2] (2) order of the same court dated March 3, 1980, which denied the State of New York's motion to dismiss the action against it for failure to state a cause of action and for failure to join necessary parties, reversed, on the law, and motion granted, and proceeding by the State of New York remitted to the Supreme Court, Suffolk County, for further proceedings consistent herewith. The respondent is to comply with the directions set forth in the opinion herewith with respect to the interest on the tenants' security within 30 days after service upon him of a copy of the order to be made hereon, with notice of entry.

One bill of costs is awarded to the State of New York.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of an order and judgment of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), that dismissed its petition brought pursuant to N.Y. Exec. Law § 63(12), declared that lease of appellee lessor was legal and binding, and denied a motion to dismiss the action against it for failure to state a cause of action and for failure to join necessary parties.


OVERVIEW: The State commenced an investigation of various acts and practices of the lessor pertaining to a fuel surcharge to tenants. The lessor commenced suit against the State seeking a declaration that the lease was legal and proper under state law and an injunction enjoining the State from interference with the lessor in the conduct of its business. The State moved to dismiss the complaint for failure to state a cause of action and to join the tenants as necessary parties. The State brought an action under N.Y. Exec. Law § 63(12). The cases were consolidated and the special term declared the lease legal and binding. The State appealed. The appellate court held that a declaratory judgment was meaningless absent the participation of the tenants and should be dismissed. The court held that the proceedings by the lessor were brought in improper format and that the State had authority under § 63(12). The court ruled a hearing was required because the record indicated the possible existence of both the procedural and substantive elements of unconscionability and to determine the appropriateness of the surcharge.


OUTCOME: The order of the supreme court, which denied a motion by the State to dismiss the action against it for failure to state a cause of action and for failure to join necessary parties, was reversed, and the motion to dismiss was granted. The State's motion, challenging the lessor's efforts to obtain a fuel surcharge from tenants and alleging that certain lease provisions were unconscionable, was remitted for a hearing. The State was awarded costs.


HEADNOTES

Pleading -- Sufficiency of Pleading -- Timeliness of Motion to Dismiss

1. In an action by plaintiff landlord against the Attorney-General seeking a declaration that plaintiff's leases are legal and proper and to enjoin defendant from interfering with plaintiff in the conduct of his business and from conducting an official investigation of plaintiff and his business, a motion to dismiss for failure to state a cause of action and for failure to join the tenants as necessary parties, made after service of an [***3] answer, was not untimely; a motion for dismissal based on lack of subject matter jurisdiction, failure to state a cause of action, or the absence of a person who should be a party, may be brought at any time even if such objection is not raised in the answer ( CPLR 3211, subd [e]).

Appeal -- Matters Reviewable

2. An action by a landlord against the State seeking a declaration that the landlord's leases are legal and proper and to enjoin the Attorney-General from interfering with the landlord in the conduct of his business and a special proceeding brought subsequently by the State against the landlord to enjoin the landlord from engaging in conduct violative of subdivision 12 of section 63 of the Executive Law were consolidated in an order and judgment which also dismissed certain causes of action in the Attorney-General's petition and declared that the leases were legal and binding, after the Attorney-General's motion to dismiss the action brought by the landlord was denied; the propriety of the landlord's action against the State is properly before the Appellate Division although no appeal was taken from the denial of the motion to dismiss, since the paper appealed from,  [***4] the order and final judgment, brought up for review the order denying said motion; an appeal from a final judgment brings up for review any nonfinal judgment or order necessarily affecting the final judgment ( CPLR 5501, subd [a]), and denial of the motion to dismiss the complaint certainly affected the final judgment.

Parties -- Necessary Parties

3. An action by a landlord against the State seeking a declaration that his leases are legal and proper is dismissed; the tenants who are parties to agreements with the landlord utilizing the leases at issue are necessary parties ( CPLR 1001, subd [a]), and although a declaratory judgment would be binding on the State and the Attorney-General, the right of any tenant in a judicial proceeding to claim that some portion of the lease was illegal would not be affected, since the tenant would not have had a full and fair opportunity to litigate the issue.

Injunctions -- When Remedy Appropriate

4. An action by a landlord to enjoin the Attorney-General from interfering in the conduct of his business and from investigating the landlord and his business, is dismissed; the landlord was seeking relief with respect to his own [***5] case only and thus should have brought an individual CPLR article 78 proceeding. Furthermore, courts do not review, by way of an action for an injunction, the exercise of discretion by public officials in the enforcement of State statutes in the absence of a clear violation of some constitutional mandate; the basis for the investigation was subdivision 12 of section 63 of the Executive Law, which empowers the Attorney-General to investigate repeated fraudulent or illegal acts or persistent fraud or illegality in the carrying on of business, and there was no claim of unconstitutionality; an accusation of harassment is insufficient to serve as a basis for enjoining the Attorney-General from attempting to enforce such a legislative mandate. Additionally, the action is merely a disguised writ of prohibition, which should also have been brought within a CPLR article 78 proceeding; however, so long as the Attorney-General's role was solely investigative, it was "executive" in nature and could not be the subject of a writ of prohibition.

Parties -- Standing

5. The Attorney-General has standing to bring a special proceeding containing a cause of action seeking to enjoin respondent [***6] landlord from attempting to obtain an unauthorized fuel surcharge from tenants, pursuant to subdivision 12 of section 63 of the Executive Law which requires a showing of repeated fraudulent or illegal acts or persistent fraud or illegality in the conducting of business; although the proceeding was instituted prior to the effective date of the 1981 amendment to the statute, which authorizes a proceeding when the respondent is guilty of only one act of alleged misconduct if it affects more than one person, and which is inapplicable because its effect would be to reverse a determination of the Supreme Court dismissing the fuel surcharge cause of action, respondent's actions are properly considered a repeated or persistent act within the meaning of the statute. The Attorney-General not only has standing to challenge the fuel surcharge on the ground of alleged fraud, but has also made a threshold demonstration that, upon satisfactory proof, he will be entitled to relief with respect to the same.

Landlord and Tenant -- Lease -- Unconscionability of Terms -- Challenge by Attorney-General

6. Causes of action questioning certain lease provisions on grounds of unconscionability [***7] are subject to challenge in a proceeding brought by the Attorney-General pursuant to subdivision 12 of section 63 of the Executive Law, which provides that when a person engages in persistent fraud or illegality in the carrying on of business the Attorney-General may apply for an injunction; unconscionability requires some showing of an absence of meaningful choice on the part of one of the parties together with contract terms which are unreasonably favorable to the other party and where the record indicates the possible existence of both procedural and substantive elements of unconscionability, a hearing is required. The fact that a lease is not negotiable and that a tenant cannot bargain over its individual terms alone warrants a hearing to afford the parties the opportunity of demonstrating the existence or lack of meaningful choice on the part of the tenants.

Parties -- Standing

7. The State of New York, through the offices of the Attorney-General, has standing to challenge the security provisions in a form lease; as of July 1, 1973, the Attorney-General was specifically empowered to bring an action or proceeding to compel compliance with the laws concerning tenants'  [***8] security deposits (General Obligations Law, § 7-107), and the fact that a petition fails to cite the statute giving standing is, at most, a defect of form; a proceeding to enjoin respondent landlord from engaging in conduct violative of subdivision 12 of section 63 of the Executive Law, insofar as it challenges a portion of the clause in the leases relating to interest on security deposits, should be deemed amended and brought under section 7-107 of the General Obligations Law.

Landlord and Tenant -- Security Deposits

8. A lease provision that a tenant's security deposit will be placed in an interest-bearing account and that the interest, less 1% service charge, will be paid to the tenant after expiration of the lease, is void as violative of section 7-103 of the General Obligations Law, which gives the tenant the option of having the landlord either hold the interest in trust for him until the expiration of the lease, apply it for the use or rental of the premises, or remit it to the tenant on an annual basis. Absent a violation of the lease justifying application of the security and interest to any deficit in the rent due, the tenant should be given a choice of the [***9] alternatives; signing the lease, absent language in that document informing the tenant of those alternatives required by section 7-103 cannot be taken as a selection thereof; new and renewed leases should not contain the violative language, but should include a provision allowing the tenant the statutorily mandated options.

 

829 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Buyce


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


97 A.D.2d 632; 469 N.Y.S.2d 29; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20260



October 20, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the County Court of Schenectady County (New York), which, upon a plea of guilty, convicted him of burglary in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant admittedly burglarized a private home in midday, gaining entry by breaking a window in the back door and unlocking it. On appeal, defendant argued that an amendment to N.Y. Penal Law § 140.25(2) classifying a daytime burglary of a dwelling as punishable as a class C violent felony, was unconstitutional. Upon review, the court found defendant's argument without merit. Absent a showing that, when it amended § 140.25(2), the New York Legislature was not acting in pursuit of permissible State objectives and that the means adopted by it were not reasonably related to the accomplishment of those objectives, the court would not substitute its judgment for that of the Legislature. The reclassifying the crime of burglarizing a dwelling during the day from a class D felony to a class C felony, with the concomitant increase in penalty, did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment. When compared with sentences imposed for other crimes within the State and punishments imposed for the same or similar crimes in other jurisdictions, the sentence was not grossly disproportionate.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for burglary in the second degree was affirmed.

 

830 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Ward v. New York State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


97 A.D.2d 640; 468 N.Y.S.2d 926; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20269



October 20, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner law firm partners sought review, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, of a determination by respondent New York State Tax Commission, which upheld notices of deficiencies and additional assessments of their state nonresident income tax returns.


OVERVIEW: The partners had used, for calculation of their individual state income taxes, a two-pronged method of computation that, they asserted, accounted for the partnership's higher rent obligations within the state. They did not, however, request prior approval to use the method. The Commission's computation used a three-pronged method, which included a calculation of the percentage of property expense and which resulted in a higher percentage of the partners' net income attributable to their practice within the state. The court, in confirming the Commission's determination, found that the partners did not carry their burden, under N.Y. Tax Law § 689(e), to overturn the deficiency assessment. In addition, the court found that, under N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 20, § 131.15(c), the Commission was required to use the three-pronged test because the partnership's books and records did not disclose to the satisfaction of the Commission an appropriate proportion of business allocable to the state. The court further found that the Commission did not act arbitrarily or capriciously, either in rejecting the partners' two-pronged method or in applying the required three-pronged test.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the Commission's determination upholding the deficiency notices and additional assessments of income taxes. The court dismissed the partners' petition, with costs.

In re Marie B.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]

96 A.D.2d 1140; 467 N.Y.S.2d 454; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19822

832 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ameigh


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


95 A.D.2d 367; 467 N.Y.S.2d 718; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19368



September 22, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, on the law, motion to suppress denied, and matter remitted to the County Court of Tompkins County for trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of the decision from the Tompkins County Court (New York), which granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence of a blood alcohol test performed by a hospital in the felony action filed against defendant for driving while intoxicated.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and charged with a felony for driving while intoxicated and he refused to take a blood alcohol test at the hospital but the hospital administered the test on its own direction. Defendant filed a motion to suppress the evidence of the blood alcohol test administered at the hospital and the trial court granted the motion on the basis that the test was not administered pursuant to the requirements of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1194. The state challenged the decision and the court reversed and held that § 1194 did not prohibit the use at trial of a medically administered blood test not procured by the police. Section 1194 applied only to situations where blood alcohol tests were procured at the direction of the police. Under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 710.20(a) a motion to suppress such evidence could only be made by a defendant who was aggrieved by unlawful or improper acquisition of evidence. Because the hospital administered the blood alcohol test on its own initiative it was not unlawfully or improperly acquired and not subject to suppression.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision to suppress evidence of a blood alcohol test in the felony action against defendant.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Chemical Tests

A motion by defendant, arrested for driving while intoxicated, to suppress the results of a chemical test to determine his blood alcohol level, which test was performed by hospital personnel for diagnostic purposes after defendant had refused a request by the arresting officer to submit to such a test pursuant to section 1194 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, is denied; the procedures established in the statute are intended solely to govern tests requested or procured by an arresting officer and do not bar admissibility of the result of a test performed without police involvement. Section 1194 does not prohibit the use at trial of a medically administered test result not procured by the police.

Sinclair v. Div. of Licensing of Dep't of State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


96 A.D.2d 1130; 467 N.Y.S.2d 719; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19797



September 22, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate challenged a judgment from the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York), which dismissed his application, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, to annul a determination of respondent, Division of Licensing of the Department of State (division), denying his request for appointment as a notary public. The inmate was a convicted predicate felon serving his sentence in a correctional facility.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was serving a term of imprisonment of 7 1/2 to 15 years at a correctional facility when he requested appointment as a notary public. The division denied the inmate's request and advised that, due to the inmate's felony conviction, he could not be so appointed unless he received either a certificate of good conduct or an executive pardon under N.Y. Exec. Law §130. The trial court dismissed the inmate's application. The inmate argued on appeal that § 130 was unconstitutional in that it erected an absolute bar to the appointment of felons to the office of notary public and utilized an irrational criterion, i.e., a prior felony conviction, for an assessment of fitness. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment and held that when the conditional disqualification set forth in § 130 was read in conjunction with the standards governing the issuance of a certificate of good conduct, the statutory scheme did not work an irrational or permanent bar to a convicted felon seeking appointment as a notary public. The court further found that the criteria in § 130 were entirely rational in view of the responsibilities attendant the office of notary public.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment affirming the division's decision to deny the inmate's request for appointment as a notary public.

 

834 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dodson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


96 A.D.2d 1116; 467 N.Y.S.2d 709; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19783



September 15, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the County Court of Montgomery County (New York), which convicted defendant of burglary in the first degree and criminal trespass in the first degree, sentenced defendant to a term of imprisonment, and assessed fines against defendant for each conviction.


OVERVIEW: The victim, defendant's former employer, testified that defendant illegally entered the victim's home, pointed a rifle at the victim, demanded money from the victim, and struck the victim with the rifle barrel. Defendant testified that he was at work and then later at a bar when the alleged crimes occurred. There was evidence corroborating both the victim's and defendant's version of the facts. On appeal, the court held that defendant's acquittal of robbery did not preclude defendant's conviction for burglary because there was sufficient evidence of defendant's larcenous intent. The court affirmed defendant's conviction for criminal trespass because criminal trespass in the first degree as defined in N.Y. Penal Law § 140.17(1) was not a lesser included offense of burglary in the first degree as defined in N.Y. Penal Law § 140.30(1). The court determined that the trial court's assessment of fines under N.Y. Penal Law § 60.35 constituted an unconstitutional ex post facto application of § 60.35 because the acts for which defendant was convicted occurred before the revenue raising measures of § 60.35 took effect.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by annulling the fines that were assessed against defendant. The court affirmed the modified judgment that convicted and sentenced defendant for burglary and criminal trespass.

 

835 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Public Employees Federation v. Cuomo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


96 A.D.2d 1118; 467 N.Y.S.2d 696; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19784



September 15, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent state appealed an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York) that granted petitioner union's motion for a preliminary injunction.


OVERVIEW: Under new legislation, a nonvested employee leaving state service would not have been allowed to withdraw his or her contributions until death or age 62, N.Y. Retire. & Soc. Sec. Law § 613(c) (1983). The union commenced a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding seeking a declaration that the new legislation was unconstitutional on various grounds and an injunction against enforcement of the legislation declared unconstitutional. The state presented a motion to dismiss the petition. The lower court granted the union's motion for a preliminary injunction. The state filed a notice of appeal, statutorily staying the preliminary injunction under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5519(a)(1). The court reversed. The court held that without a preliminary injunction, no irreparable harm would have been done to the union in the event that the union was correct on the merits of its challenge to the statute, but substantial harm would have been done to the state if the union was incorrect on the merits. That some nonvested employees opted to leave state service prior to the effective date of the statute did not constitute irreparable harm.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that portion of the special term's order granting the preliminary injunction.

 

836 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hooks


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


96 A.D.2d 1001; 467 N.Y.S.2d 8; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19611



August 18, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the County Court of Broome County (New York), which, following a jury trial, convicted defendant of two counts of manslaughter in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of manslaughter in the first degree for the killing of two people who had earlier tried to injure him. Defendant received the statutory minimum sentence of two consecutive terms of two years to six years each. Defendant sought review on the ground that the imposition of the mandated sentence of imprisonment, under the circumstances surrounding the commission of the offense and in view of defendant's character and background, constituted an unconstitutional application of New York's penal statutes in violation of the cruel and unusual punishment clauses of the United States and New York Constitutions, U.S. Const. amend. VIII, N.Y. Const. art. I, § 5. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that its discretionary power to modify sentences did not permit the reduction of a sentence below the minimum allowed by law. The court found that defendant's sentence was not cruel and unusual because it was not irrational to require some minimum prison sentence for a violent category of serious life-endangering offenses.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence of two consecutive two-year to six-year terms of imprisonment for two counts of manslaughter in the first degree.

People v. Sherman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


96 A.D.2d 1003; 467 N.Y.S.2d 91; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19613



August 18, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court at Trial Term, Chemung County (New York), convicting defendant upon his plea of guilty of the crime of forgery in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered a bargained plea of guilty to the class D felony of forgery in the second degree. The court accepted the plea in full satisfaction of a six-count indictment. Defendant pleaded guilty to an unrelated count of third degree grand larceny in another court. Defendant was sentenced to a term of two to four years on the forgery conviction; this sentence was made to run consecutively to the larceny sentence. On review, defendant maintained that a sentence of probation would have been more appropriate. The court disagreed but sent the case back in the interests of justice for a hearing to ascertain the actual terms of the forgery plea bargain. According to the court, the record of the proceedings did not, by itself, establish that the district attorney was under any obligation to recommend that defendant's sentences run concurrently. Accordingly, the court withheld its decision and ordered the matter remitted to the trial court that produced the forgery conviction for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court withheld its decision on defendant's appeal of his conviction and sentence for forgery, and the court ordered the matter remitted to the trial court for further proceedings to determine the terms of defendant's plea bargain.

 

838 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Frye v. Commissioner of Finance


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


95 A.D.2d 274; 466 N.Y.S.2d 3; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 18959



August 11, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Determination of respondents dated December 24, 1981, which found petitioner liable for commercial rent or occupancy tax is annulled and otherwise the petition is dismissed; determination of respondents dated December 18, 1981 with respect to the unincorporated business tax, and the determination of respondents dated December 24, 1981, with respect to the occupancy tax, are confirmed, all without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer filed a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 petition challenging a determination of respondent Commissioner of Finance (Commissioner) finding tax liabilities owing by the taxpayer.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer worked as a journalist, and the Commissioner assessed tax liabilities against him. The taxpayer filed a petition challenging the determinations, and the court annulled the commercial rent and occupancy tax. The court confirmed the determination of the unincorporated business and occupancy taxes, holding that the taxpayer was not exempt as a journalist. Journalism did not constitute a profession under N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 20, § 203.11(b)(1)(ii). The taxpayer did not complete a course at a specialized school of journalism, nor was he required to have a license to be a journalist. Also, there was no organized body of rules, standards of conduct, or ethics to which he is required to conform. Finally, there were no recognized standards for entry into the field of journalism, and no agency that passes upon one's qualifications to be a journalist.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the Commissioner's assessment of a commercial rent and occupancy tax against the taxpayer but confirmed the assessment of an unincorporated business tax and an occupancy tax.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Unincorporated Business Income Tax -- Exemption for Professionals

The Commissioner of Finance of the City of New York did not abuse his discretion in determining that petitioner, a self-employed journalist, was not a professional within the meaning of section S46-2.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York and [***2] thus not exempt from the unincorporated business tax; petitioner did not complete a course at a specialized school of journalism, he is not required to have a license to indicate that sufficient qualifications have been met prior to engaging in his occupation, he is not subject to any disciplinary body which has power to supervise his activities, and there is no organized body of rules, standards of conduct or ethics to which he is required to conform.

 

839 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Peterson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


96 A.D.2d 871; 465 N.Y.S.2d 743; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19465



August 8, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the County Court, Westchester County (New York), which denied defendant's motion to strike an alibi demand as unconstitutional and granted the people's motion to strike defendant's notice of alibi.


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendant appealed from the judgment of the lower court that denied his motion to strike an alibi demand as unconstitutional, and granted the people's motion to strike defendant's notice of alibi. The People argued that defendant's notice of alibi was at least 174 days late and that he neither sought permission of the court to file the late notice nor attempted to justify the delay. Moreover, the notice itself was defective in that no business addresses were listed. The court reversed the lower court's judgment and denied the People's motion to strike defendant's notice of alibi and ordered a new trial. The governing statute allowed for the exclusion of alibi testimony in the court's discretion where the defense called an alibi witness at trial without having provided the People with the requisite preliminary information. Defendant's failure to make timely service of a notice of alibi was not a fatal defect because the trial court, for good cause shown, could have extended the period for service of the notice. The People were not unduly prejudiced inasmuch as they were served with the notice of alibi two weeks prior to the tentative scheduling of the trial.


OUTCOME: Lower court's judgment was reversed and the People's motion to strike defendant's notice of alibi was denied and a new trial was ordered because defendant's failure to file a timely notice of alibi was not a fatal defect because the court, for good cause, could have extended the filing period and because the People were given two weeks notice before trial.

 

840 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Joch v. County of Tompkins


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


95 A.D.2d 84; 466 N.Y.S.2d 755; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 18521



July 28, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, on the law, with costs; defendant's motion denied, plaintiff's cross motion for summary judgment granted, it is declared that Resolutions Nos. 215, 216 and 217 are void, the term of District Attorney of Tompkins County was full time during plaintiff's tenure, and plaintiff is entitled to the difference between the amount paid to him and the amount paid to the County Judge during the period in issue, less statutory deductions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former district attorney sought review of an order from the Special Term in Tompkins County (New York), which granted respondent county's motion for summary judgment and dismissed the attorney's suit seeking a declaration that certain resolutions were void. The attorney also sought an order declaring that his position had been full-time during his term.


OVERVIEW: Prior to the attorney taking office, the county passed a series of resolutions rescinding previous resolutions and declaring the position of district attorney as a part-time position. The state legislature had recently passed an amendment to N.Y. Jud. Law § 183-a requiring full-time district attorneys in certain counties be paid the same salary as the county judge. At the end of the attorney's last term, he filed a suit against the county seeking a declaration that the resolutions were void and that his position had been full-time. The trial court granted the county's motion for summary judgment, but on appeal, the court granted the attorney's cross-motion for summary judgment. The court held that the attorney was entitled to the difference between his salary and that paid to the county judge because § 183-a entitled him to the pay both prospectively and retroactively. The court further rejected the county's estoppel and laches defenses. Finally, the court held that because the resolutions were presented as a package, the invalidity of one of them negated all of them, so the previous resolutions designating the attorney's position as full-time were still in effect.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an order from the trial court, which granted the county's motion for summary judgment. The attorney's cross-motion for summary judgment was granted.


HEADNOTES

District and Prosecuting Attorneys -- Compensation

In an action commenced December 30, 1981 by plaintiff, who served as District Attorney of Tompkins County from January 1, 1975 until December 31, 1981, seeking a declaration that certain 1974 county resolutions designating the office of District Attorney as part time (96%) and fixing plaintiff's salary for his second term at $ 30,000 annually were unconstitutional, that the office of District Attorney was full time throughout his seven-year service, and that he is entitled to [***2] receive the difference between salary received by the County Judge and plaintiff for the seven-year period, plaintiff's motion for summary judgment is granted; the requirement of section 183-a of the Judiciary Law that full-time District Attorneys in certain counties be paid at the same salary level as County Court Judges in their counties is constitutional, as is a retroactive increase in the salary of a District Attorney; plaintiff has not waived his right to object since acceptance of less than a statutorily mandated salary does not bar recovery of the full amount due because of any principle of waiver or estoppel, the claim is not barred by laches since in addition to a showing of delay there must also be evidence of prejudice to defendant attributable to the delay, and, furthermore, the 1974 resolutions have as their sole objective the unauthorized contravention of section 183-a of the Judiciary Law and subdivision 8 of section 700 of the County Law to the end that Tompkins County have a full-time District Attorney at a salary fixed by its county legislature.

 

841 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Beach v. Shanley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


94 A.D.2d 542; 466 N.Y.S.2d 725; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 18504; 9 Media L. Rep. 1991



July 21, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, on the law, and motion denied, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prosecutor sought review of an order of the Rensselaer County Court (New York) that quashed a subpoena issued to respondent reporter commanding the reporter's appearance before a special grand jury.


OVERVIEW: A special grand jury had been investigating alleged criminal conduct by two employees of the county's sheriff's office. As a result of the investigation, the grand jury issued a report that was required by law to remain sealed for 31 days following its disclosure to the person named therein. Prior to the expiration of the 31-day period, the reporter obtained information on the contents of the sealed report and broadcast such information as part of a news report. The prosecutor issued a subpoena to the reporter commanding his appearance before the grand jury to investigate the identity of the reporter's source given the fact that there was a likelihood that the source had committed a felony by divulging the contents of the report. The reporter filed a motion to quash the subpoena on the grounds that he was privileged not to reveal the identity of his source. The trial court granted the motion, and the court reversed. The court held that neither the First Amendment nor the New York Shield Law, N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 79-h, protected the reporter from making his news sources known to a grand jury.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order quashing the subpoena that commanded the reporter to appear before a special grand jury.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Press

The "Shield Law" (Civil Rights Law, § 79-h) cannot be construed to exempt a reporter from naming a news source where a Grand Jury is inquiring into possible official misconduct relating to unlawful disclosure of a sealed Grand Jury report and the identity of the newsperson's source is a significant factor in the investigation (NY Const, art I, § 6); further, neither the United States Constitution nor the New York Constitution protects a reporter from disclosing the identity of his source to the Grand Jury if the likelihood exists that the reporter can supply information to aid in determining whether illegal conduct has occurred. Accordingly, the County Court improperly granted petitioner [***2] newscaster's motion to quash a subpoena seeking to have him testify before a Special Grand Jury and to name the source who disclosed the contents of a sealed Grand Jury report, which was used in a broadcast wherein it was stated that the sealed report contained allegations against a named public official and recommended that he be removed from office; although the source may have been someone not subject to criminal liability (see Penal Law, § 215.70), the prosecutor is not required to establish that a crime has been carried out before Grand Jury testimony can be exacted.
