867 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Slominski v. Rutkowski


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


91 A.D.2d 202; 458 N.Y.S.2d 757; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16115



January 21, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously affirmed without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Erie County Comptroller, appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court of Erie County (New York), which, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78(1), dismissed her petition that sought an order that compelled respondent, the Erie County Executive, to certify the necessity of filling certain vacancies in the comptroller's office. The executive appealed the trial court's grant of attorney fees to the comptroller.


OVERVIEW: The comptroller sought to compel the county executive to certify her vacant positions. She attacked the constitutionality of Erie County, N.Y., Administrative Code § 3.09(e) and the power given the executive to block the filling of vacancies as an unlawful delegation of legislative power to the executive and a curtailment of the powers of elected officials contrary to the N.Y. Mun. Home Rule Law § 23(2)(f). The trial court granted the executive's motion to dismiss the petition but decreed that she was entitled to attorney's fees in an amount to be determined after a hearing. Both parties appealed. The court affirmed because the executive was properly granted the oversight of expenditures of budgeted funds during the fiscal year as chief budget officer under the county charter. The action was converted by the court to a declaratory judgment action after the positions were filled. The power the legislature gave the executive was in harmony and consistent with the county charter and was not an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power. Attorney's fees were proper for an office-holder who brought a good-faith action in an official capacity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which dismissed the comptroller's petition and granted her attorney fees in her action that sought to compel the county executive to certify the necessity of filling certain vacancies in the comptroller's office.


HEADNOTES

Counties -- Powers of County Executive

1. A proceeding by the Erie County Comptroller against respondent Erie County Executive and others challenging respondent's refusal to certify, pursuant to a provision of the Erie County Administrative Code, the necessity of filling certain vacancies in petitioner's office, was properly dismissed, since the duty of evaluating the need for a specific position and balancing that against the savings that would otherwise accrue if it remained unfilled for the balance of the year falls within the powers and responsibilities granted in the County Charter to the County Executive as chief executive officer, administrative head of the county government and chief budget officer, and the power the legislature has given the executive in said administrative code provision is [***2] in harmony and consistent with the charter; furthermore, since the administrative code provision transfers from other branches to the executive no additional authority but merely sets forth the details, as directed by the County Charter, of how the executive shall function within the limits of the power already granted to him, said administrative code provision effected no abolition, curtailment or transfer of the powers of the comptroller or the legislators and no referendum was necessary to effect it (Municipal Home Rule Law, § 23, subd 2, par f).

Public Officers -- Reimbursement for Attorney's Fees for Litigation in Official Capacity

2. In a CPLR article 78 proceeding brought by petitioner Erie County Comptroller against respondent Erie County Executive and others challenging respondent's refusal to certify, pursuant to a provision of the Erie County Administrative Code, the necessity of filling certain vacancies in petitioner's office, petitioner should be reimbursed for reasonable attorney's fees, although the petition was dismissed, since the petitioner has concededly brought the proceeding in her official capacity, it involves substantial questions of public interest [***3] which are likely to recur and petitioner's good faith is unquestioned; under the circumstances, where the respondents include the highest county government officials, it would have been pointless to insist that petitioner go through the motions of requesting representation from the county.

Appeal -- Academic and Moot Questions

3. In a CPLR article 78 proceeding brought by petitioner Erie County Comptroller against respondent Erie County Executive and others challenging respondent's refusal to certify, pursuant to a provision of the Erie County Administrative Code, the necessity of filling certain vacancies in petitioner's office, respondents' motion to dismiss the proceeding as moot since said positions have now been certified and filled, is denied, as this is an exceptional case involving substantial questions of public interest which are likely to recur; furthermore, because petitioner's contentions as to the invalidity of the administrative code provision and its specific application to the unfilled vacancies have been resolved, her CPLR article 78 proceeding is no longer appropriate and, accordingly, it is converted to a declaratory judgment action challenging the validity [***4] of the code provision on its face ( CPLR 103, subd [c]).

 

868 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Overseas Nat'l Airways, Inc. v. State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


91 A.D.2d 162; 458 N.Y.S.2d 711; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16109



January 20, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Petition granted, with costs, determination annulled, and matter remitted to the State Tax Commission for further proceedings not inconsistent herewith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner air transportation corporation sought review of the decision of respondent, New York State Tax Commission, which sustained franchise tax deficiencies, pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 184.


OVERVIEW: The corporation was a Delaware corporation with corporate headquarters in New York. The corporation was assessed franchise tax deficiencies for taxes allegedly due under § 184. The deficiencies were imposed as a result of various transactions in which the corporation was involved and which took place outside of New York. Thereafter, the corporation filed a petition requesting a redetermination or a refund. A hearing officer recommended granting the corporation's petition. However, the Commission sustained the deficiencies. The corporation sought review of the Commission's determination in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding. The court held that there was nothing talismanic about the concept of "commercial domicile" that automatically rendered that concept applicable when taxation of income from intangibles was at issue. Section 184 taxed earnings from sources within New York and excluded earnings from interstate commerce. The disputed earnings were not from sources within New York and should not be taxed as they were derived from business of an interstate character. The Commission's determination went beyond its authority, and in effect, it usurped the power to legislate new taxes.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the Commission's deficiency determination, granted the corporation's petition, and remitted the matter to the Commission for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Franchise Tax on Aviation Corporations

A determination by respondent State Tax Commission sustaining a franchise tax deficiency pursuant to section 184 of the Tax Law against petitioner airline, a Delaware corporation with corporate headquarters in New York, which engaged in the interstate and foreign air transport of passengers and made no flights entirely within New York State, is annulled, since the gains, interest and dividends and the fees that are the subject of this controversy derived from transactions that took place in Europe and in States other than New York; said earnings were not from sources within the State and fall within the statutory exclusion [***2] for earnings derived from business of an interstate character.

 

869 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Air Transp. Ass'n v. N.Y. State Dep't of Taxation & Fin.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


91 A.D.2d 169; 458 N.Y.S.2d 709; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16111



January 20, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state tax department sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which granted plaintiff taxpayers' motion for reargument and, upon reargument, declared N.Y. Tax Law § 184(1) unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers sought a declaration that § 184(1) imposed a tax that was prohibited by § 1113 of the Federal Aviation Act (Act), 49 U.S.C.S. § 1513, and was, therefore, unconstitutional under the Supremacy Clause of the United States Constitution. The trial court declared the statute unconstitutional. On appeal, the court affirmed. The Act prohibited states from taxing gross receipts from the sale of air transport services. The department contended that N.Y. Tax Law § 184(1) was a franchise tax, which included gross receipts in its measurement, but was not entirely composed of gross receipts from the sale of air coverage and was, thus, permissible. According to the court, the plain terms of the Act directly prohibited the tax and did not allow for a construction, which permitted a tax wither directly or indirectly on gross receipts derived from air transportation. The court further noted that its conclusion was supported by the legislative history of the Act. In enacting the Act, Congress refused to adopt suggested language that would have allowed state taxation similar to that imposed by § 184(1).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the declaration that the statute was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Franchise Tax on Aviation Corporations

Subdivision 1 of section 184 of the Tax Law, which imposes a franchise tax of three quarters of 1% of gross earnings from all sources within the State on corporations formed for or principally engaged in the conduct of aviation, is unconstitutional as it is pre-empted by section 1113 of the Federal Aviation Act (US Code, tit 49, § 1513), which provides that no State shall levy or collect a tax or other charge on persons traveling in air commerce, on the carriage of persons traveling in air commerce or on the sale of air transportation or on the gross receipts derived therefrom, and thus prohibits both direct and indirect State taxation of the sale of air transportation.

 

870 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Holy Spirit Asso. for Unification of World Christianity v. Rosenfeld


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


91 A.D.2d 190; 458 N.Y.S.2d 920; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16114



January 17, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Westchester County, dated February 18, 1981, affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant church sought review of the decision from the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), which dismissed the church's action that alleged that a zoning ordinance was unconstitutional and that respondent zoning board erred in denying the church's application for a special use permit.


OVERVIEW: The church applied for a special use permit from the zoning board. The church stipulated that there would be no activities in violation of zoning ordinances prior to the hearing on the issue. At the hearing, the zoning board learned that the church did conduct activities in violation of the ordinances and had made other misrepresentations. The board denied the church's application. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that because the evidence presented at the zoning board's hearing on the church's special use permit did not show that the use the church sought would have a direct and immediate adverse effect upon the health, safety, or welfare of the community, the board's denial on that basis was improper. However, the court held that the church's misrepresentations prior to the hearing that it would abide by the current zoning ordinances was sufficient evidence that the church would not comply with the conditions imposed on its proposed use; thus, the board's denial on that ground was proper. The court ruled that the zoning board was not biased and that the zoning board had a right to complete its own investigation of the church's application.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision that affirmed the zoning board's denial of the church's special use permit.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Special Use Permit

1. A zoning board's denial of an application for a special use permit for the operation by the Unification Church of a training and recruitment center was based on competent and substantial evidence, including findings of the church's misrepresentations and its conceded violations of provisions of the zoning ordinance, although the evidence was insufficient to support the board's finding that the proposed activities of the Unification Church posed a threat to public [***2] health or morals. The church's failure to comply with the intensity of use provisions of the zoning ordinance was a proper ground for denying the application, as were the misrepresentations made by the church which were directly related to land use while the application for a special use permit was pending; since the church would not comply with representations made while the application for a special use permit was pending, the zoning board could properly infer that it would not comply with conditions imposed on its proposed use; further the church's deceit, in and of itself, justified denial of the special use permit. Nor has the church established that the zoning board was biased.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Proposed Religious Use

2. In order to deny a special use permit for a religious use, it must be convincingly shown that the proposed use will have a direct and immediate adverse effect upon the health, safety or welfare of the community; every effort must be made to accommodate the religious use subject to conditions reasonably related to land use. Accordingly, where the Unification Church applied for a special use permit for the operation of a training [***3] and recruitment center, and there was substantial evidence that the regimen imposed by the church is not beneficial to the health of the participants, but not that the average participant is in imminent danger of losing his life or health, the evidence failed to demonstrate that the activities of the church posed a threat to public health or morals; public intolerance, animosity, or unrest does not justify a prohibition of free assembly and association.

 

871 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Candie Lee "W"


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


91 A.D.2d 1106; 458 N.Y.S.2d 347; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16448



January 13, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of a decision of the Family Court of Clinton County (New York) finding for respondent Clinton County Department of Social Services and declaring the mother's child permanently neglected under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384-b. The court found that the mother's rights in her child should be terminated and the child placed for adoption.


OVERVIEW: The Clinton County Department of Social Services sought to terminate a mother's parental rights to her child under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384-b. The trial court terminated the mother's rights and ordered that the child be put up for adoption, and the court affirmed the trial court's judgment. In reaching this decision, the court noted that the state was required to demonstrate that the parent had neglected its child by clear and convincing evidence before a parent's rights could be terminated. The court found that the mother's mental disability did not exempt her from the requirements of § 384-b, and noted that the department had undertaken efforts to strengthen the relationship between the mother and her child. Further, the court noted that the failure to plan for a child's future constituted neglect of that child even where the parent kept contact with the child.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

872 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Shell Oil Co. v. New York State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


91 A.D.2d 81; 458 N.Y.S.2d 938; 1983 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16099



January 6, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Orders and judgments modified, on the law, by declaring that (1) the anti-pass-through provision is void under the commerce clause and, for the period up to October 1, 1981, also void under the supremacy clause, and (2) the liability for the tax in question shall be permitted to accrue until a final appellate determination of the challenge to the anti-pass-through provision, and the State is required to refund taxes collected for any period during which that provision is ultimately held invalid, to the extent that such taxes were not passed on to out-of-State customers, and by adding thereto a direction to defendant State Commissioner [***3] of Taxation and Finance to determine that, pending final appellate determination of the challenge to the anti-pass-through provision, all taxes collected are necessary for refunds, and to notify the State Comptroller to that effect, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant oil companies sought review of adverse judgments in three separate cases challenging the 1980 N.Y. Laws, chs. 271 and 272, (act), which imposed a franchise tax on oil corporations which was not to be passed on to consumers, known as an "anti-pass-through" provision. Three cases were consolidated for appeal.


OVERVIEW: In response to a severe financial problem facing New York's public transportation system, the state enacted the act to create a special fund under the State Finance Law, from which allocations would be made to various regional transportation authorities by the franchise tax, known as an "anti-pass-through" provision. There also existed a "self-destruct" provision of the act, in which the tax ceased to be in force upon a judicial or administrative determination preventing enforcement. On appeal, the court modified judgment, and held that: (1) the anti-pass-through provision was void under the supremacy clause because up to expiration in 1981, federal statutes pre-empted the field of price regulation of petroleum products; and (2) the anti-pass-through provision was void under the commerce clause because the provision worked a discrimination by shifting the burden on the tax from New York customers to out of state customers. The court held that because the triggering events in the self-destruct provisions of the act were not met, the liability for the tax in question was permitted to accrue until a final appellate determination of the challenge to the anti-pass-through provision.


OUTCOME: The court modified the orders and judgments of the three cases in favor of the oil corporations, and held that: (1) the anti-pass-through provision was void under the commerce and supremacy clauses; and (2) the liability for the tax would be permitted to accrue until final appellate determination, and the state was required to refund taxes collected for any period during which the provision was ultimately held invalid.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Gross Profits Tax on Petroleum Producers

1. The amended statutory definition of "oil company" contained in section 182 (subd 2, par [a]) of the Tax Law (amd L 1981, ch 1043, § 68), identifying the class of enterprises subject to a 2% gross profits tax as including certain vertically integrated petroleum corporations that exceed threshold amounts of crude oil production and refining capacity, does not deny plaintiffs the equal protection of the laws in thereby exempting other major oil industry competitors who either are not vertically integrated or lack the significant production or refining capacity required by the statute. The Legislature could reasonably have concluded that the defined companies' corporate organization and the magnitude of their operations in every aspect of the petroleum industry afforded the statutory class a market power and economic advantage over their competitors [***4] and special opportunities to obtain the benefit of the conditions existing in the world-wide market; the equal protection clause does not prevent a State from taxing such economic power and competitive advantage.

Taxation -- Gross Profits Tax on Petroleum Producers

2. The amended statutory definition of "oil company" contained in section 182 (subd 2, par [a]) of the Tax Law (amd L 1981, ch 1043, § 68), identifying the class of enterprises subject to a 2% gross profits tax as including certain vertically integrated petroleum corporations that exceed threshold amounts of crude oil production and refining capacity, does not unlawfully discriminate against interstate commerce. In neither purpose nor effect does the tax discriminate, through the definition of "oil company", against interstate petroleum producers in favor of intrastate products, or against the statutory class in favor of New York companies similarly situated.

Taxation -- Gross Profits Tax on Petroleum Producers

3. The brief retroactive effect of a 2% gross profits tax imposed on certain oil companies (Tax Law, § 182), arising out of its application to plaintiffs' gross receipts for their entire [***5] taxable year within which the act became law (June 18, 1980), does not offend due process; nor does the hypothetically uneven effect of that limited retroactivity because of possible variations in corporate taxable years deny any "oil company" the equal protection of the laws; the administrative convenience of keying the tax to such companies' taxable years fully justifies any such discrepancy.

Taxation -- Gross Profits Tax on Petroleum Producers

4. There is no constitutional infirmity in the State's applying a 2% gross profits tax, imposed on certain oil companies pursuant to section 182 of the Tax Law, to the sales of nonpetroleum products of the oil companies, while not taxing similar sales by their non-oil company competitors; since the equal protection clause does not prevent large vertically integrated oil companies from being singled out for the purpose of imposing a State tax, it follows that the tax thus imposed may reach activities for which others not in the class are not taxed.

Taxation -- Gross Profits Tax on Petroleum Producers -- Anti-Pass-Through Provision

5. The Federal Emergency Petroleum Allocation Act and related regulations (US Code, tit [***6] 15, § 751 et seq.; 10 CFR Part 212) pre-empted the field of price regulation of petroleum products up to the point when the Federal statute expired on September 30, 1981; accordingly, enforcement of the anti-pass-through provision of section 182 (subd 11, par [a]) of the Tax Law, which imposes a 2% gross profits tax on certain oil companies while preventing such companies from passing this additional tax on the consumers of New York State, was invalid for the period from the inception of the act, June 18, 1980, to the date of the expiration of the Federal statute, as being in conflict with the supremacy clause, and plaintiffs were entitled to a declaration of that invalidity.

Taxation -- Gross Profits Tax on Petroleum Producers -- Anti-Pass-Through Provision

6. The anti-pass-through provision of section 182 (subd 11, par [a]) of the Tax Law, which imposes a 2% gross profits tax on profits generated by certain oil companies in New York State, while preventing said companies from passing this additional tax on to the State's consumers, works a discrimination violative of the commerce clause. The practical effect of the prohibition is to shift the direct burden of [***7] the tax from the companies' New York customers to their out-of-State customers, in an attempt to recoup the cost of the tax on New York sales; the potentially affected class are therefore treated differently because of the pass-through prohibition and solely upon the basis of their geographical location. If the anti-pass-through provision of the tax is upheld, the over-all inflationary impact of the tax would fall, not on New York's economy, but on those of its sister States, and this in turn would invite the kind of reciprocal reprisals to protect the economies of those States which the commerce clause was designed to prevent.

Taxation -- Gross Profits Tax on Petroleum Producers -- "Self-Destruct" Provision

7. Pursuant to the "self-destruct" provisions of an act imposing a gross profits tax on certain oil companies (Tax Law, § 182), which tax ceases to be in force and effect upon a judicial or administrative agency determination invalidating or preventing enforcement of the prohibition against pass through of the tax to consumers (L 1980, ch 271, § 12, as amd by L 1980, ch 272, § 5), the liability for the tax shall be permitted to accrue until a final appellate determination [***8] of the challenge to the anti-pass-through provision, and the State is required to refund taxes collected for any period during which that provision is ultimately held invalid, to the extent that such taxes were not passed on to out-of-State consumers.

New York University v. Regan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


91 A.D.2d 751; 462 N.Y.S.2d 517; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19641



December 23, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner university filed an action pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 for the review of a determination of respondent Comptroller of the State of New York (comptroller), which certified that the university was liable to the state for abandoned property in the form of unpaid wages pursuant to N.Y. Aband. Prop. Law § 502(1).


OVERVIEW: The Bureau of Abandoned Property audited the university's books and records and concluded that unclaimed wages were abandoned property payable to the state. When the university failed to pay a hearing officer was appointed. The hearing officer issued a certification that the university was liable for the amount of the unclaimed wages. The university then commenced a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 to annul the certification. The comptroller filed a motion to dismiss the proceeding as premature and the court held that the motion should be granted. The court found that at the time the hearing officer was designated N.Y. Aband. Prop. Law § 1412(4) provided for an aggrieved person to initiate a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 following certification by the comptroller of the amount due as abandoned property. N.Y. Aband. Prop. Law § 1412(4) was thereafter amended to allow such a proceeding after a person designated by the comptroller certified the amount due. The court held that the proceeding was premature as the controlling time was the date when the hearing officer was designated and accordingly the certification was required to be issued by the comptroller.


OUTCOME: The court granted the comptroller's motion to dismiss the proceeding as premature.

Abrams v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


91 A.D.2d 618; 456 N.Y.S.2d 798; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19470



December 13, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant board of education sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York) in favor of respondents teachers that denied its motion to dismiss the teachers' petition seeking to compel the payment of suspended salary increases and recalculation of their retirement allowances.


OVERVIEW: The board of education argued in its motion to dismiss that the teachers did not have a right to maintain the instant action because the matter had allegedly been settled by agreement with the collective bargaining agent that represented the teachers before their retirement from service. The teachers argued that their financial interests were ignored in the settlement of deferred salary litigation because restitution was agreed to in the form of salary increases in a new union contract taking effect after they had retired. The trial court denied the motion to dismiss. On appeal, the court found that the trial court erred when it held that there was an issue of fact requiring trial as to what issues were intended to be settled by the stipulation. Nothing in the record contradicted the board of education's position that the stipulation of settlement covered both the salary increase and the pension computation issues and, therefore, there was no issue of fact requiring a trial. Further, there was no legal basis for recomputation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of the teachers.

 

875 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Gilbert v. Blum


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


90 A.D.2d 288; 457 N.Y.S.2d 625; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 18837



December 9, 1982 



DISPOSITION: Determination confirmed, and petition dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner recipient sought relief from an order by the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which ordered a review, after entering judgment, of respondent commissioner's discontinuance of the recipient's welfare aid and benefits.


OVERVIEW: The agency discovered that the recipient gained income through theft. She did not disclose the income as required, and the agency sent her notice of intent to discontinue public assistance because of an inability to determine financial eligibility. She requested a hearing, but did not confer with the agency. The case was closed and benefits discontinued. At the hearing, the commissioner affirmed the agency's actions. The recipient alleged that she only refused to discuss stolen funds, and the discontinuance violated her constitutional rights. The lower court entered judgment, but ordered a review. The court affirmed and found that the statutory scheme to allocate limited welfare resources required full disclosure of the recipient's financial status to determine eligibility for assistance. The scheme was not criminal, and was constitutionally permissible to achieve the regulatory objectives. While the recipient had a constitutional right to refuse to disclose self-incriminating information, the receipt of welfare benefits was not a fundamental right, and her failure to fulfill the reporting mandates of the agency, for whatever reason, made discontinuance of her benefits appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision to discontinue the recipient's grant of aid and benefits, and dismissed her petition without costs.


HEADNOTES

Social Services -- Discontinuance of Public Assistance

Discontinuance of Aid to Dependent Children benefits after the recipient exercised her privilege against self incrimination when she resisted discussing a check for $ 800, which may have linked her to criminal activity, did not represent an unconstitutional penalty, since the reporting mandate imposed upon recipients of public assistance (18 NYCRR 351.1) is noncriminal in nature and must be complied with if the system is to function; the local agency made no effort to coerce the recipient into informing against herself, but merely sought compliance by a welfare recipient who carries the burden of establishing eligibility in accordance with a minimal self-reporting requirement.  [***2] Moreover, the determination that petitioners were ineligible for public assistance during the period following their request for a fair hearing is supported by substantial evidence.

Board of Education v. Ambach


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


90 A.D.2d 227; 458 N.Y.S.2d 680; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 18831



December 9, 1982 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, entered August 5, 1981, modified, on the law, by declaring that the requirements of 8 NYCRR 103.2 are not improper or violative of the statutory and constitutional rights of handicapped students and reversing so much thereof as awarded judgment in favor of petitioners, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.

Judgment, entered November 25, 1981, modified, on the law and the facts, by directing that the guardian ad litem recover his attorney's fees from petitioner Northport-East Northport Union Free School District, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commissioner ordered the revocation of diplomas for students who failed a competency test under N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 8, § 103.2. Petitioner district filed a petition under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 to permanently enjoin enforcement of the order. The Supreme Court of Albany County (New York) granted the petition and attorney's fees for appellant guardian ad litem. The commissioner and guardian ad litem appealed.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner ordered that diplomas of students who failed a competency test under N.Y. Comp. Code R. & Regs. tit. 8, § 103.2 would be revoked. The district filed a petition under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 to permanently enjoin enforcement of the order. The trial court granted the petition and taxed attorney's fees for the guardian ad litem against the commissioner. The commissioner and guardian ad litem appealed. The court held that N.Y. Comp. Code R. & Regs. tit. 8, § 103.2 did not (1) violate § 504 of the Rehabilitation Act of 1973 because the students were not qualified handicapped individuals, (2) deny the students' rights to a free public education, (3) violate property and liberty interests under the Fourteenth Amendment because the students had a mental defect that prevented them from passing the competency test and the commissioner did not make any public statement about the students, or (4) violate the Equal Protection Clause because the competency test was reasonably related to the state's interest in the protection of the diploma's integrity. The court held that the attorney's fees should be taxed against the district because the district improperly issued the diplomas.


OUTCOME: The judgment that permanently enjoined the commissioner's order was reversed. The award of attorney's fees was modified to direct that the guardian ad litem recover attorney's fees from the district.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- High School Diploma

1. The [***2] adoption of regulations with respect to graduation requirements, including basic competency examinations, to establish a standard that would make a local high school diploma in New York a meaningful credential of the graduate is within the authority and power of respondent Commissioner of Education of the State of New York; control and management of educational affairs are vested in the Board of Regents and the Commissioner of Education, and determinations of the commissioner, unless patently violative of statutory or constitutional mandate, are beyond the range of judicial oversight.

Schools -- Education of Handicapped Children

2. Discrimination against a handicapped person in violation of section 794 of title 29 of the United States Code arises only when the benefits of a Federally funded program are denied to an individual who is able to meet a program's requirements in spite of his handicap. Accordingly, denial of a local high school diploma to petitioners, functionally handicapped students who failed to meet the requirement of 8 NYCRR 103.2 that all students pass basic competency tests in order to graduate from high school with a diploma, does not violate their rights [***3] under section 794; neither student completed the basic competency tests and, therefore, neither of them is an "otherwise qualified handicapped individual" within the meaning of the statute.

Schools -- Education of Handicapped Children

3. Petitioners, functionally handicapped students who were provided with individualized education programs commensurate with their educational needs, were not denied their right to a "free appropriate public education" (US Code, tit 20, § 1412, subd [1]) when they were denied local high school diplomas based on their failure to pass basic competency tests, successful completion of which is a prerequisite for graduation for all students (8 NYCRR 103.2).

Schools -- Education of Handicapped Children

4. The promulgation of regulations is a legislative act and, for the purposes of actions under section 1983 of title 42 of the United States Code, respondents, the Commissioner and the Deputy Commissioner of Education of the State of New York, have absolute immunity in actions pursuant to section 1983 to recover damages suffered as a result of a regulation promulgated by respondents which is allegedly in violation of Federal statute [***4] (8 NYCRR 103.2); furthermore, as government officials performing discretionary functions, respondents acted as administrative officers in acting to enforce said regulation and are shielded from liability for civil damages to those affected thereby by a qualified immunity as long as their conduct did not violate clearly established statutory or constitutional rights of which a reasonable person would have been aware.

Schools -- Education of Handicapped Children

5. A regulation promulgated by respondent Commissioner of Education of the State of New York requiring high school students to pass basic competency tests in language, arts and mathematics, which regulation became effective upon three years' notice, does not violate the due process rights of petitioners, functionally handicapped students who are not capable of satisfying said minimum achievement requirements (8 NYCRR 103.2); petitioners do not have a protected liberty interest in a diploma and the prior practice of petitioner board of education of awarding diplomas to students who only successfully completed individualized education programs was inconsistent with extant regulations and failed to create a legitimate [***5] expectation that could rise to the level of a property right in a diploma for unqualified students in successive classes. Furthermore, the basic competency tests at issue, which are achievement tests structured so that the average ninth grade student can answer 80% of the questions correctly, are not invalid with respect to content.

Schools -- Education of Handicapped Children

6. The policy of requiring all recipients of a local high school diploma to successfully complete basic competency tests which many handicapped students are not capable of passing (8 NYCRR 103.2) is not State action that intentionally discriminates against a class of persons grouped together by accidents of birth or living so as to invoke the so-called "middle tier" test enunciated in Alevy v Downstate Med. Center of N. Y. (39 NY2d 326). Accordingly, said requirement does not violate the equal protection rights of petitioners, functionally handicapped students who are not able to pass such tests and will be denied high school diplomas; since the protection of the integrity of a high school diploma is both a legitimate State interest and one to which the competency testing program is reasonably [***6] related, petitioners have failed to demonstrate the absence of a rational basis for said regulation.

Costs -- Additional Allowances

7. A judgment denying a motion by petitioner board of education for a discretionary allowance pursuant to CPLR 8303 (subd [a], par 2) and additional disbursement for expert witness fees pursuant to CPLR 8301 (subd [a], par 12) is affirmed; further, counsel fees awarded to the guardian ad litem appointed to represent two functionally handicapped students in the proceeding should be assessed against petitioner, since it was the improper act of petitioner in awarding diplomas to unqualified handicapped students that prompted the administrative action taken by respondents to revoke such diplomas which became the subject of the instant proceeding.

 

877 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Escobar


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


90 A.D.2d 322; 456 N.Y.S.2d 766; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 18843



December 7, 1982 



DISPOSITION: Order, Supreme Court, Bronx County, entered on July 9, 1982, unanimously modified, on the law and the facts, to delete the release provision as noted in the opinion herein, and as modified, affirmed, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of New York sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York), which denied its application for a retention order continuing defendant in the care and custody of the Office of Mental Health.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found not responsible with respect to criminal charges due to mental disease or defect and placed in a psychiatric center, which was a maximum security facility. The lower court later found defendant was no longer suffering from mental illness or a dangerous mental disorder and ordered that he be released. The state appealed. The court modified the order and held that a patient could not be confined indefinitely as dangerous on the basis of a single, isolated, violent act. The court held that whether defendant was still seriously mentally ill at the time of the release order was a matter of medical conjecture. The court held that the order of conditions be modified to provide that a court hearing had to be held with respect to defendant's ultimate release. At the hearing, the court held the lower court had to consider, in the interests of defendant and the community, long-term treatment measures to insure that defendant would not regress into the substance abuse that had been his nemesis.


OUTCOME: The lower court's decision was modified to delete the release provision, a new hearing was ordered, and as modified, the decision was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Insanity -- Postcommitment Proceedings

1. In enacting the Insanity Defense Reform Act of 1980, it was not the intention of the Legislature to convert the postcommitment proceedings contained in CPL 330.20, which provides for procedures following verdict or plea of not responsible by reason of mental disease or defect, from civil to criminal; rather, the purpose for the revision was to conform the proceedings to those for involuntary civil commitments, and, accordingly, at both the commitment and release [***2] hearings, due process requires imposition on the People of a "clear and convincing" standard of proof.

Crimes -- Insanity -- Postcommitment Proceedings

2. A release order entered pursuant to CPL 330.20 transferring the respondent, who was arrested for unlawfully entering his victim's apartment and stabbing his sleeping victim and who was found not criminally responsible due to mental disease or defect, from a maximum security psychiatric facility to a community-based treatment program, which order incorporated an order of conditions pursuant to which respondent was to continue to participate in the program until it was agreed between respondent and the program's director that respondent no longer required the services offered, should be modified; although a patient cannot be confined indefinitely as dangerous on the basis of a single, isolated, violent act, and whether respondent was still seriously mentally ill at the time of the release order was a matter of medical conjecture, the order of conditions is modified, in order to provide a greater societal protection in light of the dangerous propensity demonstrated by respondent, to provide that a court hearing be held with [***3] respect to respondent's ultimate release and that the Commissioner of Mental Health be made a party to that hearing.

 

878 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Horoshko


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


90 A.D.2d 850; 456 N.Y.S.2d 99; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19117



November 29, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, distributees of a deceased owner of property, sought review of the decision of the order of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which, in an action to declare a local law and an urban renewal map unconstitutional, had stayed the trial of a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. Em. Dom. Proc. Law § 207 to review respondent town's determination to acquire property by condemnation.


OVERVIEW: The distributees sought to declare the local law and the map unconstitutional because they rendered their property substandard and thus subject to acquisition by eminent domain. On review, the court found that the town's exercise of its power of eminent domain rationally served its valid public purpose of promoting proper development of town land through the elimination of substandard lots. The court found that the distributees' action did not impede the town from exercising its power of eminent domain, although the issue whether the property had any resale value after the local law was enacted could be relevant to valuation after the condemnation. The court dismissed the appeal.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed as moot the distributees' appeal of the town's determination.

 

879 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Allies Boulevard Bookstore, Inc. v. Cohen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


90 A.D.2d 935; 457 N.Y.S.2d 913; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19214



November 24, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bookstore challenged a decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which dismissed its request to annul a determination of respondents, Planning Commission and the Zoning Board of Appeals of the City of Binghamton. The bookstore obtained a certificate of occupancy and compliance to operate a retail business in a roadside business zone, but it objected to requirements imposed under Binghampton, N.Y., Code § 703.


OVERVIEW: The bookstore was required to submit a development plan to the Commission for prior approval, and it was also prohibited from use of signs on one side of its building. In compliance, the bookstore submitted a development plan for the Commission's prior approval, but it did so under protest. The bookstore maintained that its building was previously used and continued to be used in a "retail business" category. Respondents, however, had found that the bookstore had also added "peep" shows in the building, so they reclassified the building as an indoor recreational facility and indoor theater, and they imposed the conditions to buffer the property from an adjoining residential district. The court upheld the reclassification of the building's use and the restrictions on signs on one side, finding the requirements were not arbitrary, capricious, or unreasonable. The court also ruled that while the bookstore's claim of unconstitutional vagueness of § 703 was not properly presented, the claim was without merit. The court held that the disputed requirements were rationally based under § 703 and that the record supported the zoning determinations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the supreme court's judgment.

 

880 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Aurora Corp. of Illinois v. Tully


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


90 A.D.2d 939; 457 N.Y.S.2d 907; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19218



November 24, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which dismissed the taxpayer's application to review a determination of respondent New York State Tax Commission that held the taxpayer owed a New York State license fee pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 181(1).


OVERVIEW: The assessment in question had been assessed upon a change in the taxpayer's capital structure from shares of par value stock to nonpar value stock. The taxpayer limited its appeal to the argument that the applicable statute, N.Y. Tax Law § 181, was unconstitutional as being violative of state and federal equal protection guarantees, and of the Commerce Clause. Specifically, the taxpayer challenged the constitutionality of a law which imposed a tax rate on the nonpar value shares of foreign corporations which was one cent higher than the rate imposed on domestic corporations. In the absence of legislative history conclusively bearing on the one cent differential between taxation of nonpar value shares of domestic and foreign corporations, the court accepted respondents' stated purpose for the statute. The court thus held that the challenged classification was sustainable as being rationally related to the state's legitimate interest in placing foreign corporations on an equal footing, tax wise, with domestic corporations. In affirming, the court concluded that in light of its equal protection analysis, it was unable to find that the license fee violated the Commerce Clause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the taxpayer's application for review.

 

881 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Phillips


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


90 A.D.2d 900; 456 N.Y.S.2d 887; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19172



November 18, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the County Court of Rensselaer County (New York), which was entered after a jury found her guilty of the crimes of consensual sodomy, N.Y. Penal Law § 130.38, and endangering the welfare of a child.


OVERVIEW: The state conceded that defendant's conviction for consensual sodomy had to be reversed, because a court of appeals' decision declared § 130.38 unconstitutional. Defendant argued that her conviction for endangering the welfare of a child should also be reversed based on two erroneous charges. In the charge concerning defendant's culpability for the acts of her codefendant, the trial court charged that a person was criminally liable for an offense not only when he directly committed it, but when he was present at "or" aided in its commission. The court found that although the trial court inadvertently used the disjunctive, rather than the conjunctive, the charge fairly and adequately described the state's burden in proving defendant's culpability for the acts of her codefendant when read as a whole. At the request of defendant's counsel, the trial court submitted to the jury, as a question of fact, the issue of whether the testimony of one of the state's witnesses should be treated as unsworn. The court concluded that the record established that the witness was competent to testify and that defendant was not prejudiced by the trial court's submission of this issue to the jury.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by reversing that portion that convicted defendant of the crime of consensual sodomy and affirmed that portion convicting defendant of endangering the welfare of a child.

 

882 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


State Div. of Human Rights ex rel. Geraci v. New York State Dep't of Correctional Services


Proceeding Nos. 1, 2


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


90 A.D.2d 51; 456 N.Y.S.2d 63; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 18809



November 8, 1982 



DISPOSITION: Proceeding by the State Division of Human Rights pursuant to section 298 of the Executive Law, inter alia, to enforce its determination dated June 28, 1978, on behalf of claimant Santo Geraci, and motion by the New York State Department of Audit and Control and the New York State Department of Civil Service to intervene and vacate an order of this court dated December 1, 1980 granting enforcement of an order of the Commissioner of Human Rights dated April 25, 1980.

Application denied, without costs or disbursements, and motion granted only to the extent that intervention is sought and otherwise denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state agency sought to enforce a determination by petitioner New York Commissioner of Human Rights on behalf of a claimant against respondent state employer. A motion was filed by state agency to intervene and to vacate an order of the court that granted enforcement of an order of petitioner New York Commissioner of Human Rights against respondent state employer.


OVERVIEW: Respondent state employer was found by petitioner state agency to have unlawfully discriminated against a complainant in dismissing him from his position due to his age. The second matter was brought to the court pursuant to an order of default that was issued by the court on a complainant's behalf, against respondent state employer that petitioner state agency sought to have enforced. Intervenor state comptroller sought leave to intervene and set aside the order because petitioner state agency threatened to initiate contempt proceedings for noncompliance. The court held that as to the first matter, there was no lawful predicate for petitioner commissioner's order. As to the second matter, the court permitted intervenors to proceed. The court held, however, that there was no jurisdictional infirmity or practical difficulty in intervenors' obeying the order, and therefore, denied the request to vacate the order.


OUTCOME: The court denied the application, without costs or disbursements, for lack of basis in law supporting the award. That part of the motion by petitioner state agency to vacate the court's prior enforcement order was denied because there was no jurisdictional infirmity or practical difficulty in obeisance of the order.


HEADNOTES

State -- Claim against State -- Violation  [***2] of Human Rights Law

1. A money award against the State of New York or one of its agencies can constitutionally be made by the Commissioner of Human Rights for unlawful discrimination in violation of the Human Rights Law (Executive Law, art 15; § 297); furthermore, such an award may be enforced by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in a CPLR article 78 proceeding in the nature of mandamus rather than in the Court of Claims, since the State's liability has already been established by law and this is not the sort of unresolved claim against which the doctrine of sovereign immunity can properly be raised by the State.

Civil Rights -- Commissioner of Human Rights -- Judicial Enforcement of Award

2. A CPLR article 78 proceeding by the Commissioner of Human Rights to enforce an unappealed award against respondent State Parks and Recreation Commission representing back pay with interest and an award for mental anguish suffered as a result of respondent's refusal to hire complainant on the basis of religion, is not time barred under the provision of section 298 of the Executive Law that a "proceeding under this section when instituted by any complainant, respondent [***3] or other person aggrieved" be commenced within 30 days of service of the commissioner's order; this language refers to a proceeding for judicial review of an order by the commissioner and does not apply to the instant proceeding for judicial enforcement of such an order, which only the commissioner is authorized to bring.

Civil Rights -- Commissioner of Human Rights -- Judicial Enforcement of Award

3. The issue of whether there is substantial evidence to support an award by the Commissioner of Human Rights against the Department of Correctional Services for discrimination in employment was not waived by the department's failure to take an administrative appeal or to seek judicial review of the award; the issue must be resolved by the Appellate Division on an application to enforce the award under section 298 of the Executive Law using the same degree of scrutiny that would have been exercised on an application for judicial review. Furthermore, testimony by complainant correction officer, who was dismissed because his age at the time of his appointment exceeded the maximum age of 37 fixed by the department, constituted substantial evidence supporting an award of compensation [***4] for mental anguish, where he stated that he was 45 when he was dismissed, that his age was known to his superiors, that he had resigned from a private sector position to rejoin the civil service at their urging and that he was the sole support of his wife and six children.

Civil Rights -- Discrimination Based on Age -- Maximum Age for Correction Officer Candidates

4. In a proceeding pursuant to CPLR article 78 brought in the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court, an application by the Commissioner of Human Rights to enforce an award against the Department of Correctional Services for unlawful discrimination in employment based on age in dismissing complainant from his position as a correction officer because his age exceeded the maximum age of 37 fixed by the department for applicants for appointment, is denied (Executive Law, § 297-a, subd 7, par a), since the Court of Appeals found a similar age limit valid in a case decided while this matter was pending before the State Division of Human Rights and the Human Rights Appeal Board; a reviewing court is required to apply the law as it exists at the time of its decision, absent a vesting of rights not present here, and [***5] the fact that the Comptroller has paid a portion of the sum directed to be paid by the commissioner's order does not constitute a waiver of the error insofar as the remainder of the order now before the court is sought to be enforced.

Parties -- Intervention

5. In a CPLR article 78 proceeding in the nature of mandamus, brought in the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court to enforce an unappealed award by the Commissioner of Human Rights on behalf of complainant against respondent State Parks and Recreation Commission representing lost earnings, with interest, and mental anguish suffered as a result of respondent's refusal to hire complainant on the basis of his religion, the Comptroller and the Department of Civil Service are granted permission to intervene with respect to issues they raise as to the validity of the award and the propriety of its enforcement in the Appellate Division.

Civil Rights -- Discrimination in Employment -- Award

6. An award by the Commissioner of Human Rights against respondent State Parks and Recreation Commission for unlawful discrimination against complainant based on religion, representing earnings he lost when he was denied a [***6] noncompetitive summer position, was properly made; there is no reasonable probability that complainant would not have been certified for the position, and the Civil Service Department does not suggest any grounds for disqualifying complainant. Accordingly, the award purportedly representing back pay less ordinary payroll deductions was legally an award for a violation of complainant's civil rights, requiring no discount in the amount of lost wages to reflect the contingency of nonappointment.

Civil Rights -- Discrimination in Employment -- Award

7. An award of compensatory damages pursuant to section 297 of the Executive Law to a person aggrieved by discrimination on the part of the State or one of its agencies in employment may include compensation for mental anguish; furthermore, an award of interest on "back pay" awards to aggrieved civil servants is also proper.

Parties -- Necessary Parties

8. The Comptroller need not be joined as a party in administrative proceedings before the Commissioner of Human Rights against the State or one of its agencies to recover for alleged discrimination against an employee; the Comptroller's role in paying such an award would [***7] be purely a ministerial one of auditor under the New York Constitution (art V, § 1) and the State Finance Law ( § 109, subd 4).

 

883 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


New York State Coalition of Public Employers v. New York State Dep't of Labor


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


89 A.D.2d 283; 456 N.Y.S.2d 465; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 18376



November 4, 1982 



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified, on the law, by adding thereto a provision that record-keeping regulations must be issued pursuant to the commissioner's general power to issue regulations and in accordance with rule-making procedures contained in section 29 of the Labor Law, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner public employers and respondent department of labor cross appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County, New York, which, in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding, declared that the promulgation by the department's commissioner of certain rules was valid under N.Y. Lab. Law § 27-a(d)(4)(a), and that the incorporation by reference of the federal standards in N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 12, pt. 800 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The public employers requested from the department the full text of its proposed regulations. Copies of 29 C.F.R. pt. 1910, 1926, the Occupational Safety and Health Act (OSHA) regulations, were supplied. The department refused further clarification, and the public employers commenced an action seeking to have their request granted, to have N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 12, pt. 800 annulled, to have the text of all promulgated rules filed with the secretary of state, to have the commissioner adopt only those OSHA standards necessary to protect public employees, and to have any record-keeping regulations annulled. The supreme court held that (1) all federal OSHA regulations were to be adopted by the commissioner due to the statutory mandate of N.Y. Lab. Law § 27-a; (2) the entire text of the regulations must be filed; (3) the Freedom of Information Law request would become moot by the filing; and (4) because a rule-making proceeding had been initiated with regard to record-keeping regulations, a determination of their enforceability was unripe for judicial review. On appeal, the court affirmed, after modifying the judgment to allow a provision for record-keeping regulations.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by adding thereto a provision that record-keeping regulations must be issued pursuant to the commissioner's general power to issue regulations and in accordance with rule-making procedures of the Labor Law, and, as so modified, affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Labor -- Public Employees' Occupational Safety and Health Standards

The Industrial Commissioner of the Department of Labor, acting under the [***2] mandate of section 27-a (subd 4, par a) of the Labor Law that the commissioner "adopt all safety and health standards promulgated under the United States Occupational Safety and Health Act of 1970 [OSHA, US Code, tit 29, § 651 et seq.] * * * in order to provide reasonable and adequate protection to the lives, safety and health of public employees", validly adopted all OSHA standards in promulgating 12 NYCRR Part 800, although many of said standards do not apply to public employees and could have been redacted; however, to the extent that said standards were incorporated by reference in 12 NYCRR 800.3, they were improperly adopted in violation of section 8 of article IV of the New York Constitution, which requires that the text of such departmental regulations be filed and published. Furthermore, the commissioner's adoption of record-keeping regulations was not authorized by subdivision 4 of section 27-a of the Labor Law, which speaks only in terms of standards; the record-keeping regulations must be issued pursuant to the commissioner's general power to issue regulations (Labor Law, § 21, subd 11) and in accordance with rule-making procedures contained in section 29 of the [***3] Labor Law.

 

884 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Axelrod v. Branche


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


90 A.D.2d 862; 456 N.Y.S.2d 847; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19135



November 4, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant landowners appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which granted plaintiff health commissioner summary judgment in his action under N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1116 to recover a penalty from the landowners. The trial court also enforced an order for the landowners to construct water supply and sewage disposal facilities upon four lots of their residential subdivision.


OVERVIEW: The landowners subdivided their tract of land into 10 parcels that they subsequently sold. The health department determined that the landowners violated § 1116 by subdividing the tract and selling the parcels without first obtaining approval from the department of a plan or map showing methods for obtaining and furnishing adequate water supply and sewage facilities for the parcels. In an administrative hearing, the landowners were assessed a penalty and were ordered to install water and sewage facilities. The commissioner brought an action to enforce the prior order. The trial court granted the commissioner's motion for summary judgment, and the court affirmed. The department had jurisdiction over the action because the parcels constituted a subdivision under N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1115 where they were along an existing highway for sale as residential lots. The landowners could not rely on a possible retroactive application of a definitional amendment to § 1115, which exempted plots of more than five acres from consideration in applying N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1116, because they alleged that only one of their parcels was over five acres in size.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment for the commissioner.

Durante v. New Paltz Zoning Bd. of Appeals


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


90 A.D.2d 866; 456 N.Y.S.2d 485; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19138



November 4, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of the judgment from the Supreme Court at Special Term, Ulster County (New York), which dismissed the owner's application, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, to compel respondent zoning board to issue a special use permit.


OVERVIEW: The owner's land was zoned A-1.5 for agricultural/residential use. He applied for a special use permit pursuant to Town of New Paltz, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance § 3(A), to establish a bar and restaurant, vacation resort and riding academy on his premises. At public hearings, property owners in the community expressed vehement opposition. The board unanimously denied the permit. The special term sustained the board's determination and dismissed the petition. In the meantime, the board placed a one-year moratorium on applications for and the issuance of special use permits in A-1.5 zones. Following the denial of the property owner's motion to reargue/renew, he appealed. The court held that the it could not conclude that the board had acted in an arbitrary or capricious manner, finding that two public hearings were held at which specific examples of standards in the ordinance were considered repeatedly. The court noted that the premises were on a heavily traveled secondary country road that was already the scene of several vehicular accidents including fatalities and that other bars in the town had produced an influx of strangers, late night disturbances and traffic hazards.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment dismissing the owner's application to compel the zoning board to issue him a special use permit.

 

886 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Metropolitan Package Store Ass'n v. Koch


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


89 A.D.2d 317; 457 N.Y.S.2d 481; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 18382; 1982-83 Trade Cas. (CCH) P65,108



November 4, 1982 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff and plaintiff-intervenor liquor retailers, wholesalers, and retail trade association appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court of Albany County (New York) in their suit against defendant City of New York and others, seeking in part to enjoin collection of the tax under City of New York, N.Y., Local Law No. 30 (1980). The judgment, inter alia, granted a summary judgment to defendants and declared Local Law No. 30 constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Liquor retailers, wholesalers, and a trade association brought suit against the City of New York and others challenging the constitutionality of New York City, N.Y., Local Law No. 30 (1980), which was enacted under N.Y. Tax Law § 445 and which imposed excise taxes on beer and liquor that was sold or used in the city. It was alleged, inter alia, that Local Law No. 30 violated the U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The lower court, inter alia, granted the city a summary judgment, declared Local Law No. 30 constitutional, and refused to enjoin the enforcement of it. In affirming the judgment, the court held, inter alia, that where Local Law No. 30 was enacted solely as an exercise of the city's taxing power and had no purpose other than to raise revenue, the due process clause was not violated as that clause was applicable to a tax statute only if the statute was so arbitrary as to compel the conclusion that it was not an exercise of the taxing power. Further, the terms of Local Law No. 30 and its reference to state tax law were clear and understandable to persons of common intelligence, and the city's interpretation of it conformed with the common usage and meaning applied by state tax law.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed, which, inter alia, granted a summary judgment to the city and others, in the suit by liquor retailers, wholesalers, and a trade association against the city and others. The local law, which imposed an excise tax on beer and liquor sold or used in the city, was declared constitutional and an injunction, enjoining enforcement of the law, was denied.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Law of the Case

1. The court's prior decision in Metropolitan Package Store Assn. v Koch (80 AD2d 940), which reversed that portion of an order granting plaintiffs a preliminary injunction enjoining the administration and enforcement of Local Law No. 30 of 1980 of the City of New York, but affirmed the denial of the city's motion to dismiss the complaint holding: "we find the allegations of the complaint sufficient to withstand such a motion", does not constitute law of the case so as to entitle plaintiffs in the instant action to summary judgment on their constitutional attacks upon Local Law No. 30; Special Term, having made no determination on the merits [***2] in plaintiffs' favor, was not obligated by the court's prior decision to render judgment in plaintiffs' favor.

Constitutional Law -- New York City Excise Tax on Beer and Liquor -- Import-Export Clause

2. Local Law No. 30 of 1980 of the City of New York, which imposes an excise tax upon distributors and noncommercial importers of beer and alcohol which are sold or used in the city (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y46-2.0), does not violate the import-export clause (US Const, art I, § 10, cl 2), which bans only imports or duties on imports or exports. The subject tax is imposed in a nondiscriminatory fashion upon importers and persons acquiring alcoholic beverages or producing them and is effective only upon the sale or use of such beverages in New York City after they have come to rest in the city, a purely local event; moreover, the tax is not on the act of transporting or shipping beer and liquor into the State, but upon the sale or distribution after they have come to rest in the city.

Constitutional Law -- New York City Excise Tax on Beer and Liquor -- Commerce Clause

3. Local Law No. 30 of 1980 of the City of New York, which imposes an excise [***3] tax upon distributors and noncommercial importers of beer and alcohol which are sold or used in the city (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y46-2.0) is not violative of the commerce clause (US Const, art I, § 8, cl 3). The excise tax imposed has virtually an exclusive nexus with New York City and does not unfairly discriminate against plaintiff liquor dealers and distributors in the city; nor is the free flow of goods between States seriously affected, because the tax applies only after the goods come to rest in the city.

Constitutional Law -- New York City Excise Tax on Beer and Liquor -- Due Process of Law

4. Local Law No. 30 of 1980 of the City of New York, which imposes an excise tax upon distributors and noncommercial importers of beer and alcohol which are sold or used in the city (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y46-2.0) violates neither the 14th Amendment to the United States Constitution nor section 6 of article I of the New York Constitution on the grounds that it is too indefinite, vague and unclear, and, as implemented, constitutes a taking of property without due process. The terms of the law and its references to the Tax Law are clear [***4] and understandable to persons of common intelligence; nor have retailers been denied due process of law because the local law does not classify them as "distributors"; defendants' interpretation of the word "import" in the definition of "distributor" refers to transactions in foreign commerce not intrastate commerce, and is neither arbitrary nor unreasonable.

Constitutional Law -- New York City Excise Tax on Beer and Liquor -- Equal Protection of Laws

5. Local Law No. 30 of 1980 of the City of New York, which imposes an excise tax upon distributors who import into the city any alcoholic beverages for sale or use in the city (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y46-2.0), and does not distinguish between retailers and wholesalers, is not violative of equal protection; the tax is imposed upon those who import alcoholic beverages for sale or use in the City of New York and since it is solely intended to produce optimum revenue, it is a valid legislative enactment.

Constitutional Law -- New York City Excise Tax on Beer and Liquor -- Incorporation of Law by Reference

6. Local Law No. 30 of 1980 of the City of New York, which imposes an excise tax upon distributors [***5] and noncommercial importers of beer and alcohol which are sold or used in the city (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y46-2.0) and section 445 of the Tax Law, the enabling legislation, are not in violation of a provision of the State Constitution against incorporation of sections of other statutes by reference (NY Const, art III, § 22); this section of the Constitution was originally intended to apply to annual recurring taxes known at the time of its adoption, imposed generally upon the entire property in the State, and does not apply to all local taxes. Nor does the local law violate section 16 of article III of the State Constitution prohibiting incorporation of statutes by reference without verbatim insertion of such act; although the enabling act and the local law both incorporate by reference other sections of the Tax Law dealing with administration and collection, reference to the procedure of an earlier general statute does not contravene this provision; moreover, the enabling act was an amendment to article 18 of the Tax Law and as such was not a violation of section 16 of article III.

Constitutional Law -- New York City Excise Tax on Beer and Liquor --  [***6] Supremacy Clause

7. Local Law No. 30 of 1980 of the City of New York, which imposes an excise tax upon distributors and noncommercial importers of beer and alcohol which are sold or used in the city (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y46-2.0) does not constitute price fixing per se in violation of the Sherman Antitrust Act (US Code, tit 15, § 1 et seq.) and the supremacy clause (US Const, art VI, cl 2); the subject laws and regulations fall within the "State action" exemption to the Sherman Act and thus do not violate the act nor, by implication, the supremacy clause; the exemption applies since the excise tax was specifically authorized by State laws and enforced by State and municipal officials acting as State agents.

Statutes -- Adoption of Inconsistent Laws

8. There is no inconsistency between Local Law No. 30 of 1980 of the City of New York, which imposes an excise tax upon distributors and noncommercial importers of beer and alcohol which are sold or used in the city (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y46-2.0), and section 101-b of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law which is violative of section 2 (subd [c], par [ii]) of article IX [***7] of the New York Constitution. Local Law No. 30 is solely revenue producing while section 101-b is purely regulatory; such statutes are to be harmonized whenever possible, and Local Law No. 30 and its enabling legislation, section 445 of the Tax Law, enacted long after section 101-b, should prevail.

Local Laws -- Referendum Requirement

9. The technical violation of section 39 of the New York City Charter, which requires approval at a public referendum of any enactment transferring powers vested in a municipal agency to a nonmunicipal agency, does not void Local Law No. 30 of 1980 of the City of New York, which imposes an excise tax upon distributors and noncommercial importers of beer and alcohol which are sold or used in the city (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y46-2.0). Although the power to collect excise taxes has been transferred to the State Tax Commission without referendum approval, the State, through the enabling act (Tax Law, § 445) authorizing Local Law No. 30 and section Y46-2.0 of the Administrative Code, has effectively superseded the referendum requirement provision of section 39.

Statutes -- Adoption of Inconsistent Laws

10. Any [***8] inconsistencies between Local Law No. 30 of 1980 of the City of New York, which imposes an excise tax upon distributors and noncommercial importers of beer and alcohol which are sold or used in the city (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y46-2.0) and section 171-a of the Tax Law, respecting disposition of the excise taxes, present no real conflict. The statutes relate to different taxes from different sources, and may easily be harmonized since proceeds from State taxes inure to the State and those from the city tax revert to the city.

Constitutional Law -- New York City Excise Tax on Beer and Liquor -- Transportation of Intoxicating Liquors

11. Local Law No. 30 of 1980 of the City of New York, which imposes an excise tax upon distributors and noncommercial importers of beer and alcohol which are sold or used in the city (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y46-2.0), does not offend the 21st Amendment to the United States Constitution, because there is no attempt made to regulate either the transportation or importation of liquor into the State; the imposition of an excise tax at the moment of sale or use of goods after they have come to rest in the city [***9] is within the sovereign power of the State, here, passed on to the city by the enabling act.
