915 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Futorian Corp. v. New York, Dep't of State-Division of Licensing Services


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


87 A.D.2d 967; 451 N.Y.S.2d 201; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16499



April 29, 1982 



DISPOSITION: Determination confirmed, and petition dismissed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant furniture manufacturer challenged a decision of the Division of Licensing Services (New York) and respondent Department of State (New York), which found that the manufacturer violated N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law §§ 383, 386, and imposed a $ 250 fine.


OVERVIEW: An inspection of three retail establishments, carried out by the Department revealed several hundred pieces of furniture lacking tags required by N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 386, which required that every piece of furniture had attached thereto a tag reciting certain information concerning the manufacturer as well as the contents of the manufactured furniture. Because the manufacturer had made 18 of those pieces, it was charged with violating § 386. A hearing was conducted at which one of the Department's senior bedding inspectors testified that he found no evidence of tags or remnants of tags on the furniture. The court held that the inspector's testimony afforded ample justification for concluding that the manufacturer violated § 386 by failing to attach the tags, or by attaching them insecurely.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the determination of the division and the Department, and dismissed the petition.

 

916 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. O'Neill


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


86 A.D.2d 213; 449 N.Y.S.2d 515; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15387



April 26, 1982 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County, rendered September 29, 1980, affirmed.

This case is remitted to the Supreme Court, Kings County, for further proceedings pursuant to CPL 460.50 (subd 5).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his sentence of one to three years ordered by the Supreme Court in Kings County (New York) for attempted robbery in the second degree under N.Y. Penal Law § 70.02(c). Defendant claimed that he should have been granted youthful offender status pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law 720.10(3)(i) because of mitigating circumstances.


OVERVIEW: At the age of 16, defendant and three others approached two victims. Defendant produced a gun and threatened the victims. One of defendant's friends took a medallion from one victim. Shortly thereafter, defendant and his friends fled the scene and were apprehended by the police. While in flight, the arresting officer saw defendant place the gun in a garbage can. The weapon was only a starter pistol, incapable of discharging a bullet. Defendant pled guilty to attempted robbery in the second degree under N.Y. Penal Law § 70.02(c) and was sentenced to one to three years. Defendant appealed, claiming that in view of his prior clean record and the fact that the gun could not discharge, he should have been afforded youthful offender treatment pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law 720.10(3)(i). The court held that the lower court did not abuse its discretion because the fact that the pistol could not discharge was not a mitigating circumstance bearing directly on how the crime was committed to have permitted the court to grant such status. The court explained that it was not a mitigating circumstance because N.Y. Penal Law § 70.02(c) merely required the display of what appeared to be a pistol.


OUTCOME: The court held that the lower court did not abuse its discretion in sentencing defendant to an indeterminate term for attempted robbery and refusing to grant youthful offender status. The court explained that the fact that that the pistol involved in the robbery could not discharge was not a mitigating circumstance bearing directly on how the crime was committed to have permitted the court to grant such status.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- Youthful Offenders

The fact alone that, in the commission of an armed felony, the defendant uses a starter pistol, incapable of discharging a bullet, does not provide the requisite "mitigating circumstances that bear directly upon the manner in which the crime was committed", so as to permit the court to consider a youth eligible for youthful offender treatment under CPLR 720.10 (subd 3, par [i]). The mitigating circumstances that bear directly upon the manner in which the crime was committed was intended by the Legislature to include facts which would tend to diminish the defendant's culpability and alleviate his guilt; it was never intended that an exception be created whereby every conviction of robbery by an "eligible youth" using a toy gun would automatically [***2] permit a sentencing court to grant youthful offender treatment.

 

917 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ardito


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


86 A.D.2d 144; 449 N.Y.S.2d 202; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15380



April 15, 1982 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, Supreme Court, Bronx County, rendered on October 1, 1980, reversed, on the law, and the indictment dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Supreme Court of New York, Bronx County, convicting defendant of criminal usury in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed defendant's conviction for criminal usury in the first degree. The court held that the trial court improperly admitted hearsay statements of an alleged co-conspirator against defendant with no prima facie showing of a conspiracy established without recourse to the challenged statements. A witness testified about statements made by an alleged conspirator, but the statements were not made when defendant was present, nor was there any other evidence establishing a conspiracy. Absent evidence of a conspiracy independent of the challenged statements, statements made by the alleged co-conspirator were not admissible against defendant. The court therefore reversed the conviction and dismissed the indictment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for criminal usury in the first degree, because the trial court improperly admitted hearsay statements of an alleged co-conspirator against defendant when there was no prima facie showing of a conspiracy independent of the challenged statements.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Evidence -- Declaration of Coconspirator

A declaration by a coconspirator during the course of and in furtherance of the conspiracy is admissible against another coconspirator, as an exception to the hearsay rule, and it is not necessary, in order to make such proof competent, that the conspiracy be charged in the indictment; however, this evidence may be admitted only upon a showing that a prima facie case of conspiracy has been established, which must be made without recourse to the declarations sought to be introduced. Accordingly, the judgment convicting defendant of criminal usury is reversed and the indictment dismissed, where the testimony of the People's sole witness established only that the witness borrowed money from and made payments to defendant's alleged accomplice and that defendant appeared at about [***2] the time of the witness' meetings with others to obtain funds on four occasions and also "happened to come up" at the conclusion of the witness' meeting with the alleged accomplice after the money changed hands; the out-of-court declarations of defendant's alleged coconspirator and accomplice that defendant was the source of some of the money and was receiving some of the interest thereon, made to the witness out of the presence of defendant, were never rendered admissible against defendant and, without these declarations, the prosecution must fail.

 

918 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Rochester v. Chiarella


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


86 A.D.2d 110; 449 N.Y.S.2d 112; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15373



April 12, 1982 



DISPOSITION: Appeal No. 1. -- Order unanimously modified and as modified affirmed, without costs, and matter remitted to Supreme Court, Monroe County, for further proceedings, in accordance with opinion by Doerr, J.

Appeal No. 2. -- Order unanimously reversed, without costs, and matter remitted to Supreme Court, Monroe [***2] County, for further proceedings in accordance with same opinion as in City of Rochester v Chiarella, Appeal No. 1, 86 AD2d 110, decided herewith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city filed a class action seeking an order establishing the rights of defendant taxpayers to a refund from an unconstitutional property tax. Defendant utilities appealed the order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Monroe County (New York) that certified the class action and dismissed the utilities' individual actions. The city and taxpayers appealed the order providing that nonprotesting taxpayers were entitled to a refund.


OVERVIEW: A property tax was declared unconstitutional. The city filed a class action, seeking a declaration of its rights and liabilities with respect to refunding the taxes paid. The class action was certified after it was subdivided into four subclasses, a law firm was appointed to represent the entire class, and several individual actions were dismissed or consolidated. The trial court determined that taxpayers who paid the tax without protest were legally entitled to refunds. The court reversed the order allowing nonprotesting taxpayers to recover a refund; formal written protest was necessary to receive a refund as there was no evidence that the tax was paid by such nonprotesting taxpayers under duress. On remand, the trial court was instructed to consider whether the nonprotesting taxpayers would be entitled to a refund under any other laws. The court found that a class action was properly certified but modified the four subclasses so that conflicting interests did not inhere in any single subclass. On remand, the trial court was directed to consider whether the legal representative could represent the class as a whole or whether separate counsel was required for each subclass.


OUTCOME: The order that certified the class action was modified to create different subclasses of taxpayers; on remand, the trial court had to appoint legal representation either for the general class or for each subclass. The order that awarded nonprotesting taxpayers a refund was reversed; on remand, the trial court was directed to determine the legality of the payment of the refund to such taxpayers in light of other laws.


HEADNOTES

Actions -- Class Actions

1. It was error to dismiss actions by plaintiffs, local public utilities, against defendant city for tax refunds for excess real property taxes they paid during a four-year period on the ground that another action was already pending between the parties ( CPLR 3211, subd [a], par 4), where plaintiffs' actions were commenced the same day that the city commenced a class action seeking declaratory relief establishing the rights and liabilities of all taxpayers in those years and a court order directing a method of payment to those entitled to a tax refund; the class action cannot be said to have preceded plaintiffs' actions and, accordingly, the proper procedure was for the court to join plaintiffs' actions with the class action, as it did with other actions that had been commenced prior to the class action.

Taxation -- Tax Refund Proceeding -- Payment under Protest

2. A taxpayer is entitled to a refund for the payment of illegal taxes only if he can show that the payment was made under protest [***3] or was otherwise involuntarily made and, accordingly, those taxpayers who did not protest the payment of real property taxes which were subsequently found to have been excessive, and were not subject to any immediate threat to the possession or use of the real property except that a lien was routinely created against the property by plaintiff city until the tax was paid, are not entitled as a matter of legal right to a tax refund for the years in question; the fact that there is a lien against the real property is not the kind of duress which makes the tax payment involuntary so as to excuse the lack of formal protest by the taxpayer. However, the issue of whether the city may nonetheless make tax refund payments to nonprotesters under the theory that such payments constitute equitable claims under subdivision 5 of section 20 of the General City Law is remitted to Special Term for determination.

Actions -- Class Actions -- Representation of Subclasses

3. In a class action commenced by a city for declaratory relief and an order directing a method of payment to those taxpayers found to be entitled to a tax refund for excess real property taxes paid over a four-year period,  [***4] the appointment of one law firm to represent the general class of all taxpayers who paid taxes during those years and the creation of a subclass with separate counsel to represent those taxpayers who paid taxes under protest and who object to nonprotesters receiving refunds, creates an inherent conflict of interest for the firm representing the general class, where only limited funds are available to pay claims, which may be compromised, as this may well set those who paid the taxes under protest and are legally entitled to a refund against and those who did not make the requisite formal protest. Accordingly, representation of the general class should be eliminated and a new subclass created consisting only of nonprotesters, and the subclass of protesters should be expanded to cover all protesters, not merely those who protest and also object to payment of nonprotesters.

 

919 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mangiapane


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


87 A.D.2d 851; 449 N.Y.S.2d 244; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16324



April 12, 1982 



DISPOSITION: Sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a sentence imposed by the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), upon his adjudication as a second felony offender.


OVERVIEW: The sentence on the predicate felony was imposed less than 10 years before the date of that defendant committed the second felony. Defendant asserted that the delay of almost six months between the date of his plea on the predicate felony and the date of sentence on the predicate felony invalidated the sentence on the predicate offence, which in turn invalidated his second felony offender status. However, the court found that defendant waived the delay in sentencing on the predicate felony at the time of his plea pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. § 472. The defendant also argued that N.Y. Penal Law § 70.06, as applied to him, was an ex post facto law and, therefore, unconstitutional. He argued that he received a heavier sentence as a second felony offender and that the predicate felony conviction, which served as the foundation therefor under the provisions of the challenged statute, was obtained prior to the enactment of the statute. The court rejected the theory that these circumstances rendered the statute an ex post facto law as applied to defendant because the increased punishment was inflicted for the second crime only and was not an additional penalty for the prior offense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the sentence imposed by the trial court.

People v. Carr


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


87 A.D.2d 969; 450 N.Y.S.2d 94; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16501



April 2, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence for first degree attempted murder pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law §§ 125.27(1)(a)(i), 125.27(1)(b), 110.00.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of attempted murder in the first degree under N.Y. Penal Law §§ 125.27(1)(a)(i), 125.27(1)(b), and 110.00. Defendant was sentenced to a minimum of 25 years and a maximum of life. On appeal, defendant contended that that the trial court's instruction to the jury that defendant was presumed to have intended the probable consequences of his act unconstitutionally shifted the burden of proof onto him and that the sentence was too harsh. Defendant also asserted that since the death penalty statute for murder in the first degree was unconstitutional, he could not constitutionally be convicted of attempting to commit a crime that did not exist. The court affirmed the conviction, but held that the sentence was excessive and accordingly reduced it to 15 years to life. The court concluded that the jury instructions were proper and did not violate U.S. Const. amend. XIV's requirement that a state prove every element of a criminal offense beyond a reasonable doubt. The court also found no merit to the contention that the unconstitutionality of the death penalty statute rendered the penalty provision for first degree attempted murder invalid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction, but held that the sentence was excessive and accordingly reduced it to 15 years to life. The court concluded that the jury instructions were proper and did not violate the requirement that a state prove every element of a criminal offense beyond a reasonable doubt.

 

921 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Consumer Protection Bd. v. Public Serv. Comm'n


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


85 A.D.2d 321; 449 N.Y.S.2d 65; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14977



March 18, 1982 



DISPOSITION: Determination confirmed, and petition dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, petitioner consumers requested review of a determination of respondent public service commission that upheld the legality of fuel adjustment clauses in the tariff of New York State electric utilities.


OVERVIEW: The fuel adjustment clauses in the tariff of each New York State electric utility permitted the utilities to use a formula to adjust their rates for electricity. The formula permitted the utilities to automatically pass through increases in the cost of the utilities' fuel to consumers. The consumers sought to annul a public service commission decision upholding the fuel adjustment clauses, claiming that the determination was illegal and unconstitutional. The court confirmed the determination and dismissed the petition. The court held that the public service commission had the authority to authorize the fuel adjustment clauses pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law §§ 66, 72, 72-a because the clauses survived the procedural requirements of N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law §§ 66(12), and the public service commission monitored the fuel adjustments on a continuing basis. In addition, the fuel adjustment clause was a fixed rule under which future rates were determined. It was not retroactive rate-making, since the utilities were not being permitted to recoup past earning deficiencies The court also held that the consumers did not have procedural rights to attack the rates beyond those granted by statute.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the public service commission's determination and dismissed the consumer's petition seeking to annul the fuel adjustment clauses in the electric utility tariffs.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding against Body or Officer -- Transfer to Appellate Division

1. Where the Supreme Court transfers a CPLR article 78 proceeding to the Appellate Division even though it involves only questions of law which should have been decided by Special Term, the Appellate Division may decide the issues as if they had properly come before that court if the record is sufficient to dispose of the issues raised.

Public Utilities -- Fuel Adjustment Clause

2. A fuel adjustment clause, contained in the tariff of each New York State utility and designed to reflect monthly increases above or decreases below the "base cost" for fuel used by the utility, is a legal rate-making device necessary to maintain the financial integrity of utility companies and to [***2] establish reasonable rates for consumers; such clauses do not do violence to the system of fixed rates since initial approval and any subsequent change are subject to the provisions requiring public notice and hearing as in any rate change and are thus within the statutory authority of the Public Service Commission in its determination of a just and reasonable rate. The fuel adjustment clause, which is a fixed rule under which future rates to be charged to the public are determined, is not retroactive rate-making, since the utilities are not being permitted to recoup past earning deficiencies, and inasmuch as consumers possess only the procedural rights given to them by statute, there is no unconstitutional deprivation of due process.

In re Jamie D.

People v. Felix


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


87 A.D.2d 529; 447 N.Y.S.2d 945; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15777



March 16, 1982

926 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Crouse v. First Trust Union Bank


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


86 A.D.2d 978; 448 N.Y.S.2d 329; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15686; 33 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. (Callaghan) 1170



February 26, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant bank sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Cattaraugus County (New York), which granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiff borrower in plaintiff's action to declare N.Y. U.C.C. Law §§ 9-503 and 9-504 unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant bank loaned money to plaintiff and took back a promissory note and a security agreement covering plaintiff's automobile. When plaintiff defaulted on the loan, defendant peaceably took the automobile to sell it, as authorized by the terms of the security agreement and by N.Y. U.C.C. Laws §§ 9-503 and 9-504. Plaintiff sued to declare the statutes unconstitutional. The trial court granted summary judgment for plaintiff, holding that the procedure deprived plaintiff of due process in violation of the New York Constitution. On defendant's appeal, the court reviewed the record and concluded that there was no state action depriving plaintiff of due process; rather, the statutes merely acknowledged previous lawful conduct under common law in chattel mortgage transactions where the chattel mortgagee had the right, upon default, to take possession of the chattel and sell it if he chose. Accordingly, the court granted summary judgment declaring N.Y. U.C.C. Laws §§ 9-503 and 9-504 constitutional to the extent they confirmed defendant's right to peaceably take and sell plaintiff's automobile without prior notice and hearing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, granted summary judgment declaring statutes constitutional to the extent they confirmed defendant's right to peaceably take and sell plaintiff's automobile without prior notice and hearing where no state action deprived plaintiff of due process because statutes merely acknowledged previous lawful conduct under common law, and dismissed plaintiff's complaint.

 

927 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Sherman v. Frazier


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


84 A.D.2d 401; 446 N.Y.S.2d 372; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14935



January 29, 1982 



DISPOSITION: In a proceeding pursuant to CPLR article 78 to (1) review a determination of the Two-Family Review Board of the Town of Babylon granting a special permit to convert a one-family home into a two-family home and (2) declare Local Law No. 9 of the Town of Babylon to be invalid and unconstitutional, the appeal is from a judgment of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County, entered September 29, 1980 which granted the petition, and decreed that the determination and Local Law No. 9 are void.

Judgment reversed, on the law, without costs or disbursements, and petition granted to the extent of annulling the determination, and that part of the proceeding which was to declare Local Law No. 9 of the Town of Babylon invalid and unconstitutional is converted into an action for a declaratory judgment and it is declared that [***2] Local Law No. 9 is valid. The matter is remanded to the Two-Family Review Board for further proceedings consistent herewith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Two-family Review Board of the town of Babylon (review board), sought review of an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Suffolk County (New York), which ruled in favor of respondent homeowner's neighbors in their suit to have the review board's special permit for intervenor homeowner allowing a zoning exception and a local law declared invalid.


OVERVIEW: The town enacted a local law which created a Two-family Review Board empowered to conduct public hearings and determine special permit applications by those who owned illegally converted two-family houses or who intended to undertake such a conversion. A homeowner applied for a special permit to convert his single-family home to two-family use. The review board approved the application and granted the permit. However, the homeowner's neighbors filed suit to declare the permit and a local law invalid, arguing that the local law violated N.Y. Town Law § 267 because it delegated powers to the review board instead of the zoning board of appeals and because it was not in accordance with a comprehensive plan. The trial court ruled in favor of the neighbors. On appeal, the court reversed, and held that the determination of the review board granting the special permit was valid. Also, the local law was valid because a town was able to adopt zoning regulations by virtue of its Municipal Home Rule Law powers, as well as those granted by town law. However, the court ruled that the review board's determination was annulled because there were no findings of fact.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court holding the special permit and local law void, and remanded the matter to the review board for a new determination accompanied by findings of fact.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

1. A local law enacted to deal with the problem of unauthorized conversions of one-family homes for two-family use which provided for selective grants of temporary permits for two-family homes and created a Two-Family Review Board empowered to conduct public hearings and determine special permit applications by those who owned illegally converted two-family houses or intended to undertake such conversion does not violate section 267 of the Town Law because it delegates powers to the Two-Family Review Board instead of to the Zoning Board of Appeals, since a 1976 amendment to section 10 of the Municipal Home Rule Law (see L 1976, ch 365) permits supersession or amendment of the Town Law with relation to the "property, affairs or government of the town" and to "other matters in relation to which and to the extent to which [the town] is authorized to adopt local laws"; nothing in section 267 of the Town Law can be read to preclude a town board from delegating special permit powers to a body it creates by ordinance.

 [***3] Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

2. A local law enacted to deal with the problem of unauthorized conversions of one-family homes for two-family use which provided for selective grants of temporary permits for two-family homes and created a Two-Family Review Board empowered to conduct public hearings and determine special permit applications by those who owned illegally converted two-family houses or intended to undertake such conversion does not violate section 263 of the Town Law, which requires consideration of the peculiar suitability of the zoning district for particular uses, with a view to encouraging the most appropriate use of land throughout the municipality; although a comprehensive plan requires that a zoning amendment should not conflict with the fundamental land use policies and development plans of the community, a contention that the local law does not accord with the town's comprehensive plan is baseless not only because it is a bald and unsupported conclusion, but because the town board's exercise of the supersession power under a 1976 amendment to section 10 of the Municipal Home Rule Law, which permits supersession or amendment of the Town Law with relation [***4] to the "property, affairs or government of the town" and to "other matters in relation to which and the extent to which [the town] is authorized to adopt local laws", overrides the mandate of section 263 of the Town Law.

Administrative Law -- Judicial Review

3. A determination of a town's Two-Family Review Board granting a special permit to convert a single-family home to two-family use is annulled and the matter remanded to the board for a new determination accompanied by findings of fact; when an agency fails to delineate the findings which provided the basis for its decision, proper judicial review is impossible.

Declaratory Judgments -- When Remedy Appropriate

4. Although a CPLR article 78 proceeding is not the proper vehicle by which to review legislative action, a court can treat the declaratory portion of the petition as an action for a declaratory judgment and dispose of the proceeding on the merits.

 

928 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Drelich v. Kenlyn Homes, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


86 A.D.2d 648; 446 N.Y.S.2d 408; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15191



January 29, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant homebuilder challenged an order from the Supreme Court of Suffolk County (New York), which denied the homebuilder's motion to strike plaintiff homeowner's action off the jury calendar pursuant to a clause in the building contract that expressly stated that the homeowner agreed to waive a jury trial.


OVERVIEW: A homeowner, whose house had been built by a homebuilder, asserted N.Y. C.P.L.R. 4544 against the homebuilder when he attempted to argue that the homeowner had expressly waived his right to a jury trial in the building contract in his dispute with the homebuilder. The trial court found that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 4544 applied to a contract for the construction and sale of a one-family dwelling. The court reversed and struck the matter off the jury trial docket, placing it on the non-jury trial docket. The court held that to extend N.Y. C.P.L.R. 4544 so as to make it applicable to a contract for the construction and sale of a one-family dwelling would require a strained reading of the express language of the statute and would require that such meaning be determined by implication. The court concluded that the homebuilding contract was not within the scope of a consumer transaction since it was not included in the express terms of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 4544.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's denial of the homebuilder's motion to strike the action off of its jury trial docket, and struck the matter off the jury trial docket, placing it on the non-jury trial docket.

 

929 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Vicki B.(Anonymous) v. David H. (Anonymous)


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


86 A.D.2d 659; 447 N.Y.S.2d 17; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15204



January 29, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father challenged the decision of the Family Court, Richmond County (New York), which entered an order finding him to be the father of petitioner mother's child. The father had made support payments more than two years after the child's birth; but the mother had not filed her petition within two years of the final support payment. The family court found that the statute of limitations, N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 517(a) was not applicable.


OVERVIEW: More than two years after a child was born, a man began giving the mother a weekly cash payment for the child's support. More than two years after the last payment, the mother filed a paternity action against him. N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 517(a), the statute of limitations, required a paternity action to be instituted within two years of the child's birth, unless paternity had been acknowledged by the father in writing or by furnishing support, or unless the mother was under 18. The family court held that the support payments were an acknowledgement of paternity. On appeal, the putative father argued that he could not be accountable for his acknowledgement because it occurred more than two years after the child's birth. The court explained that the statute could not be tolled after it had expired. However, the court analogized the father's support payments to payments on a debt after the contract limitations period had expired. The acknowledgement of the debt started the running of the limitations period anew. In this case, the mother's failure to file the petition within two years of the last payment meant that the statute had expired. The court reversed and dismissed the petition.


OUTCOME: The court held that the two year statute of limitations barred the mother's paternity action against the father. The court reversed and dismissed the mother's petition.

 

930 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Repka v. Meyers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


86 A.D.2d 770; 448 N.Y.S.2d 67; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15347



January 29, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent town board challenged a judgment of the Erie Supreme Court (New York), which annulled its determination denying petitioner property owner's application for rezoning and directed the board to legislate a change in the zoning ordinance. The owner's application included a claim that the existing legislation was unconstitutional, so the supreme court had converted the N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding into a declaratory judgment action.


OVERVIEW: The owner was a lawyer that sought to have his property rezoned from residential to restricted business in order to utilize a portion of the duplex for a law office. The court found that zoning classifications were clothed with a presumption of constitutional legitimacy. The court found that when a zoning regulation was attacked as confiscatory and unconstitutional, it had to be shown that by no permissible interpretation could the regulation as written, or in the instance applied, be justified as a reasonable exercise of the police power. The court found that the property owner had to show more than that the current zoning classification had caused a significant diminution in value, or that a substantially higher value could be obtained if an alternative use was permitted. The court found that the proper test was whether the owner could presently receive a reasonable return on his property, and that to succeed with a constitutional challenge, the owner had to establish that no reasonable return could be had from any permitted use. The court reversed and concluded that the owner had not shown that the ordinance as it presently affected his property was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of the owner and dismissed the owner's petition.

Van Hoesen v. Pennsylvania Millers Mut. Ins. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


86 A.D.2d 733; 447 N.Y.S.2d 47; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15291



January 28, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action pleading nine separate causes of action against several parties, plaintiff appealed from a decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York) that dismissed the complaint as to defendants insurer and company.


OVERVIEW: In an action pleading nine separate causes of action against several parties, the trial court dismissed the complaint as against the insurer and the company. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court decision. The court rejected plaintiff's contention that the existence of good faith negotiations estopped the interposing of the defense of the two-year contractual time limitation. The court also rejected plaintiff's contention that the contractual statute of limitations was unconstitutional. The court found that the supporting affidavit of plaintiff's attorney was conclusory and virtually devoid of evidentiary facts. The court noted that the mere fact that settlement negotiations were conducted did not warrant an estoppel, and because there was no indication that the insurer and the company intended to settle the matter, or that they had improperly lulled plaintiff into inactivity, they were not estopped from raising the two-year limitation provision.


OUTCOME: In an action pleading nine separate causes of action against several parties, the court affirmed a decision of the trial court that dismissed the complaint as to the insurer and the company.

 

933 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Kravetz v. Plenge


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


84 A.D.2d 422; 446 N.Y.S.2d 807; 1982 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14938



January 22, 1982 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court in Monroe County (New York), which found in favor of defendant developer in the property owners' suit challenging a zoning amendment which permitted the developer to develop a hotel.


OVERVIEW: The developer proposed to operate a hotel on property located in a neighborhood zoned as a medium-high density historic district. Zoning ordinances for the historic district were meant to promote the retention of existing structures but allowed for specially permitted uses. The property had a 112-unit apartment building which had remained vacant since its construction four years earlier. The planning commission and the preservation board recommended denial of the developer's application for a zoning amendment. The city council voted to adopt a zoning amendment allowing hotels in existing structures in the historic district. The property owners sought a declaratory judgment that the amendment was invalid and an injunction preventing the developer from using the property as a hotel. The court found in favor of the developer, holding that the property owners failed to overcome the strong presumption of constitutionality. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the amendment resulted from proper use of the zoning process. The amendment did not amount to spot zoning because it was adopted pursuant to a comprehensive plan which was for the welfare of the community.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order in favor of the developer in the property owners' suit challenging a zoning amendment.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Spot Zoning

1. Spot zoning is the singling out of a small parcel of land for a use classification totally different from that of the surrounding area, for the benefit of the owner of the property and to the detriment of other owners; where the enacting authority acts in accordance with a comprehensive plan, it will not be condemned as spot zoning, even though it singles out and affects but one lot or creates a small area in the center of a large zone devoted to a different use.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Comprehensive Plan

2. A comprehensive plan has as its underlying purpose the control of land uses for the benefits of the whole community based upon consideration of its problems and applying the enactment or a general policy to obtain a uniform result not enacted in a haphazard or piecemeal fashion, and in general, if a need is shown to exist for the betterment [***2] of the community and an enactment satisfies that need, such change may become part of a comprehensive and well-conceived plan; a determining factor in deciding whether zoning is part of a comprehensive plan is whether forethought has been given to the community's land use problems. Enactments to change zoning plans should be analyzed in terms of consistency and rationality, with the requirement they not conflict with the fundamental land use policies and development plans of the community, keeping in mind that a comprehensive plan must be flexible and subject to some changes.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Spot Zoning

3. An amendment of a zoning ordinance enacted for the promotion of medium to high density residential neighborhoods within an historic preservation district, which provided for the establishment of hotels in existing structures, is a valid exercise of the zoning power and is not an exercise of "spot zoning"; the facts that property values in the vicinity of a structure proposed to be used as a hotel would change and that the amendment was proposed by individuals with a financial interest in its outcome do not demonstrate how the amendment conflicts with [***3] an existing comprehensive plan. There was no showing that the amendment, which was adopted in strict terms and with care, granted defendants a discriminatory benefit at the expense and detriment of their neighbors, without public advantage or justification, and since the amendment restricts itself to existing buildings and greatly limits the allowable exterior changes, the historic character of the district will be preserved.

 

934 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Marcus v. Baron


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


84 A.D.2d 118; 445 N.Y.S.2d 587; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15829



December 31, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Rockland County, entered July 28, 1980, reversed, on the law, with costs, Local Law No. 3 of 1967 of the Town of Ramapo is declared valid and the proceedings are otherwise dismissed on the merits.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant town sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Rockland County (New York), which declared Ramapo, N.Y., Local Law No. 3 of 1967 to be invalid and void on the grounds that it was inconsistent with state law and violated the state constitution. Respondent citizens had challenged Local Law No. 3 as the basis for denial of their petition to incorporate a village within the town.


OVERVIEW: The citizens, wishing to incorporate as a village within the town's boundaries, filed their petition. The town supervisor determined that the petition did not meet the requirements of Local Law No. 3. The citizens challenged the determination and challenged the law, arguing that it was preempted by state law. The citizens prevailed and the town sought review. The court reversed, finding that the local law was validly enacted. The court rejected the citizens' argument that the state's Village Law preempted enactment of local laws governing incorporation of villages, finding that preemption was limited to situations in which the intent to occupy an area of legislation was clearly evident. The court found no indication in the state law that it was intended to preclude local laws. The court found further that where local law was not preempted, a local law would be upheld even if it differed from or added to state law so long as it was not inconsistent with state law, and held that Local Law No. 3 was a proper zoning technique, executed for legitimate purposes and a necessary concomitant to the town's authority to determine the lines along which local development would proceed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's ruling that the town's local law governing incorporation of villages within the town's boundaries was invalid and therefore violated the state constitution. The court held that the local law was validly enacted and that it was neither preempted by nor inconsistent with state law governing village incorporation.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Incorporation of Village

Respondent town, through its town board, had the power to adopt a local law requiring that any proposed incorporation of a village within the town borders be in the over-all public interest of the territory proposed to be incorporated, the remaining area of the town and any school, fire or other town improvement district situated wholly or partly within the territory to be incorporated, which local law was enacted in order to preserve, enhance and implement the comprehensive master plan that was developed by the town in response to rapid and dramatic increases in population within its borders [***2] (Local Laws, 1967, No. 3 of Town of Ramapo); there is no suggestion that the Legislature, in enacting article 2 of the Village Law, intended or contemplated that in passing upon such an application for incorporation, no consideration be given by the responsible town officials to the possible deleterious effects of incorporation upon town plans which are in accordance with and reflect broad State-wide and national policy in the field of longrange and comprehensive land use planning (Municipal Home Rule Law, § 10).

 

935 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Brady v. Ottaway Newspapers, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


84 A.D.2d 226; 445 N.Y.S.2d 786; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15836; 8 Media L. Rep. 1671



December 31, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, Orange County, dated August 13, 1980, affirmed insofar as appealed from, without costs or disbursements, without prejudice to renewal of the cross motions for summary judgment when discovery has been completed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In this libel action, a preliminary conflict arose regarding interrogatories served by the plaintiff individual police officer. The individual police officer and the defendant media entities both appealed a decision from the Supreme Court at Special Term, Orange County (New York), denying their motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The media entities contended that the individual police officer was not permitted to maintain a libel action because he could not have shown that the relevant editorial comments were "of and concerning" him given the size of the group to which the editorial referred. The media entities also contended that the statements were opinions that were privileged by the new opinion privilege. However, the appellate court rejected the media entities' contentions. Pertaining to the issue of individual application in group defamation, there was no absolute limit on size in New York. The editorial comments were capable of personal application. Also, as to the claim of privilege, accusations of criminal activity, even in the form of opinion, were not constitutionally protected.


OUTCOME: The trial court's decision denying the parties' motions for summary judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Libel and Slander -- Defamation of Group

It is the "intensity of suspicion" cast upon the unnamed, individual members of a defamed group, rather than the number of persons in the group, which determines whether a publication is actionable by a group member as one "of and concerning" him; the size of the group should be considered with other factors, including the definiteness of number and composition of the group, its degree of organization, the prominence of the group and the prominence of the individual within the group to determine if the requisite element of personal application to the plaintiff is present. Accordingly, where a newspaper editor voiced the opinion that the appointed commissioner system should be retained in the police department [***2] of a small city and, to support this position, cited the indictment of 18 of the officers on the force seven years earlier for various misdeeds, which led to the installation of the commissioner system, and stated that the entire department was then under a cloud and that it is "inconceivable to us that so much misconduct could have taken place without the guilty knowledge of the unindicted members of the department", plaintiff, 1 of 27 present members of the police force who were among the group of at least 53 officers that were not indicted during that period, is not barred from bringing a libel action against the newspaper owner and others responsible for the publication by virtue of the size of said group and, in light of the factual allegation of corruption made in the editorial, defendants are not protected from liability by an asserted constitutional privilege for statements of opinion.

 

936 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Harris


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


84 A.D.2d 63; 445 N.Y.S.2d 520; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15828; 31 A.L.R.4th 525



December 30, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the County Court, Westchester County, rendered March 20, 1981, as amended May 6, 1981, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Westchester County Court (New York), which convicted her of murder in the second degree and criminal possession of a weapon in the second and third degrees.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed her former lover in his home. She contended that, during a struggle to prevent her from committing suicide, the victim was accidentally shot and killed by a handgun she brought to his residence. The prosecution contended that defendant had entered the victim's home with the intent to kill him because he had recently broken up their 13-year relationship and was involved with another woman. After police arrived at the murder scene, defendant was Mirandized three times before she requested to call her attorney from the residence. During the phone call an officer overheard her say that she thought she had killed the victim. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in failing to suppress this testimony by the officer because it violated her constitutional right to counsel. The court agreed but ruled that in view of the entire record of her trial, there was no harmful error. The court stated that the officer's testimony, evidencing she was surprised by his death, tended to support her contention that she did not intend to kill him. Finally, the court held statutory law did not allow removal of a juror peremptorily after he had already been impaneled.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court convicting defendant of murder in the second degree and criminal possession of a weapon.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Challenge to Jury

1. Under the Criminal Procedure Law, a sworn juror may be challenged only for cause and not peremptorily (see CPL 270.15, 270.20). Accordingly, the trial court did not err in refusing to exclude a sworn juror from the jury after it became known that the prosecutor had played a role in the dismissal of an indictment against her daughter, since there was no indication in the record that the juror had knowledge of the prosecutor's role in the dismissal of the case so as to make the juror vulnerable to a dismissal for cause. Moreover, during the course of the trial, defense counsel himself objected to the substitution of an alternate juror so as to permit the subject juror, among others, to comply with previously made travel plans.

Crimes -- Fair Trial -- Inspection  [***2] of Premises

2. Defendant was not deprived of a fair trial when the court denied her application for an inspection of the premises, such application having been made some 60 days after her arraignment, so as to be not only less than prompt, but actually untimely and subject to summary denial (see CPL 240.80, 255.20, subd 1). Defendant suffered no prejudice, since there is no indication that defense experts were ever refused access to the residence where the shooting took place; nor is there evidence that any representative of the defense ever made a request to the police or to the District Attorney's office to preserve any particular items of evidence.

Crimes -- Fair Trial -- Rebuttal Testimony

3. Defendant was not deprived of a fair trial when the court received testimony in rebuttal from a witness who had overheard a telephone conversation between the victim and the defendant. The witness' testimony was properly received at this point since it did not tend to establish defendant's intent to kill but rather had a tendency to disprove the alternate state of mind proffered by the defendant at the trial, and was thus offered in denial of an affirmative fact which the [***3] defendant endeavored to prove.

Crimes -- Fair Trial -- Pretrial Publicity

4. Defendant was not denied a fair trial by massive pretrial publicity intentionally generated by the prosecution, which allowed the press to gain ready access to the defendant's statements made on the night of the shooting. Pretrial publicity, even if pervasive and concentrated, does not necessarily lead to an unfair trial; here, none of the defendant's statements were suppressed and, therefore, the early access to them enjoyed by the press did not inure to the defendant's prejudice. Moreover, defense counsel himself participated in much of the media coverage of the case, and made no requests for a change of venue or a postponement of trial to permit public attention to subside.

Crimes -- Argument and Conduct of Counsel

5. The prosecutor's cross-examination of the defendant was not unfair, compelling defendant to characterize certain of the People's witnesses as liars and injecting prejudicial references to defendant's social standing. It was defendant herself who made such accusations and initiated the offending colloquy.

Crimes -- Fair Trial

6. Defendant was not deprived [***4] of a fair trial by the prosecutor's telephone call to an expert witness who had been retained by the defense, which call did not amount to an unconstitutional invasion of the councils of the defense; such conversations are permissible so long as the prosecutor does not suggest or imply that the witness is in any way obligated to converse with him.

Crimes -- Argument and Conduct of Counsel

7. The prosecutor's summation, wherein he attempted to discredit the defendant's firsthand account of the facts and suggested a different scenario based upon what he saw to be fair inferences to be drawn from evidence presented, did not exceed the bounds of proper advocacy; moreover, the jurors were repeatedly reminded that it was their recollection, rather than that of counsel, that was to control.

Crimes -- Argument and Conduct of Counsel

8. The prosecutor's reference to a wound in the victim's posterior shoulder as a "shot in the back" did not prejudice defendant, since there was never any real dispute over the location of the wounds and the bullet did in fact travel slightly from back to front.

Crimes -- Misconduct of Jurors

9. The prosecutor did not improperly [***5] invite the jury to perform tests in the jury room, defense counsel himself having made a similar request. In any event, even though the jurors apparently followed counsel's advice and attempted to re-enact the shooting during their deliberations in accordance with their own recollection of the testimony, there was no evidence of juror misconduct, such conduct constituting nothing more than an application of everyday perceptions and common sense to the issues presented in the trial.

Crimes -- Instructions -- Possession of Weapon

10. The court did not err in refusing to charge that a defendant can have two homes within the meaning of the weapons possession statute. The charge on the question of defendant's home did not state or imply that she could have only one home or request the jury to determine which of either of the two residences in question was defendant's home; moreover, the evidence clearly demonstrated that the victim's residence was not defendant's home within the meaning of the statute. Furthermore, any argument that the court's charge on the meaning of "home" was erroneous, inadequate or incomplete was not preserved for review.

Crimes -- Right to  [***6] Counsel -- Harmless and Prejudicial Error

11. The admission into evidence of defendant's statement over the telephone to her attorney, made after she received her Miranda warnings but in the presence of a police officer, to the effect that "Oh, my God, I think I've killed Hy", constituted a violation of her right to counsel. Once the right to counsel has attached, no statement which a suspect directs to his attorney may be reported at trial by a police officer, regardless of whether he intentionally or inadvertently overheard it. Nevertheless, the admission of the statement, although error, was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt, since it neither indicates nor suggests an intent to kill on the part of the defendant, but rather suggests the contrary since it reflects her surprise at the victim's condition and her uncertainty with respect to her responsibility for that condition.

 

937 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Nesbitt v. New York City Conciliation & Appeals Bd.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


85 A.D.2d 581; 445 N.Y.S.2d 457; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16374



December 29, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Conciliation and Appeals Board (CAB) as well as a landlord, sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which granted respondent tenants' petitions for renewal leases on rent stabilized terms. The tenants cross-appealed from the denial of their application for an award of reasonable attorneys' fees and expenses to be paid by the landlord.


OVERVIEW: The landlord operated a church and also owned and operated 60 residential apartments, two of which were occupied by the tenants. When each tenant commenced occupancy her apartment had become vacancy decontrolled. Upon the expiration of the term of each tenant's lease, renewal was granted upon increased rental. After successive renewals and increases, the tenants complained to the CAB of being charged excessive rents. The CAB upheld the landlord's contention that it was exempt from regulation by virtue of the ownership of the properties by a religious institution operated exclusively for charitable purposes on a nonprofit basis. The trial court annulled the determination and appellants sought review. The court held that the intent of the legislature appeared to provide exceptions to the rent stabilization laws for charitable or educational institutions. The court determined that CAB had a solid basis in reason for its determination. The court held that the claim for attorneys' fees, made pursuant to N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 234, was properly denied. The landlord's refusal to offer the tenants renewal leases on rent stabilized terms was not a failure to perform a covenant or agreement.


OUTCOME: The court unanimously modified, on the law, the judgment of the trial court by reversing the judgments insofar as it annulled the CAB's determination, confirmed the determinations, and dismissed the tenants petitions. As modified, the court affirmed.

939 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


New York v. Christiansen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


85 A.D.2d 663; 445 N.Y.S.2d 212; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16485



December 21, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In petitioner city's action seeking a tax exemption for a pumping station, the city appealed the order of the trial court (New York), which denied the city's motion for partial summary judgment exempting the pumping station from taxation.


OVERVIEW: The city brought an action which sought to declare its pumping station tax exempt. In its motion for partial summary judgment, the city sought an order granting exemption for its facility that drew water from the river, purified it, and deposited it in the aqueduct system by means of a pump. The trial court held that the term aqueduct was limited to the lines themselves, and denied the exemption. On appeal, the court reversed the decision of the trial court. The court held that in the context of the statute, the term aqueduct referred to the entire system of volume and pressure regulated delivery of water, including the system of collecting and pumping the water from its sources to its destination in the city. The court held that the definition of aqueduct included the pumping station, which should have been declared tax exempt.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which had denied the city's request for a tax exemption for one of its water pumping stations.

Crosby v. New York Workers' Compensation Bd.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


85 A.D.2d 810; 445 N.Y.S.2d 634; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16627



December 17, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff worker appealed from the Supreme Court, Tompkins County (New York) judgment which granted defendant State Workers' Compensation Board's motion for summary judgment and dismissed her complaint, which sought in a declaratory judgment action to have N.Y. Workers' Comp. Law § 24 declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Injured in an industrial accident, the worker initially received workers' compensation benefits. When benefits were discontinued she sought the services of a highly regarded local trial attorney. Aware of N.Y. Workers' Comp. Law § 24, regarding legal fees in workers' compensation cases, the attorney accepted a $ 300 retainer to be applied against any subsequent fee approved by the Workers' Compensation Board (board) and so informed the board. When the board indicated that the arrangement was illegal, the retainer was returned. The worker then sued for a judgment declaring the statute unconstitutional and enjoining its enforcement. She argued that it violated her right to privacy, her freedom to contract, and her right to equal protection. The board's motion for summary judgment was granted. The court affirmed. The worker's right to privacy was unaffected. The right to contract was not absolute. Abridging the right to freely contract with attorneys was within the reach of the state's police power. The statute was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified by adding a provision declaring the challenged statute constitutional, and was affirmed as modified, as there was a reasonable affinity between the legislative objective and the means used.

 

942 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Marthen v. Evans


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


83 A.D.2d 415; 445 N.Y.S.2d 329; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15503



December 11, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Reversed.

Order unanimously reversed, on the law, without costs, motion granted and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Chief Administrative Judge of the Courts of the State of New York (administrative judge) sought review of that part of an order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which denied its motion to dismiss a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 petition as to petitioner county judge. The county judge sought to review the actions of the administrative judge by which he was assigned to preside in other counties to alleviate a backlog of cases.


OVERVIEW: The administrative judge assigned the county judge to two terms of court outside his district as part of a larger transfer scheme to relieve calendar congestion. The county judge contended that his transfer was arbitrary and capricious because of the personal inconvenience it occasioned him and because of the disparity between his salary and that of similar judges in the counties to which he was assigned. The administrative judge unsuccessfully sought to dismiss the county judge's petition. On appeal, the court reversed. The court found that the county judge's petition failed to state grounds for relief based upon arbitrary or capricious administrative action. The court noted that prior to the unification of the court system, the salaries of county court judges were largely determined by the respective county legislatures. Although the disparities were rectified, the change had not taken place when the county judge was reassigned. The court concluded that due to the urgency surrounding the judicial backlog, the temporary continuation of salary disparity did not constitute a violation of the county judge's constitutional rights entitling him to retroactive pay of the difference.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and dismissed the county judge's petition.


HEADNOTES

Judges -- Temporary Assignment out of Home County -- Salary Disparities

The petition in a CPLR article 78 proceeding commenced by petitioner County Judge to review the actions of respondent Chief Administrative Judge of the State of New York by which petitioner was assigned to preside at terms of courts in counties outside his own county in 1980, wherein he alleges that to require him and others like him to perform judicial duties in counties other than those in which they reside for a salary significantly less than that earned by similarly situated Judges in the transferee counties is arbitrary and capricious and [***2] a violation of article 7-B of the Judiciary Law, and that the disparity in salary constitutes a denial of due process and equal protection in violation of both the State and Federal Constitutions, is dismissed; the Chief Administrative Judge's transfer order did not impose an illegal duty on petitioner, because respondent is empowered to transfer Judges (NY Const, art VI, § 26; Judiciary Law, § 212), and did not illegally deprive petitioner of salary due, as set under the former provisions of article 7-B of the Judiciary Law, and, in light of the urgency surrounding the court calendar backlog such transfers were intended to alleviate and the Legislature's original goal of remedying inequities in judicial compensation (L 1979, ch 55), the one-year continuation of the disparity complained of until the effective date of chapter 881 of the Laws of 1980, which eliminated the disparity challenged herein, did not constitute a violation of petitioner's constitutional rights entitling him to retroactive payment of the differential.

 

943 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Turf House, Inc. v. Hammond


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


83 A.D.2d 367; 445 N.Y.S.2d 296; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15142



December 10, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner hotel challenged the constitutionality of 1980 N.Y. Laws 693 (Act) and a local law that imposed the hotel occupancy tax in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding against respondents, County and County Finance Director, in the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York). The trial court granted respondents' motion for summary judgment and declared that the act and the local law were constitutional. The hotel appealed.


OVERVIEW: The hotel alleged that the Act and the local act that imposed the occupancy tax impermissibly delegated legislative powers to a private association in violation of N.Y. Const. art. III, § 1. The court held that the Act and the local law were constitutional and did not delegate legislative powers to a private association. Although the Act and the local law provided that the revenue derived from the tax would be expended in accordance with contracts between the county and the county convention bureau, the acts did not authorize a delegation of legislative power to the bureau. The terms of the contracts for the expenditure of the tax receipts were solely within the discretion of the county legislature. The convention bureau was an independent contractor that was entitled to compensation for services performed for the county. The Act and the local law merely required that the tax revenue be expended in accordance with the contracts, thus insuring public accountability for the expenditures.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order that granted the County and the County Finance Director's motion for summary judgment and that held that the act and the local law were constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Hotel Occupancy Tax

Chapter 693 of the Laws of 1980, entitled "An Act to enable the county of Albany to impose and collect taxes on occupancy of hotel or motel rooms in Albany county", and Local Law No. 3 of 1980 of Albany County, which imposed a 1% occupancy tax on persons renting hotel or motel rooms in that county to be allocated to pay for services performed subject to the terms and conditions contained in contracts which may be entered into between the county and the Albany County Convention and Visitors Bureau for the purpose of promoting convention, trade show and tourist business within the county, are constitutional; the act and the local law do not delegate legislative powers to a private association (NY Const, art III, § 1), but merely place the bureau, a not-for-profit corporation, in the role of an independent contractor, the terms of the contract between the county and bureau [***2] are not incorporated into said legislation in violation of sections 1 and 16 of article III of the New York Constitution, and adequate guidelines are provided therein for the expenditure of the tax revenues generated thereunder for the stated public purpose.

Raffone v. Islip


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


85 A.D.2d 597; 444 N.Y.S.2d 700; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16405



December 7, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer challenged an order of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which granted defendant town summary judgment and dismissed his action seeking a declaration that a resolution was unconstitutional and an injunction restraining the town from imposing taxes against taxpayers due to the resolution.


OVERVIEW: The town adopted a resolution that approved the establishment of an ambulance district. The taxpayer claimed that the resolution was unconstitutional because it included the imposition of an additional tax. The trial court dismissed the complaint and found that there was no duplicative taxation. The court held that the ambulance district was not a municipal entity apart from the town, and there was no impermissible double taxation. An ambulance district was an improvement district that was only a special administrative area and not a municipal corporation. The town board made findings of fact that certain areas of the town did not have an adequate quantity of ambulance service available to them. The town acted pursuant to its enabling legislation. The court found that it was an improper procedure to dismiss the complaint. Because the action was for a declaratory judgment, the rights of the parties should have been declared.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order modified by deleting the provision dismissing the taxpayer's complaint against the town in its entirety and substituting therefor a provision declaring that the resolution did not constitute a double taxation and otherwise dismissing the complaint. The court affirmed the order as so modified.

Hodes v. Axelrod


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


84 A.D.2d 895; 444 N.Y.S.2d 769; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16153



November 25, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner licensees instituted an action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 against respondent Commissioner of Health and others to review the determination of the commissioner that revoked the licensees' hospital operating certificate for a nursing home.


OVERVIEW: The licensees were convicted of grand larceny based on fictitious invoices and receipt of Medicaid funds for expenses not incurred for patient care. After the conviction the hearing held pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2806(5) determined that there was sufficient evidence to warrant revocation of the operating certificates. The acting director revoked the licenses. The licensees asserted that: revocation was precluded by the licensees' receipt of certificates of relief from disabilities and forfeitures, the acting director was not authorized to act in the commissioner's stead, and the licensees were subjected to an ex post facto law or deprived of their property without compensation. The court affirmed the revocation and determined that: (1) the receipt of certificates of relief from disabilities and forfeitures did not exempt them from a license revocation; (2) the acting director had the full authority of the commissioner; and (3) the loss of their property, if any, was the result of the license revocation which, itself, was not invalid as an application of an ex post facto law.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the determination made by the commissioner that revoked the licensees' hospital operating certificate for a nursing home.

Carmen v. West Hudson Hospital


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]

84 A.D.2d 875; 445 N.Y.S.2d 32; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16124

948 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Niagara Recycling, Inc. v. Niagara


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


83 A.D.2d 316; 443 N.Y.S.2d 939; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15135



November 13, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously modified and as modified, affirmed, without costs, in accordance with opinion by Hancock, Jr., J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed from an order denying their motion for a preliminary injunction pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6301, in a declaratory judgment action in which they attack the constitutionality of Niagara Town, N.Y., Local Law No. 8. Defendant cross-appealed from the denial of its motion for a preliminary injunction to compel compliance with Local Law No. 8 and from the court's dismissal pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a)(4) of its counterclaim.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs operated waste disposal facilities and were applying for additional permits to expand the facilities. After substantially completing the application process, including complying with the state environmental regulations, defendant town enacted Niagara Town, N.Y., Local Law No. 8. This law essentially established a complex regulatory mechanism under which the town board was given authority to grant or deny permits for facilities within the town for waste disposal and management operations. Plaintiff sought a preliminary injunction and declaratory judgment to keep the law from being applied to them. The court found that plaintiff might be able to show that, in view of the extensive protection already provided by state law and regulations, the town law so little benefited the public welfare that the imposition on plaintiffs of the burdens of compliance therewith could not be justified. Thus, the preliminary injunction was improperly denied. Further, the court concluded that the lower court properly rejected the argument that the local law was not beyond the authority of the town board to enact, and that it did not impose an undue burden on interstate commerce.


OUTCOME: The court found that it was error to deny plaintiffs' application for a preliminary injunction, that defendant's motion for an injunction to compel compliance was properly denied, and that the grant of plaintiffs' cross motion for dismissal of defendant's counter claim was proper. The order was unanimously modified and as modified, affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Injunctions -- Preliminary Injunction

1. In order to be entitled to a preliminary injunction, the moving party must demonstrate the likelihood of ultimate success on the merits, irreparable injury to him absent granting of the preliminary injunction and that a balancing of equities favors his position; on appeal from an order denying a motion for a preliminary injunction, the Appellate Division should not finally determine the merits of the action but should review only the determination of whether the exercise of discretion by Special Term has been abused.

Environmental Conservation -- Solid Waste Disposal Facilities -- Local Regulation

2. Special Term abused its discretion in denying the [***2] application by plaintiffs, operators of commercial waste disposal and management facilities, for a preliminary injunction restraining defendant Town of Niagara from enforcing a local law which gives the town board power coextensive with that of the State Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC) to grant or deny permits for facilities within the town for waste disposal and management operations, since there is a substantial likelihood that the local law will be found unconstitutional as violative of due process in its effect on plaintiffs' existing operations with respect to the power given the town board to compel a cessation of plaintiffs' businesses by denying their permit applications even though the DEC might have already issued permits based upon the identical data and to the extent that the local law could be applied so as to result in a denial of proposed additions for which lengthy and costly application procedures and hearings have already been completed; the public interest to be served by the local law is not sufficiently compelling to warrant the heavy burden it imposes on plaintiffs' existing and contemplated operations in view of the extensive protection already [***3] afforded by the comprehensive system of State law and regulations for solid and hazardous wastes ( ECL art 27, tits 7, 11; 6 NYCRR Parts 360, 617) and plaintiffs are not required to make their permit applications before attacking the validity of the law in view of the far-reaching and irreparable damage that could result.

Environmental Conservation -- Solid Waste Disposal Facilities -- Local Regulation

3. Although plaintiffs, the operators of commercial waste disposal and management facilities, are entitled to a preliminary injunction restraining defendant Town of Niagara from enforcing a local law which gives the town board power coextensive with that of the State Department of Environmental Conservation to grant or deny permits for waste disposal facilities operating within the town because there is substantial likelihood that the law could be unconstitutional in its effects on plaintiffs' operations, the local law is not invalid for being inconsistent with the State Solid Waste Management and Resource Recovery Facilities Act ( ECL art 27, tit 7) since local municipalities have the power under the act to adopt local laws pertaining to solid waste facilities that are "not [***4] inconsistent with" any provision in the State law ( ECL 27-0711) and, inasmuch as all of the measures of the local law are expressly stated to be in addition to those contained in the State law and its implementing regulations, the local law fits the specific definition of consistency established by ECL 27-0711; the local law does not impose an undue burden on or discriminate against interstate commerce since the law "regulates evenhandedly" without regard to the location of the customers or to the origin of the waste materials brought into the town.

[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]
