950 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Doe v. Buffalo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


84 A.D.2d 920; 446 N.Y.S.2d 652; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16183



November 13, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant City of Buffalo appealed a judgment of the Erie Supreme Court (New York), holding unconstitutional a Buffalo City Ordinance requiring all movie theatre operators to file a certificate of registration specifying the name and address of the owner of the building, the name and address of the owner of the business, and the names and addresses of the officers of the business.


OVERVIEW: A city ordinance required all movie theatre operators to file a certificate of registration. Failure to register would result in revocation of all other licenses issued to the business. An anonymous plaintiff commenced a declaratory judgment action seeking to have the ordinance declared unconstitutional. The court below granted the request, finding that the ordinance violated plaintiff's rights to be free from compelled disclosure, guaranteed by the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments. On appeal by defendant city the court reversed, holding that only when disclosure requirements were directed at a group inherently suspected of criminal activities would they be deemed to entail a substantial risk of self-incrimination. Theatre operators were not such a group. The court found that the ordinance was regulatory in nature, and it was not an unreasonable exercise of the police power for a city to desire the names and addresses of those operating businesses within its boundaries. Nor had the city singled out movie operators, as it had similar disclosure requirements applicable to various other businesses. Plaintiff's claim that the disclosure ordinance violated its rights was rejected.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment because theatre operators were not a group inherently suspected of criminal activities, and the disclosure ordinance did not violate plaintiff's rights against self-incrimination.

 

951 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bank of New York v. Tully


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


84 A.D.2d 704; 443 N.Y.S.2d 832; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15857



November 10, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the trustees and the bank, filed suits against defendants, the New York Department of Taxation and Finance and the State Tax Commissioner, seeking recovery of certain capital gains taxes paid through the retroactive application of N.Y. Tax Law § 618(4), the retroactive aspect of which was declared unconstitutional. The Supreme Court, New York County (New York) dismissed the actions. The trustees and the bank appealed.


OVERVIEW: The bank and the trustees filed suits against the New York Department of Taxation and Finance and the State Tax Commissioner to recover capital gains taxes paid through the retroactive application of N.Y. Tax Law § 618(4), the retroactive aspect of which was declared unconstitutional. The bank's suit, besides demanding a money judgment, sought in the alternative a declaration of the parties' rights. The remaining suits for the most part sought money judgments. The trial court dismissed the bank's suit with leave to file a claim in the court of claims and dismissed the remaining suits. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that the suits, whatever the variations in relief requested, were essentially suits to recover money from the state and as such were required to be brought in the court of claims. The court further found that even those actions which sought declaratory relief, in addition to or as an alternative to money damages, clearly did not present a genuine issue justifying the exercise of ancillary jurisdiction, in view of the obvious circumstance that the question raised had been definitively answered and was no longer a question requiring declaratory relief.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's orders dismissing the actions filed by the trustees and the bank against the New York Department of Taxation and Finance and the State Tax Commissioner to recover certain capital gains taxes paid through the retroactive application of the applicable statute, the retroactive aspect of which was declared unconstitutional.

 

952 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Esler v. Walters


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


83 A.D.2d 91; 444 N.Y.S.2d 961; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14758



November 5, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law, without costs, and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, including a town supervisor, sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which, in a proceeding filed by petitioner residents pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R 78, declared a special town election null and void.


OVERVIEW: A special election on the question of whether to consolidate two water districts was submitted to a public vote limited to owners of taxable property situated within the districts as assessed upon the last preceding town assessment role pursuant to N.Y. Town Law § 206(7). The residents were not owners of taxable property. The court reversed the order of the trial court. The court held that (1) the state court was obligated to follow a two-part test as applied by United States Supreme Court precedent rather than conflicting state precedent because the matter involved a federal question, the application of the Due Process Clause; (2) the issue submitted for vote was far removed from normal governmental activities because the water districts' sole power involved the distribution of water, and a town board exercised all control over the use of the water; and (3) the effect of the water districts' activities on property owners was disproportionately greater than on those who did not own property because the costs of the water districts were assessed against owners in proportion to the value of the land they owned and liens were imposed upon the land for nonpayment of assessments.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court and dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Limited Franchise in Special Election

A special election on the question of whether two water districts within respondent town should be consolidated, submitted to public vote by owners of taxable property within the districts pursuant to subdivision 7 of section 206 of the Town Law, is not unconstitutional under the due process clause (US Const, 14th Amdt, § 1), where the districts are financed by ad valorem taxes upon real property situated within the districts and by water rents, which debt, if unpaid, becomes a lien on the realty, and the districts' sole power involves the distribution of water; the principle of one person, one vote is inapplicable herein, since the issue submitted for vote is one which is removed from normal governmental activities and the effect of the water districts' activities on franchised property owners [***2] is disproportionately greater than on nonproperty owners and, further, there is a rational basis for the restricted franchise imposed, because the property owners in the water districts have a more permanent and enduring interest therein than the nonproperty owners have.

 

953 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Beam


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


83 A.D.2d 82; 444 N.Y.S.2d 300; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14756



October 29, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Broome County Court (New York), which convicted defendant of three counts of first-degree sodomy. Defendant argued it was legal error to introduce into evidence uncharged crimes on the issue of defendant's identity as perpetrator, and to admit testimony of a police officer regarding a witness's identification. Defendant also argued the People failed to prove forcible compulsion beyond a reasonable doubt.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was tried on various charges of sodomy. Defendant moved for a dismissal, alleging the People failed to prove forcible compulsion. The lower court denied defendant's motion, and entered a judgment of conviction against defendant. The appellate court affirmed defendant's convictions. The court found that the victim testified that after he initially refused to engage in consensual sodomy, defendant put an arm around his neck so that it was almost choking victim, and thus holding him, pushed him along a pathway under a bridge. The victim did not describe the manner of restraint under the bridge, and testified that he made no effort at resistance and did not attempt to leave until after defendant left. The court held that defendant's application of superior force at the outset of the encounter was sufficient to support the inference of an implicit threat of serious physical injury if complainant did not submit to the deviate intercourse. The court therefore found that the People proved forcible compulsion, as defined in N.Y. Penal Law § 130.00(8). The court also held that evidence of certain uncharged crimes, and testimony of police identification, was admissible.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed defendant's convictions of sodomy. The court held that defendant's application of superior force at the outset of the encounter was sufficient to support the inference of an implicit threat of serious physical injury if complainant did not submit to the deviate intercourse. The court also held that evidence of certain uncharged crimes, and testimony of police identification, was admissible.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sodomy -- Forcible Compulsion

Defendant's application of superior force at the outset of an encounter with complainant who had refused to engage in consensual sodomy is sufficient to support the inference of an implicit threat of serious physical injury if complainant did not submit to the deviate intercourse; accordingly, the People proved the element of forcible compulsion, as that term is defined in subdivision 8 of section 130.00 of the Penal Law, through the complainant's testimony as to the initial use of physical force and his testimony that he offered no great resistance because he knew he would have been hurt, and defendant's conviction of sodomy in the first degree (Penal Law, § 130.50, subd 1) is affirmed. Further, defendant has not established any unconstitutionality of the statutory definition of forcible compulsion, i.e., that force which is capable of overcoming "resistance of a type reasonably to be expected from a person who genuinely refuses [***2] to participate"; the statutory requirements do not create any unreasonable ambiguity as a standard of conduct.

 

954 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Delgaizo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


84 A.D.2d 646; 444 N.Y.S.2d 368; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15771



October 29, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty and then sought review of his conviction for the criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree by the County Court, Ulster County (New York). On appeal, defendant argued that the trial court should have granted his motion to suppress and that his arrest without a warrant was illegal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested as a result of the undercover activities of an informer. The informer allowed police officers to enter defendant's apartment. The apartment was searched and cocaine was found, along with money that had been marked by the police. To determine if the police complied with N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 140.15(4), the police had the burden of proving that they had a reasonable belief that a crime had just been committed in the apartment to justify their entry without a warrant. The court held that: (1) the precedent of recent case law was prospective and not applicable; (2) the facts showed probable cause to enter defendant's apartment but a general search of the apartment without a warrant was not lawful because once defendant was arrested, there was no further danger that evidence would have been lost or destroyed; (3) exigent circumstances were not shown; (4) denial of defendant's motion to suppress may have contributed to his pleading guilty so the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to suppress was not harmless; and (5) the evidence seized in the apartment, except for that taken from defendant's person and from his immediate vicinity, had to be suppressed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, vacated defendant's plea, and remitted the matter to the trial court.

 

955 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. Nyquist


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


83 A.D.2d 217; 443 N.Y.S.2d 843; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14777



October 26, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County, dated December 22, 1978, modified, on the law and the facts (1) by deleting the final three sentences from decretal paragraph 1(a) and substituting the following: "The system presently impinges upon the important right of education guaranteed to all children of this State by section 1 of article XI of the New [***2] York Constitution in the plaintiff and intervenor-plaintiff school districts. The system fails to further the asserted State interest of preserving local control over education in these districts. It also fails because there are methods of financing public school education which would further local control over education with less intrusion upon the right of education guaranteed to all children of the State. Accordingly, New York's public school finance system violates the equal protection clause of the New York State Constitution (art I, § 11)"; (2) by deleting from the penultimate sentence of decretal paragraph 1(c) the words "and irrational"; and (3) by deleting from the final sentences of decretal paragraphs 1(c), 1(d) and 1(e) the words "and of the Federal Constitution (the Fourteenth Amendment)" and substituting the following declaration: "New York's school financing system is constitutional under the Fourteenth Amendment to the Constitution of the United States." As so modified, judgment affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant school districts appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court in Nassau County (New York), which concluded that the school finance system violated the equal protection and education clauses of the state constitution and the federal equal protection clause. The trial court found constitutional the use of local districts and reliance on local taxation and permitted the system to continue until changes were made or an injunction issued.


OVERVIEW: The court agreed that New York method of financing public education was constitutionally defective. The court applied intermediate scrutiny to the system of education finance. The wealth-based system severely constrained the ability of local education to provide the educational offerings they deemed suitable for their pupils. The court departed from the trial court's determination that the Fourteenth Amendment was transgressed. The court held the palpable proof of clearly lesser educational opportunity based on wealth discrimination established violation of the equal protection provision of the state constitution. The court held that a judicial remedy to the unconstitutionality of the system could be fashioned. In the face of evidence demonstrating that large numbers of children emerged from the school system lacking even the minimal tools necessary to function in society and that the current financing scheme was in good measure a cause for the failures, the court concluded that the education article was violated by a method of financing which failed to establish a school system capable of providing an education for many educable children.


OUTCOME: The court modified the lower court's judgment by substituting language that the system impinged upon the constitutionally guaranteed right of education and failed to further the state interest of preserving local control over school districts. The court also modified the language of the order to reflect its holding that the Fourteenth Amendment was not violated. The court affirmed the modified judgment.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Standing -- Justiciability

1. Plaintiffs, various boards of education throughout the State of New York, have standing [***3] to challenge the State's current method for allocating resources to meet the constitutional mandate that it provide for "the maintenance and support of a system of free common schools, wherein all the children of this state may be educated" (NY Const, art XI, § 1) in a suit wherein they assert that said present financing method violates the equal protection clauses of the State and Federal Constitutions (NY Const, art I, § 11; US Const, 14th Amdt, § 1), and, in view of the expanding scope of standing, plaintiffs school children, represented by their parents, have similar standing; moreover, the controversy is susceptible of judicial resolution and, accordingly, the merits of plaintiffs' claims of constitutional infringement are properly reached by the court, which need not reorder fiscal priorities or interject itself into the day-to-day administration of the school system or educational policies to entertain the action.

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- Public School Finance System

2. New York's current public school finance system, whereunder the quality of the educational opportunity offered by any particular local school district is largely determined [***4] by the amount of taxable property in the district, violates the equal protection clause of the New York State Constitution (art I, § 11); the State has failed to meet its burden, under the "intermediate" or "heightened" level of State constitutional scrutiny, of demonstrating that the system, which presently impinges upon the important constitutional right of an education guaranteed to all of the children of this State by section 1 of article XI of the New York Constitution, furthers the asserted State interest of preserving local control over education in these districts, and it has also failed to show that the objective advanced by the classification cannot be achieved by a less intrusive method of financing the public school system.

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- Public School Finance System

3. New York's current public school finance system, whereunder the quality of educational opportunity offered by any particular local school district is largely determined by the amount of taxable property in the district, does not violate the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution; education is not a fundamental right under the Federal Constitution [***5] and, even assuming that some identifiable quantum of education is a constitutionally protected requisite of the exercise of the First Amendment or electoral franchise rights, both of which the Constitution does guarantee, evidence that under the existing system certain children have not been provided an education which meets basic minimum standards and evidence of a significant variation in educational opportunities provided by local districts, does not demonstrate that New York has deprived its young people of First Amendment rights and electoral franchise rights to such an extent that the Fourteenth Amendment is violated.

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection -- Public School Finance System

4. New York's current public school finance system, which creates vast variances among local school districts in the State in the quality of education provided to the children within those districts, violates the imperative of section 1 of article XI of the New York State Constitution that the State maintain and support a system of free common schools "wherein all the children of this state may be educated", since said method of financing fails to establish a school system capable [***6] of providing an education for many educable children; a mandate that the State of New York maintain a system to provide an education for the children of the State which is "thorough and efficient" or "ample" is implicit in the unembellished language of the education article.

In re Guardianship of M.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


83 A.D.2d 925; 443 N.Y.S.2d 214; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15379

Soc. Serv. Law § 384-b was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The agency sought to terminate the parental rights of a parent, and the court denied the petition. The court further found that § 384-b, which required the guardianship of a child to revert to the agency if the foster parents failed to institute adoption proceedings, was unconstitutional. The attorney general, as intervenor, sought reargument. The court granted reargument and modified the decision. 957 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Long Island Trust Co. v. Rosenberg


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


82 A.D.2d 591; 442 N.Y.S.2d 563; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11835



September 8, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Upon an appeal by permission, order of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court for the Ninth and Tenth Judicial Districts, dated November 14, 1979, affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bank sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, Appellate Division (New York), which reversed a trial court order and denied appellant's motion to punish respondent, decedent's employer, as a witness for civil contempt for failure to comply with an information restraint subpoena.


OVERVIEW: Appellant bank challenged an order from the lower court that denied its motion to punish respondent, decedent's employer, as a witness for civil contempt for failure to comply with an information restraint subpoena. The underlying dispute arose from a supplementary proceeding brought by appellant against defendant decedent debtor to enforce a default judgment. Decedent had conveyed the residence in question to third parties prior to death, and in an effort to acquire information concerning the conveyance, appellant served an information subpoena upon respondent. The subpoena was served by certified mail, return receipt requested. The court held that respondent was properly served and that its refusal to answer was punishable as a contempt but an independent special proceeding was necessary to invoke punishment and should have been commenced in another county. The court affirmed the order of the lower court because for the commencement of a special proceeding, service could not be obtained by ordinary mail service.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order from the lower court that denied appellant bank's motion to punish respondent, decedent's employer, for civil contempt because an independent special proceeding was necessary and service of such could not be obtained by ordinary mail service.


HEADNOTES

Contempt -- Civil Contempt -- Service of Process -- Nonparty Witness

Civil contempt proceedings cannot be instituted by service of a notice of motion by ordinary mail upon an alleged contemnor who is not a party to the underlying action in which the contempt is claimed to have been committed; recent amendments to sections 756 and 761 of the Judiciary Law have not altered the pre-existing requirement that civil contempt proceedings against a nonparty witness be commenced in the same fashion as other special proceedings against new parties, i.e., by service of notice [***2] of petition in the same manner as a summons in an action or, with the court's permission, by order to show cause ( CPLR 403, subds [c], [d]).

 

958 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re C.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


83 A.D.2d 932; 442 N.Y.S.2d 579; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15385



September 8, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant adoptive parents sought review of a decision of the Surrogate's Court, Nassau County (New York), which dismissed the adoptive parents' petition for the adoption of a child and declared N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111 unconstitutional as applied to appellee biological parents.


OVERVIEW: The biological mother consented to the adoption of her child by the adoptive parents; the biological father was largely absent before and after the child's birth and did not seek to stop the adoption until it was apparent that it would be successful. On appeal, the court reversed the order of the trial court, reinstated the adoption petition, and remitted the case. Under § 111(1)(e), the biological father's consent was not needed for the adoption to proceed. Preadoption consent was required under § 111(1)(e) for unwed fathers in certain circumstances. The consent requirement did not apply to the biological father because he (1) did not live openly with the biological mother or the child for a continuous six-month period before the adoption, (2) he did not hold himself out openly to be the father of the child, and (3) he did not pay a fair and reasonable sum for costs associated with the mother's support during her pregnancy or for the child's birth. The father failed to establish some semblance of a family unit evidenced by his failure to meet the requirements of § 111(1)(e), and his consent for the adoption of his child before the age of six months was not required.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court, reinstated the adoptive parents' petition to adopt the child, and remitted the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

In re D.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


83 A.D.2d 933; 442 N.Y.S.2d 575; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15386



September 8, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant adoptive parents appealed from an order of the Surrogate's Court, Nassau County (New York), which held N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111(1)(e) to be unconstitutional as applied to respondent natural father and adjudged that his consent to the adoption was required, vacated the previously given consent of respondent natural mother on the ground of duress, and dismissed the adoption proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Respondents filed a motion to set aside the consent of the natural mother in the proceedings to finalize adoption on the ground that it had been procured by coercion, duress, and fraud. While the matter was pending, N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111 was declared unconstitutional because it did not require the preadoption consent of the natural father. The natural father then filed a motion for summary judgment dismissing the adoption petition. Section 111 had been amended to require unwed fathers' consent in certain circumstances, therefore, an acting surrogate ruled in favor of respondents. Modifying the order, the court held that the natural mother's claim was contrary to the weight of the evidence. The court held that § 111(1)(e) was constitutional, but, because the natural father did not meet its criteria, the adoption could have gone forward without his consent; therefore, the proceeding was to continue.


OUTCOME: The court modified the surrogate court's order to hold that the statute was constitutional, that the natural mother's consent had been freely given, and that the adoption proceeding was to continue. As modified, the court affirmed.

 

960 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Rosenfeld v. Blum


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


82 A.D.2d 559; 442 N.Y.S.2d 89; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11390



August 24, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Petition granted to the extent that the determination of the State commissioner, dated July 6, 1979, is annulled, on the law, without costs or disbursements, and the matter is remitted to the State commissioner for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner aid recipient sought review of a determination of respondent Commissioner of the New York State Department of Social Services (agency), which affirmed a determination reducing her public assistance grant by the amount established by the Social Security Administration to be "in-kind" income under the Federal Supplemental Security Income program, and discontinuing recipient's food stamp authorization.


OVERVIEW: The recipient received both home relief and food stamps due to a disability. After the recipient qualified for Federal Supplemental Security Income (SSI), the agency informed her that her public assistance was being reduced and her food stamps were being discontinued. At the fair hearing requested by the recipient, the agency representative testified that the deduction of the in-kind income did not result from any investigation by the local agency but was the consequence of information conveyed to it by the Social Security Administration. The agency rejected the recipient's challenge to the use of in-kind income and stated that the termination of food stamps was mandated by passage of the Food Stamp Act of 1977. The recipient petitioned for review. The food stamp issue was resolved prior to the petition. With respect to the reduction of public assistance by an amount calculated by the Social Security Administration to be "in-kind" income, the record was short of evidence sufficient to sustain the agency's determination and the determination was annulled. The agency could not use the SSI figure as an administrative shortcut to a determination of state public assistance needs.


OUTCOME: The appellate court annulled the determination of the agency which reduced the amount of the recipient's public assistance based on findings of the Social Security Administration.


HEADNOTES

Social Services -- Public Assistance -- "In-kind" Income

1. The automatic deduction of certain "in-kind" income, as defined by the Social Security Administration for purposes of calculating a disabled petitioner's Federal Supplemental Security Income [***2] (SSI) benefits, from petitioner's State-funded public assistance grant (Social Services Law, § 157 et seq.), impermissibly attributes such income to petitioner, since there is nothing in the record to indicate how this figure, which is less than the amount that petitioner's relatives actually contribute toward her rent to supplement the maximum home relief shelter allowance in subject county, was determined, or to show that petitioner could dispose of that money to meet any of her other needs; neither the Social Services Law nor the regulations promulgated thereunder authorize the Commissioner of the New York State Department of Social Services to adopt the presumptions reflected in the Federal regulations, which reduce the flat SSI grant in a fixed amount to reflect a multi-person household, in making a determination of need under the State public assistance scheme, which purports to represent the extent to which income and resources actually available fail to sustain the statutory public assistance level (Social Services Law, § 131-a).

Social Services -- Termination of Food Stamp Authorization -- Notice

2. A determination by the Commissioner of Social Services,  [***3] following a fair hearing, to discontinue petitioner's food stamp authorization, which determination is based upon a reason not specified in the advance notice of said action given to petitioner public assistance recipient, must be annulled (18 NYCRR 358.8 [a]); petitioner is entitled to a new fair hearing upon proper notice concerning her entitlement to benefits under the food stamp program.

 

961 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Hasenbein v. Siebert


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


83 A.D.2d 875; 442 N.Y.S.2d 102; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15271



August 24, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the state superintendent of banks and others, sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court, Queens County (New York), which granted a motion for summary judgment by plaintiff, a former director of a savings and loan association, who alleged that N.Y. Banking Law § 397(2)(b)(3) was unconstitutional in that it violated his equal protection and due process rights and sought reinstatement as a director.


OVERVIEW: The former director was married to the niece of a director of the association and was himself a director until he was removed pursuant to a directive issued by the State of New York Banking Department on the ground that his election as a director of violated N.Y. Banking Law § 397(2)(b)(3), which made individuals related to a director of a savings and loan association ineligible to become directors themselves. He claimed that the provision was unconstitutional. The trial court ordered his reinstatement. Because the question was likely to recur even though the association had become a federally chartered institution, the court ruled that the provision was constitutional. Elimination of nepotism was a legitimate state purpose; the statute was not overbroad. A suspect classification was not involved and the classification at issue did not interfere with the right to marry or an individual's ability to carry on a profession. States had wide latitude in regulating their local economies under their police powers, and rational distinctions could be made with less than mathematical exactitude. The provision had a rational basis and was a valid exercise of the state's police power.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision, denied the former director's summary judgment motion, granted defendants' cross motion for summary judgment, and declared that the statute at issue was constitutional.

 

962 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Freeport Randall Co. v. Herman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


83 A.D.2d 812; 441 N.Y.S.2d 826; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15171



August 13, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent state agency appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which directed the state agency to hold a hearing on the issue of comparability of rents and which upheld the constitutionality of § 9(b) of the New York Emergency Tenant Protection Act (ETPA) in petitioner landlords' action that challenged the ETPA.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the ETPA, the village passed a resolution declaring a housing emergency. The county board established guidelines for fair-market rents for all apartments in the village subject to the ETPA. Thirteen tenants applied for and received adjustments of their initial legal regulated rents from the state agency. The landlords brought an action against the state agency to vacate the orders and for a declaratory judgment that § 9(b) of the ETPA and N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 2502.3 were unconstitutional. The trial court remanded for a hearing and upheld the constitutionality of the statute. On appeal, the court modified to strike the direction to remand for a hearing and otherwise affirmed. The state agency correctly applied the formula set forth in the ETPA for the determination of fair-market rents and refunds. No provision was made in the statute for a hearing such as requested by the landlords. Even assuming the ETPA impaired contracts, it was a valid exercise of police power that provided a reasonable alternative for the protection and general welfare of citizens under a declared housing emergency.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment that directed the state agency to hold a hearing on the issue of comparability of rents and that upheld the constitutionality of the ETPA in the landlords' action that challenged the ETPA. The court struck the direction to remand for a hearing and otherwise affirmed.

 

963 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Guardianship of M.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


82 A.D.2d 217; 443 N.Y.S.2d 214; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11345



July 23, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Orders, Family Court of the State of New York, New York County, entered on May 15, 1980, unanimously modified, on the law, to the extent of reversing that part of the orders which found certain sections of the Social Services Law unconstitutional and substituting therefore a finding that those sections are constitutional, and the orders are otherwise affirmed, without costs and without disbursements. The parental rights of Manuela M. are terminated.

Orders, Family Court of the State of New York, Bronx County, entered on November 15, 1979, unanimously modified, on the law and the facts, to the extent of reversing that part of the orders which [***2] found there was insufficient proof of respondent's mental illness and substituting therefore a finding that there is clear and convincing proof of mental illness and terminating parental rights, and the orders are otherwise affirmed, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant home challenged the order from the Family Court, New York County (New York), dismissing its petition to terminate respondent parent's parental rights and finding N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384-b(4)(c), (6)(a), to be unconstitutional. Appellant guardian challenged the order from the Family Court, Bronx County (New York), dismissing its petition to terminate respondent parent's parental rights and finding the same statute constitutional.


OVERVIEW: In separate actions, the parents challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 348-b, as it allowed for the termination of parental rights for mentally ill parents. The court rejected the parents' challenge. Section 384-b did not violate the Equal Protection Clause because it reasonably promoted the substantial state interest in the welfare of children, did not facially distinguish between mentally ill parents and all other parents, and fairly balanced the parties' interests. By focusing on conduct rather than status, § 384-b had not created an irrefutable presumption of parental incapacity for mental ill parents to care for their children. The parents' unconstitutional vagueness challenge failed because §348-b was sufficiently elastic to protect parental rights, but not so indefinite as to deprive parents of fair notice of the behavior affected. Section 384-b did not penalize status rather than conduct for purposes of substantive due process because it provided for a hearing, not automatic termination. The court held that substantial evidence supported the denial of parental rights to both parents.


OUTCOME: The court modified the orders of the family courts and directed the family courts to enter a finding that the statute was constitutional. The court reversed the family courts' orders on the issue of termination of parental rights, finding that both parents' parental rights should be terminated.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights

1. The provisions of section 384-b (subd 4, par [c]; subd 6, par [a]) of the Social Services Law, insofar as they deal with mental illness and the termination of parental rights, are constitutional. The statute (1) does not violate equal protection by treating mentally ill parents differently from other parents since the distinction is neither arbitrary nor invidious, but rather balances the interests of the parents, the children and the State by requiring a clear and convincing quantum of proof that the parent's mental illness precludes him from properly caring for his child; (2) does not violate procedural due process by running the risk of erroneously depriving a parent of his child inasmuch as it has a high standard of proof and an exacting test which must be met if parental rights are to be terminated; (3) is not unconstitutionally vague since [***3] it is not so indefinite as to deprive parents of fair notice of the behavior affected; and (4) does not violate substantive due process by penalizing status rather than conduct, inasmuch as it does not penalize a parent for being mentally ill, but rather terminates a mentally ill parent's parental rights only when the illness renders the parent unable to provide a child with the minimum requirements of care.

Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights

2. The parental rights of a mother should be terminated where there is clear and convincing proof of mental illness which renders her unable to provide adequate supervision and guidance to her children for the foreseeable future. (Social Services Law, § 384-b, subd 4, par [c]; subd 6, par [a].)

Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights

3. The parental rights of a father should be terminated where there is clear and convincing proof that he is presently and for the foreseeable future unable, by reason of mental illness, to provide adequate and proper care for his children. (Social Services Law, § 384-b, subd 4, par [c]; subd 6, par [a].)

Parties -- Standing

4. In order to [***4] establish standing, a party must demonstrate that he is possessed of rights which the statute infringes, and that he is within the class of persons affected by the statute; accordingly, a natural mother has standing to challenge the constitutionality of a statute where it appears that her right to raise her children is infringed by the statute, and that, as a mentally ill parent, she is within the class of persons affected by the statute. Furthermore, a natural father has standing to challenge the constitutionality of the statute where he had raised that issue during a fact-finding hearing to terminate his parental rights based on mental illness, notwithstanding that the petition against him was dismissed for insufficient proof, since the constitutional issue will be important if there is a successful challenge to the finding of insufficient proof.

Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights

5. The right of a parent to raise his child is one which enjoys strong constitutional protection; furthermore, the termination of parental rights is one of the most severe forms of deprivation that can be imposed upon a parent, and only the most countervailing interests can [***5] outweigh the rights abridged by termination.

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws

6. In order to ascertain whether a law violates equal protection standards, a court must examine the character of the classification, the individual interests affected, and the governmental interest involved.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law

7. Procedural due process is applied flexibly and calls for procedures appropriate to the situation; a court must examine the private interests affected, the risk of erroneous deprivation and the probable value of additional safeguards, and the governmental interest involved.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute

8. In order to avoid a finding of vagueness, a statute must provide fair notice of the proscribed behavior, set clear enough standards so that the statute will not be enforced in a subjective or arbitrary way, and not be so overbroad as to result in an inhibition of constitutionally protected activity.

Johnson v. Blum


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


83 A.D.2d 731; 442 N.Y.S.2d 618; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15045



July 23, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applicants challenged a judgment by the Supreme Court at Special Term, Chemung County (New York), which denied attorney's fees in favor of respondent Commissioner of the New York State Department of Social Services (commissioner), in the applicants' action challenging the constitutionality of an administrative directive. The commissioner cross-appealed the determination that the applicants' children were eligible for public assistance.


OVERVIEW: The applicants applied for public aid for their respective minor children and the local agency denied their applications because they refused to dispose of their cars as required by an administrative directive. The applicants challenged the constitutionality of the directive, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, in the trial court, which granted the applicants' petition and ordered that public assistance be furnished provided that the children otherwise qualified for assistance. On appeal the applicants challenged the denial of attorney's fees and the commissioner cross-appealed the decision to the extent that it determined that the applicants' children were eligible for public assistance. The court affirmed on both counts. The court held that judicial review was permissible before the applicants exhausted their administrative remedies because the hearing officer would have been required to follow the challenged directive and the delay would have caused irreparable harm to the children. The court determined that recipient children could not have their aid discontinued or reduced based on their parents' failure to dispose of a resource without first determining their lack of need.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment declaring the administrative directive unconstitutional and affirmed the denial of attorney's fees to the applicants, in their action challenging the commissioner's denial of their children's applications for public assistance.

 

965 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Holmes


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


82 A.D.2d 61; 442 N.Y.S.2d 256; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10943



July 16, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law, and a new trial ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed her conviction, rendered by the Supreme Court, Broome County (New York), entered upon a jury verdict, which convicted defendant of robbery in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, aided and abetted by another person, was charged with forcibly stealing property consisting of a wallet and its contents. The people served on defendant a demand that if defendant intended to offer an alibi defense, she must within eight days of service of such demand, serve upon the People "notice of alibi," reciting the names of every alibi witness upon whom she intended to rely, pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 250.20(1). The People contended that defendant's tardy service without court authorization relieved the People of its reciprocal duty of serving defendant with a list of the People's rebuttal witnesses. The trial court permitted the rebuttal testimony and refused defendant's application for an adjournment not in excess of three days, as provided by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 250.20(3)-(4). The jury found defendant guilty, and the trial court entered judgment on the verdict. The court concluded that the trial court erred in not granting defendant's requested adjournment. The court found that since proof of defendant's guilt was not overwhelming without the testimony of the rebuttal witnesses, the error of the trial court was not harmless.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment, entered upon a jury verdict, which convicted defendant of robbery in the second degree. The court ordered a new trial.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Alibi Witness

The trial court erred in not granting an adjournment not exceeding three days when the People called its first alibi rebuttal witness and the defendant made an application for such an adjournment as permitted by statute ( CPL 250.20, subds 3, 4), notwithstanding a late filing of the notice of alibi by defendant and the lack of authorization therefor ( CPL 250.20, subd 1); furthermore, the error of the trial court was not harmless since without the testimony of the People's rebuttal witnesses, proof of defendant's guilt was not overwhelming in the face of the defendant's alibi witness' testimony, and a new trial is necessary.

 

966 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Butler v. New York City Health & Hospitals Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


82 A.D.2d 136; 441 N.Y.S.2d 245; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11333



July 16, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, Supreme Court, New York County, entered on March 31, 1980, unanimously reversed, on the law, without costs and without disbursements, and the petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent New York City Health and Hospitals Corporation (city corporation) appealed from a judgment from the Supreme Court at Special Term, New York County (New York), which directed the hospital to reinstate petitioner employee in this N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding, which involved the construction of the New York City Health and Hospitals Corporation Act, § 9(2)(b).


OVERVIEW: The employee worked as a methadone counselor in a clinic at a private, voluntary hospital. The clinic, but not the hospital, was operated under contract to the city corporation. The hospital ceased operations, and was taken over by the city corporation. The city corporation retained the employee but required him to sign a contract in which he acknowledged that his employment was provisional. Through the machinations of the civil service system the employee was bumped from his job in favor of permanent civil servants and discharged. The employee claimed that he should have been accorded permanent status by virtue of the takeover, or by his reading of § 9(2)(b), but in reversing the judgment, the court disagreed. The court held that under § 9(2)(b) only those employees of affiliated voluntary hospitals and medical schools who performed services for the city corporation in the municipal hospitals were within its embrace. The court held that because the employee did not perform a function covered by the legislation he could not be included in the class of protected employees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, which reinstated the employee to his position with the city corporation.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Termination of Employment

Petitioner, who was discharged from his position as an addiction counselor by respondent New York City Health and Hospitals Corporation after having been provisionally appointed to that position by respondent upon its take over of a private voluntary hospital in which he was an employee, is not entitled to a judgment requiring respondent to reinstate him to the position, inasmuch as only those employees of affiliated voluntary hospitals and medical schools who perform services for respondent in municipal hospitals have permanent competitive status, and since petitioner did not perform a function within the ambit of the legislation, he cannot be included in the class [***2] of protected employees (New York City Health and Hospitals Corporation Act, § 9, subd 2, par [b], L 1969, ch 1016, as amd by L 1973, ch 884, § 1); furthermore, the scope of the New York City Health and Hospitals Corporation Act should not be broadened any further than the ordinary meaning of its words permit, for to do so would be inconsistent with the mandate of section 6 of article V of the New York State Constitution that appointments be made according to merit and fitness and, as far as practicable, by competitive examination.

 

967 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kagan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


83 A.D.2d 517; 441 N.Y.S.2d 256; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14808



July 16, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action commenced by the state against defendant bank officers, the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), upon a jury verdict, convicted the bank officers of numerous counts of misapplication of bank funds under N.Y. Banking Law § 673 and conspiracy in the third degree under former N.Y. Penal Law § 105.05, and imposed various monetary fines. The bank officers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The evidence at trial established the bank officers used the bank's assets, in a systematic way, for other than corporate purposes or proper and legitimate investment. The bank officers, however, did not profit directly from any of the illegal transactions. N.Y. Banking Law § 673 made it a felony for a bank officer to willfully misapply any of the bank's funds, property or its credit. The court affirmed the judgment on the ground that it was a violation of § 673 for a bank officer to use a bank's assets for other than proper purposes even though the officer had not intention of defrauding anyone. The court found that (1) the evidence was more than sufficient to sustain the verdict convicting the bank officers of the various counts alleging a violation of § 673; and (2) the officers' actions were motivated by a desire to accommodate the needs of the bank's new owner, a purpose which surely concerned their private interests.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the bank officers' convictions.

 

968 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Connor v. Siebert


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


83 A.D.2d 698; 442 N.Y.S.2d 599; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15014



July 16, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, Superintendent of the New York State Banking Department, sought review from orders and judgments of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Columbia County (New York), which granted plaintiff trustees' motions for summary judgment declaring the provisions of N.Y. Banking Law § 246(1)(c)(ii) unconstitutional and enjoining the Superintendent from enforcing its provisions.


OVERVIEW: The trustees of a savings institution were approaching age 75 when they filed separate actions for a declaration that § 246(1)(c)(ii) was unconstitutional. The trial court granted the trustees' summary judgment. In a single decision, the trial court held that the challenged statute was unconstitutional upon finding that it discriminated against those over 75 years old and had no substantial and rational relation to a legitimate and compelling state interest. The Superintendent was enjoined from enforcing the invalidated provision. On appeal, the court reversed. The court found it most significant that the savings institution had adopted a by-law establishing 75 as the mandatory retirement age for its trustees, and yet it had not been made a party to the actions. The trustees stated that they did not challenge the subject by-law, and there was nothing in the record that indicated that the by-law would have been repealed if the statute in question were declared unconstitutional. No justiciable controversy existed upon which the court could have properly ruled because, even if the court sustained the invalidation of the statute, no benefit would have accrued to the trustees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders and judgments, on the law, without costs, and dismissed the complaints.

Taylor v. State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


83 A.D.2d 703; 442 N.Y.S.2d 808; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15020



July 16, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers filed a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R art. 78 to review a determination of respondent, the State Tax Commission, which sustained personal income tax deficiencies for the years 1972 and 1973. The taxpayers argued that the commission's determination was unconstitutional in that an arbitrary or discriminatory classification had been made between residents and the taxpayers, who had changed their status to non-residents.


OVERVIEW: Prior to moving out of state the taxpayers sold real property in New York and realized a long-term capital gain. The sales were made on an installment basis and the taxpayers reported the gain and paid the tax due thereon each year as they received the installment payments. In January 1972 the as yet unreported taxable gain was $ 96,648. In their 1972 returns they included that portion of the sales price of the property received by them. The taxable gain received in 1973 was also timely reported. The income tax bureau found that the capital gain could not be reported on an installment basis, as the taxpayers did not comply with N.Y. Tax Law § 654(c)(4). A notice of deficiency for 1972 was issued. The commission found that the unreported taxable gain of $ 96,648 had accrued in 1972 and was fully taxable then and that the interest income acquired in 1973, which was derived from the property's sale, was includible in the taxpayers' adjusted gross income. On appeal, the court affirmed, noting the taxpayers were obligated to file a bond before changing their status to nonresident under § 654(c)(4). Had they done so they could have continued to report their gain on an installment basis.


OUTCOME: The commission's determination was confirmed, and the taxpayers' petition was dismissed, without costs.

 

970 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Laskaris


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


82 A.D.2d 34; 441 N.Y.S.2d 110; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10939



July 13, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, Queens County, dated February 7, 1980, affirmed insofar as appealed from.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People of the State of New York appealed from an order of the Supreme Court in Queens County (New York), which granted a motion by defendant to suppress certain physical evidence and oral statements.


OVERVIEW: An individual met with an undercover police officer in the officer's apartment to negotiate the sale of cocaine. The individual made a call, left, and returned to the apartment with drugs. One of the two names listed for the apartment where the call was made was defendant's. The same undercover officer later contacted the individual, and a similar transaction took place. The individual was arrested. The police then went to arrest the person in the apartment where the individual obtained the drugs. After knocking on the door, defendant opened the door, and the officers secured defendant and a woman. Defendant stated he would show them where the drugs were and led the officers to a room where they seized bags containing a white powder, and then they searched the apartment. Following his indictment on drug charges, defendant's motion to suppress his statement and all physical evidence on the grounds that the search was without probable cause, consent, warrant, or other justification was granted. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that the entry and seizure of the individuals were illegal and that, thus, defendant's statements and the physical evidence were properly suppressed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, finding that the entry and seizure of the individuals were illegal and that, therefor, defendant's statements and the physical evidence were properly suppressed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Arrest -- Warrantless Arrest in Felony Suspect's Home

1. A decision of the United States Supreme Court holding that the warrantless entry of a felony suspect's home for the purpose of arresting him is unconstitutional in the absence of exigent circumstances (see Payton v New York, 445 U.S. 537) is not retroactive since the rationale for the new constitutional doctrine is not founded upon considerations of overcoming an "aspect of the criminal trial that substantially impairs its truth-finding function ". Accordingly, the legality of defendant's warrantless arrest in his apartment by police who suspected that he was a "connection" in a drug case must be determined under the prior standard ( CPL 140.15) which permitted the warrantless arrest of a felony suspect in his home upon an unembellished showing of "reasonable cause" to believe that the individual [***2] sought has committed a crime with the reasonableness of the police conduct to be measured by the facts and inferences within police knowledge at the time they crossed the threshold of defendant's apartment.

Crimes -- Arrest -- Warrantless Arrest in Felony Suspect's Home -- Probable Cause

2. Probable cause to make an arrest, which requires far less proof than is required for conviction, is that quantum of evidence which would warrant a person of reasonable caution in believing that the suspect had committed or was committing an offense; the probable cause standard requires probable cause to believe that an offense has been committed, as well as probable cause to believe that the individual to be arrested committed it; accordingly, since the police, when they entered defendant's apartment without a warrant and seized him after following a suspect in a cocaine sale to what they believed to be the suspect's supplier, only knew that the suspect had made a phone call to someone in defendant's apartment prior to the drug sale and had entered defendant's apartment with marked money and departed without it, they lacked concrete facts which could elevate the suspicion that an individual [***3] in defendant's apartment was the suspect's "connection" to the probable cause necessary for the ensuing arrest and, therefore, the entry and seizure of the individuals in defendant's apartment were illegal and defendant's statements and the physical evidence seized must be suppressed.

 

971 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. White


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


81 A.D.2d 486; 442 N.Y.S.2d 300; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10935



July 9, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Appeal No. 1. -- Judgment unanimously affirmed.

Appeal No. 2. -- Judgment unanimously affirmed. Same opinion as in People v White, Appeal No. 1, 81 AD2d 486, decided herewith.

Appeal No. 3. -- Judgment unanimously affirmed. Same opinion as in People v White, Appeal No. 1, 81 AD2d 486, decided herewith.

Appeal No. 4. -- Judgment unanimously affirmed. Same opinion as in People v White, Appeal No. 1, 81 AD2d 486, decided herewith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of the separate judgments of the Erie County Court (New York) that convicted defendant one of attempted murder in the second degree, murder in the second degree, and criminal possession of a weapon in the second and fourth degrees, and of the judgment of the Supreme Court rendered in Erie County convicting defendants two and three of murder in the second degree and criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree.


OVERVIEW: Upon defendants' separate convictions stemming from a murder, defendants appealed. The court affirmed, holding that the trial court did not err when it failed to charge that a state's witness was an accomplice to the crime. Even if the witness was an accomplice, her testimony was sufficiently corroborated by other witnesses. Although defendants' cases were delayed over 21 months prior to going to trial, the court found that none of defendants' speedy trial rights had been violated. Because much of the delay was due to defendants' pretrial motions and the failure of the state to locate an essential witness, the court found that the delay was not unconstitutional. Nor did the delay violate the speedy trial rights provided by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 30.20 and N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 12.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the trial courts that convicted defendant one of attempted murder in the second degree, murder in the second degree, and criminal possession of a weapon in the second and fourth degrees, and convicted defendants two and three of murder in the second degree and criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Right to Speedy Trial -- Time Spent in Search of Prosecution Witness

The 21 1/2-month interval between defendant's indictment on murder charges and the commencement of hearings immediately prior to trial during [***2] which time she remained in jail does not mandate dismissal of the indictment on constitutional speedy trial grounds; each constitutional speedy trial determination requires "a sensitive weighing process of the diversified factors" such as the extent and reason for the delay, the nature of the underlying charge and whether or not there has been an extended period of pretrial incarceration or any indication that the defense has been impaired by reason of the delay (see People v Taranovich, 37 NY2d 442), and, of the 19 months not attributable to the defendant, the six-month period during which time the police exercised "due diligence" in attempting to locate the prosecution's main witness should not be charged to the People and the remaining 13-month delay attributable to the People did not deprive defendant of her right to a speedy trial considering the "unprovoked predatory" nature of the offense charged and the absence of any showing of actual prejudice; in addition, defendant's two accomplices, who were tried after her, were not denied their rights to a speedy trial since they spent only two and one-half months in jail and made no showing of actual prejudice.

 

972 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Knapp v. Shoemaker


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


82 A.D.2d 15; 442 N.Y.S.2d 287; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10937



July 9, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously reversed, on the law, without costs, motion granted and complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, driver of a car involved in an automobile accident, sought review of an order from the Supreme Court of Erie County at Special Term (New York), which denied his motion for summary judgment seeking to dismiss the personal injury action of plaintiff, passenger in, and owner of, the subject car, or, alternatively, for leave to serve an amended answer pleading the affirmative defense of lack of personal jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: The passenger filed a personal injury action in New York against the driver, a California resident, seeking damages for injuries sustained as the result of a car accident in Nebraska while a passenger in his own car that was being driven by the driver. The driver moved for summary judgment dismissing the complaint or, alternatively, for leave to serve an amended answer pleading the affirmative defense of lack of jurisdiction after the United State Supreme Court issued its decision in Rush . Savchuk, which declared invalid the quasi in rem jurisdiction. On appeal, the court reversed the trial court's denial of the driver's motion and held that dismissal of the passenger's claims was proper because the trial court lacked personal jurisdiction over the driver in light of Rush. The court determined that retroactive application Rush was appropriate because the minimum contacts necessary to satisfy the requirements of due process never existed. The court granted the driver's motion for summary judgment and dismissed the complaint.


OUTCOME: The court granted the driver's motion for summary judgment seeking dismissal of the passenger's personal injury action for lack of personal jurisdiction.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Jurisdiction -- Attachment of Insurance Policy

Defendant's motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint on the ground that quasi in rem jurisdiction was obtained in the action by the attachment of an insurance policy in this State under the doctrine of Seider v Roth (17 NY2d 111), which jurisdictional basis the United States Supreme Court declared invalid while plaintiff's action was pending ( Rush v Savchuk, 444 U.S. 320), is granted, notwithstanding the fact that defendant did not challenge jurisdiction, either by motion to dismiss or by affirmative defense until after said Supreme Court decision; moreover, since the minimum contacts necessary to satisfy the requirements of due process were never in existence, this action and those like it were void ab initio, leaving the court without the authority to condition defendant's assertion of his jurisdictional [***2] defense upon the waiver of another defense, viz., that of timeliness.

 

973 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Crouse-Irving Memorial Hospital v. Axelrod


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


82 A.D.2d 83; 442 N.Y.S.2d 338; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10946



July 9, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously reversed, without costs, motion granted and complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the Commissioner of the Department of Health of the State of New York, sought review of an order from the Supreme Court at Special Term, Onondaga County (New York), which denied his motion to dismiss plaintiff hospital's action challenging the legality of Medicaid rates.


OVERVIEW: The dispute involved Medicaid reimbursement rates for the year 1976. The hospital contended that the 1976 rates were improperly calculated and unreasonably related to its costs. The hospital filed a declaratory judgment action against the Commissioner, seeking a determination that the 1976 rates were illegal. The hospital also sought a directive ordering that the rates be recomputed. The trial court denied the Commissioner's motion to dismiss the complaint. On appeal, the court noted that while the action was pending, a state regulation was promulgated that established a procedure for reviewing a certified rate by providing for a hearing before a rate review officer. Because the case was a pending administrative proceeding at the time the amendment of the regulation was made, the intervening regulation was to be applied. Accordingly, the court held that before the hospital could seek judicial review, it was required to pursue the administrative remedy provided for in the amended regulation. The court did not consider it unjust to require the hospital to appeal administratively in a case that had been pending for five years.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order denying the Commissioner's motion to dismiss the hospital's complaint. The court dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Administrative Law -- Failure to Exhaust Administrative Remedies -- Retroactive Application of Administrative Review Procedure

A regulation (10 NYCRR 86-1.17 [b], now 10 NYCRR 86-1.17 [c]) establishing an administrative review procedure for certified Medicaid reimbursement rates may be given retroactive effect in a pending proceeding and, accordingly, plaintiff hospital should not be allowed to continue its action at law challenging the rates set by the Commissioner of the Health Department for a year prior to the effective date of the amended regulation until it first pursues the administrative remedy provided for in the regulation, which remedy was not previously available to it; the regulation must be given retroactive effect since no substantive right of the plaintiff was changed thereby, nor was plaintiff deprived of a right to challenge these rates or to institute an action at law by [***2] the provisions of said regulation, which, at most, work to postpone the right to seek judicial review until a final administrative determination is made.

People v. Rudney


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


83 A.D.2d 746; 443 N.Y.S.2d 502; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15060



July 9, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Livingston County Court (New York) that, after a jury trial, convicted her of welfare fraud. Defendant alleged an erroneous jury instruction regarding the application of N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §145(1) deprived her of a fair trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a recipient of public assistance under the Aid For Dependent Children Program, was convicted after a jury trial of welfare fraud for failure to disclose a pay increase and the receipt of unemployment benefits during a temporary lay-off period. Defendant appealed, alleging a jury instruction on the application of the presumption of guilt pursuant to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 145 deprived her of a fair trial. Upon review, the court agreed, reversed the conviction and ordered a new trial. It noted that the statutory presumption was a permissive rebuttable presumption that the jury could, but was not mandated to, rely upon; therefore, the statute was constitutionally sound. However, the jury was not told that the presumption was permissive, that they had a choice as to whether or not to apply it and, if the presumption were given effect, that it could be rebutted. Accordingly, the instruction had the effect of impermissibly shifting the burden of proof to defendant, which deprived her of a fair trial.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for welfare fraud was reversed, and the court ordered a new trial.

 

975 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Cosgrove v. Cloud Books, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


83 A.D.2d 789; 443 N.Y.S.2d 450; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15126



July 9, 1981 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant businesses sought review of a decision of the Erie Supreme Court (New York), which denied their motion for partial summary judgment in an action by plaintiff district attorney seeking a permanent injunction of the sale of certain publications by defendants.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff district attorney sought to permanently enjoin defendant businesses from selling certain publications, as well as all other future "obscene" publications. The lower court denied a motion for partial summary judgment brought by defendants. On appeal, the court found that defendants should have been granted summary judgment regarding the future publications. A prior restraint of expression may appear before the court with a heavy presumption against its constitutional validity. In this instance, the court found the plaintiff's proposed injunction would be an unreasonable prior restraint on freedom of the press. Otherwise, defendants would be forced to engage in a form of self-censorship in direct contravention of the protections guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend I.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying defendant businesses' motion for partial summary judgment in an action by plaintiff district attorney seeking a permanent injunction of the sale of certain publications by defendants was reversed.

 

976 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Craig


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


81 A.D.2d 413; 440 N.Y.S.2d 839; 1981 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10926



July 6, 1981 



DISPOSITION: Orders of the Supreme Court, Queens County, dated February 4, 1981, affirmed insofar as appealed from.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review from so much of an order of the Supreme Court in Queens County (New York) as, upon granting reargument, adhered to its original decision dismissing the first counts of the indictments charging defendants with murder in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with the crimes of murder in the first degree, murder in the second degree (felony murder), robbery in the first degree (three counts), attempted robbery in the first degree (three counts) and criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree. Defendants challenged the first counts of the respective indictments charging them with murder in the first degree under N.Y. Penal Law § 125.27, on constitutional grounds. The court found N.Y. Penal Law § 125.27 was inextricably bound to the death penalty, under N.Y. Penal Law § 60.06, as its exclusive form of punishment, so that the one could not exist without the other. Thus, because the state death penalty statute was unconstitutional, the first count in each indictment was dismissed. On appeal by the state, the court found that the lower court had correctly granted leave to the state to resubmit to another grand jury for possible consideration of murder in the second degree. Accordingly, the orders insofar as appealed from, were affirmed.


OUTCOME: The order that dismissed the first counts of the indictments charging defendants with murder in the first degree was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Murder

The Court of Appeals, in holding section 60.06 of the Penal Law, which provided for the death penalty upon conviction of murder in the first degree under section 125.27 of the Penal Law, unconstitutional, concomitantly held the latter section to be likewise unconstitutional; the two statutes are in pari materia and must be read together since the legislative purpose can be effectuated only if both are [***2] applied and enforced concomitantly. Inasmuch as section 125.27 of the Penal Law, as it may provide a substantive basis for murder in the first degree, is so inextricably bound to the death penalty, under section 60.06 of the Penal Law, the former is burdened with the same constitutional infirmity as renders the latter invalid.

Defendant asserted that he was held at gunpoint while officers searched his car, finding the gun under the driver's seat. The reviewing court found that the evidence should not have been suppressed. Kloepfer v. Commissioner of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department
